THE RED PAGE.
IN TRANQUILLISSIMAM MEMORIAM.
J.F.
Dead? I suppose so! And what doe s it matter ?
1 sleep well, anyway—and what's the odds ?
Is n’t it perfect calm we give the gods ?
—And calm is best, in spite of all the chatter.
I don’t know that I’d care to grow much fatter.
Duller and wearier—keep on pickling rods
To beat old age with. So, as who 's-this nods,
The earlier end is better than the latter.
Besides, I’ve done my share of hurrying:
Let others take the pace—l won’t be missed:
P’r’aps two or three will think of me—say one,
To save me from the fate of Tomlinson —
You know it ? Fine thing ! . . . Well, I won’t insist ...
Then that’s all right—and what’s the use of ivorrying?
In the Early Days.
W CRAIG, a veteran among immi-
grants, who writes from Invercar-
• gill, M.L., has set down his reminis-
cences of adventure on the Victorian
goldfields in the early ’fifties. Under the title of
My Adventures on the Australian Goldfields, he
makes a book of some 340 pages—a garrulous old
gentleman’s book, with slight literary value, but
pithy enough to be readable. Special interest
comes in the fact that eye-witnesses of the early-
life at Port Phillip, and especially of the inci-
dents of the Eureka rebellion, are now few in
number, and every year Death takes from the
remnant. Any unprejudiced account of the time
when Australia was in the making, when her
course in some main directions of national life
was definitely set, must necessarily be interest-
ing. Craig’s picture is meagre enough, but it
seems to hold truth and sincerity; and one is
glad to have it.
Craig came to Port Phillip in the ship that
brought Peter Lalor, and he describes him in his
prime. “He was a picture of robust manhood,
and from his demeanour I surmispd he was a man
who thought for himself, and that something
would be heard of him later on. After a time a
friendly feeling became established between us,
and I discovered in him a man of high intelligence
and of sterling worth, yet one who might be led
into unwise courses by sheer impulsiveness. Still,
he possessed important qualifications for asuccess-
ful career—ambition, energy, and courage. He
has passed away to the Unknown Land. But
for the services he rendered to the working
classes, especially the miners, and the blow he
struck at official despotism and corruption, the
name of Peter Lalor should be kept green in the
hearts of dwellers in these Colonies.”
PETER LALOR
I speak of Mr. Lalor as I interpreted hit character—
and few had a better opportunity of knowing him.
Descended from a family of high social status and political
influence in Ireland, his leading characteristics were
patriotic ardour and a warm attachment to the land of
his I) rth. He never tired of descanting on the wrongs
of Ireland. I inferred from his conversations that either
he himself or some of his relatives had been associated
with the leaders of the rising of ’4B ; and disgust at the
ignominious collapse of that movement was his principal
reason, as in the case of many other eminent Irishmen,
for migrating to the Antipodes. The prospect of
acquiring wealth on the goldfields did not seem to
interest Lalor ; indeed, he appeared to have made up his
mind before he left to engage in politics in the land of
his adoption. I gathered that much from him one
afternoon during the voyage out in the course of a
discussion on Irish affairs. . . .
“Well,” he said, “we shall see if a better state of
things cannot he worked out in Australia. I intend to
have a voice in its government before two years are over.
The Lalors have always had a weakness for politics. My
father sat in the British Parliament for Queen’s County,
and I intend to sit in the Victorian Parliament after I
find out where improvements are needed.”
DIGGERS AND POLICE
When gold was first discovered in Victoria the colony
was ultra-conservative. The officials were mostly mem-
bers of influential English families, and the management
of public affairs was principally in the hands of pastoral
tenants and bankers. These naturally viewed with alarm
the irruption of a mining population. The stations
became almost deserted for the goldfields, and before long
drastic laws were enacted for harassing the diggers....
The police were composed principally of ne’er-do-wells of
influential British families. A number of these black
sheep arrived by every ship, bringing letters of recom-
mendation from friends in high positions ; and in a short
time nearly all the Government offices were occupied by
people of that class. Too lazy, or unused, to work, and
ashamed to beg, a billet on the goldfields or enrolment in
the police force was the only choice left to to them. . . .
Governor Latrobe had been superseded by Sir Charles
Hotham, a British naval captain, and, as might be ex-
pected from such training, pretty much of an autocrat.
With quarter-deck absolutism he decreed that licence
fees should be paid punctually, and that strict search for
unauthorised miners should be made by the goldfields
officials.
The Governor's advisers, and those acquainted with
the temper of the diggers, suggested an export duty on
gold as the fairest way to raise revenue from the mines,
but, like most old-dme man-of-war martinets, Governor
Hotham stiffened all the more when opposition in any
shape or form was offered to his arbitrary manifestoes,
and his orders were zealously given effect to by the
“ new-chum ” policemen. A digger hunt to them was as
exhilarating as a fox bunt would have been in England,
and on each occasion dozens of respectable men, whose
onfc’ crime was that they were unfortunate, and were
unable to pay the licence fee, were manacled like felons,
and in that state exposed to public gaze. Such as could
not be accommodated in the lock-ups were shackled to
trees, and armed sentries were placed over them until
punishment could be meted out to them...
THE EUREKA HOTEL
Among those who were permitted to engage in the
liquor traffic was a ruffian named Bentley, who had
served several years of convict life in Norfolk Island.
His place—the Eureka Hotel—was the resort of most of
the camp officials, and it was understood that many of
them were indebted to him for pecuniary assistance.
The resident police magistrate acknowledged after his
dismissal that several of the officials were even more
corrupt than himself, and were indebted to Bentley for
large sums of money. With such friends “at court'” he
was in the habit of acting high handedly to visitors. So
it came about that a miner, who called at his place one
night for refreshment after closing time, and on being
refused continued to demand it, was thrown out on the
highway hy Bentley, and in the scuffle lost his life.
When the case against Bentley was disnii-sed by Police
Magistrate Dewes, it was felt that the edifice of official
nial-administration had received its coping-stone, and
the tide of discontent rose higher and higher. The uni-
versal feeling was that a miscarriage of justice had taken
place, and in the evening a large gathering of miners
assembled in front of the hotel and threatened Bentley.
The police were sent to protect the house, but their pre-
sence, and that of their unpopular chief, served but to
increase the indignation of the crowd
The lamp in front of the hotel was first demolished,
and the crashing noise of the glass stimulated the miners
to further mischief. In a few minutes all the windows
were smashed, and then the work ot devastation com-
menced in earnest. The building was rushed, and beer,
spirits, bedding, furniture, and everything contained
therein were brought outside. A bonfire was made of
the furniture, and casks and bottles of liquor were
smashed, and the contents allowed to run to waste.
Lastly, the building itself was burned to the ground.
Bentley and his wife barely escaped with their lives
through the back entrance to the police camp. A shot
was fired at him in retreating, and general regret was
felt that the aim was inaccurate.
Three men were arrested for burning the building, and
sent to Melbourne for trial. They were found guilty,
and sentenced to various terms of incarceration. At the
same sessions, Bentley, who had been re-arrested, was
again arraigned for the murder of the man Scobie, and,
being found guilty of manslaughter, was sentenced to
three years’ imprisonment with hard labour
Delegates were now sent from Ballarat to Melbourne to
represent the unfairness of punishing the three men con-
victed of burning the building, but they were unfavour-
ably received by the Governor and his executive ; and on
receipt of their reply some 12.000 miners assembled on
Bakery Hill and passed resolutions in favour of reform in
the administration of goldfields affairs...
EUREKA STOCKADE
From a man of Lalor’s temperament, moderation, in the
face of official aggressiveness, was out of the question, so
drill and the erection of a stockade on Bakery II II were
carried on vigorously, while some 500 men passed under
the “ Southern Cross ” and swore allegiance to it in the
presence of Lalor and his subordinates. The following
was the wording of the oath administered : “We swear
b.v the Southern Cross to stand truly to each other, and
fight to defend our rights and liberties.”
Meanwhile the camp officials were not idle. A Captain
Thomas was in command of the military, and as it was
considered risky to attack the stockade during the day,
when the Government camp would he unprotected, he
decided to strike a blow at the rebellion early on the
following morning.
Accordingly, some 300 men, composed of troops of the
line and police, approached the entrenchment at the time
appointed. They' advanced to within a few hundred
paces of it without being observed, when a shot from the
sentry inside the stockade announced that the crisis had
arrived. The insurgent camp was immediately aroused,
and several irregular volleys were fired at the military.
So long as the conflict was confined .to shooting, those
inside the fence had the best of the fight. This was plain
to the officer in command of the troops, and so the order
was given for a bayonet charge. The soldiers were soon
inside the palisade, and ten minutes later, as might have
been expected with the crowd of partially armed and un-
disciplined men, it was a case of sauve qui peut.
The attack upon a company of infantry from Melbourne
a few day’s previously by a concealed party of insurgents
had aroused the passions of the soldiers, and stimulated
them to revenge when opportunity should offer. But to
their credit be it said, they' fought fairly. In no case
were they known to kill a wounded man. The dastardly
proceeding of shooting and stabbing disabled miners was
resorted to solely' by the raw fledgling police, who lost all
self-control when assured that the stockade had been
captured and that the defenders were at their merev. In
one case, after the surrender of a number of miners to
the military', a body of police rushed up, and would have
smit them but for the determined interference of an
officer of the 40th Regiment, who insisted that they were
Crown prisoners in his charge, and would have to be
dealt with by the civil authorities. One man had fifteen
wounds on different parts of his body, most of which
were received after he was disabled by a sabre cut on the
head. Another miner was shot by a trooper when retreat-
liig from hi* tent, and, while bleeding to death, was hand-
cuffed by his captor. . In this case the services of a black-
smith had to be obtained to cut the manacles from bis
wrists before the surgeon could probe the wound for the
ball.
During the brief conflict forty were killed on each side,
and about an equal number wounded, some of whom
subsequently died from their injuries. A number of the
wounded were also reported to have been burned alive in
stores and tents where they had taken refuge before the
police set fire to them.
CONDUCT OF READERS
To their discredit be it said, those of the insurrec-
tionary leaders who had clamoured most for armed
resistance were the first to consult personal safety when
the crucial point was reached. A Hanoverian named
Vern, who claimed to have had a military training in
Germany and America, and who at the various meetings
of the League was most declamatory in impeaching the
Government, was observed, after the' first volley from the
troops, bounding over the palisades in the opposite direc-
tion, and was not heard of for months afterwards. As
he was regarded as the most dangerous of the agitators,
a higher reward was offered for his apprehension than in
the case of the others.
Several others of the leaders displayed not less celerity
in clearing out at the first alarm ; while others who had
ostentatiously paraded the diggings and the main street
the day before, levying toll on stores in the name of the
“Republic,” ignominiously retreated down shafts and
up log chimneys when the cry was raised, “Turnout—
the soldiers are coming !,” . Indeed, the only two leaders
who displayed courage during the fight were Peter Lalor
and Wallace Ross, a Canadian. The former was maimed
for life, the latter was shot dead near the pole on which
he had hoisted the Southern Cross ensign the day before.
Lalor did not seek the unenviable office that had been
thrust upon him. The choice of leadership lay between
him and the German Vern, and when he was proposed,
his demeanour and remarks contrasted so favourably
with the truculent appearance and sanguinary utterances
of Vern that he was almost unanimously elected as the
fittest man for the work. Responding to the serious
trust that had been imposed upon him, he remarked
that, conscious as he was that a certain amount of mili-
tary training was required for the position, he had no
desire to occupy it, but that, as he was urged to allow
his name to go to the vote, he would accept the respon-
sibility and unflinchingly discharge to the best of his
ability the duties required of him. Nor did he fail to
fulfil his promise.
When the alarm was first sounded Lalor made strenuous
efforts to organise an effective line of defence, but it was
labour in vain. Many of the miners had freely indulged
in strong drink the previous night, and when they were
hurriedly turned out at daylight in a semi-stupid con-
dition, they failed to comprehend the orders that were
issued, and uttei confusion ensued on every side. There
was no plan of action, no system, and no order could be
evolved out of the crude material under Lalor's com-
mand.
ABORTIVE TRIADS
A number of the more prominent ringleaders of the
League were forwarded to Melbourne for trial, but public
opinion there was so pronounced on the misgovernment
of the goldfields, and the indignities and unequal taxa-
tion the miners had been subjected to, that it was clear
no conviction would ensue.
The first to be arraigned was a man named Hayes, who
had acted as chairman when the League met to'transact
its ordinary business. His acquittal was the signal for
suspending further proceedings, and a few days later the
prosecutions were abandoned, and a general amnesty
was proclaimed. . Hayes was a jovial, even-tempered,
peace-loving 1 individual, but his fighting deficiencies were
amply compensated for by his wife’s pronounced com-
bativeness. As a witness for the prosecution, Lieutenant
Richards, of the 40th Regiment, deposed that when he
ordered the arrest of Hayes, the prisoner’s wife came up
to him and told him that “if she were a man she would
not have allowed herself to be taken alive,” and added,
“ Why didn’t you attack the stockade yesterday, when
we were prepared to receive you ? ”
What most surprised the'prisoners and the general
puhlic was the fact that the leading evidence for the
Crown was furnished by a man named Goodenough, who
was one of the most trusted of the disaffected miners.
He had been a constant attendant at their meetings, and
every detail of their plans was furnished daily to the
camp officials. It was on his suggestion that the attack
was made at daybreak on Sunday morning. He had
noticed that a large quantity of liquor was brought to a
tent the previous day, and suspecting that a prolonged
carouse would be held that night, and that a large pro-
portion of the malcontents would be unprepared to meet
an assault, he assured the officer in-charge of the military
that all, or nearly all, in the stockade could' be made
prisoners without opposition.
Goodenough was a recent arrival in the colony, andhad
been a detective in Ireland. Being in possession of
trustworthy credentials, he was taken into the police
service at Melbourne, and forwarded to Ballarat with the
special object of gaining information regarding the aims
and objects of the Miners’ League. After the acquittal of
Hayes he mysteriously disappeared, and although assi-
duous inquiries were made for him by former Ballarat
acquaintances, no authentic information about him was
ever forthcoming.
“IRISH INDEPENDENCE”
The collapse of the rising at Ballarat may be regarded
as mainly attributable to the password given by Lalor on
the night before the assault.” Asked by one of the subor-
dinate leaders of the revolt for the “ night-pass,” he gave
“Vinegar Hill,” where the Irish insurgents suffered a de-
cisive defeat in ’9B by English troops. Many at Ballarat
who were disposed before that to resist the military now
quietly withdrew from the movement. They concluded
that Lalor’s object was more to strike a blow for Ireland
than at official despotism. So instead of t! ere being, as
in the morning, some 700 men inside the defences, there
were barely 230 at the time of the attack. Bendigo,
Forest Creek, and Creswick contributed contingents to
assist in the struggle. From the latter place alone a
thousand men were on the march to Ballarat ; but when
the news circulated that Irish inth pendence had crept
into the movement, almost all turned back.
It may be mentioned that both Bentley and Dewes—
main factors in the Eureka tragedy—terminated their
careers by suicide. After doing his three years for the
manslaughter of Scobie, Bentley took to drink atid per-
formed the “ happy despatch ” with laudanum. On being
dismissed from office, Dewes departed for America.
Thence he gravitated to Paris, where he took his own life.
DOTHAM AND THE MINERS
Although conscientious and painstaking, a more unsuit'
able man than Sir Charles Hotham could not have been
selected to extricate the ship of state from the dangers
and confusion that beset her at that time. As governor
of a colony peopled by planters and negroes, he would
have been in a congenial position, but in a self-governing
offshoot of the motherland, inhabited by hardy, intelli-
gent, and independent toilers, his limited administrative
ability and haughty demeanour weft a source of danger.
Although he was accorded a magnificent reception on his
arrival, his supercilious bearing effectively repelled popu-
lar sentiment. The suggestive advertisement that was so
long a prominent feature of the Melbourne Argus when
the first Governor, Mr. Latrobe, held office (“ Wanted, a
Governor for the Colony of Victoria ”), voiced public
opinion for a change, but after a short experience of his
successor it was universally admitted that of the two evils
Latrobe was the lesser.
Sir Charles evidently arrived in the colony with the
preconceived idea that he had to deal with rough adven-
turers, released prisoners, and convicts, and that the
mines were to be used-simply as so many sources of
revenue ; and it was elicited in the Legislative Assembly
that, in the course of a conversation during his outward
voyage, he sententiously remarked that “a little blood-
letting would not do the unruly gold-nib ers any harm.”
It also came out that he was accustomed to communicate
in cypher with the Commissioners, on goldfields manage-
ment, without consulting his responsible advisers. The
Governor, however, was not so much to blame as those
who appointed him. By instinct and training he was
opposed to compromise or concession. His failure told
on his health, and fulminated in his premature death on
the 31st of December, 1855, barely two and a half years
after his arrival in the colony.
The Commission of Inquiry appointed later on by the
Legislative Assembly to investigate the origin of the riots
and loss of life, reported that “the diggers had been
governed three times over,” and that “while in certain
cases they had been guilty of excesses, they had been
goaded thereto by bad laws that were badly enforced.”
The sacrifice at Eureka was a heartrending one, but
beneficial effects ensued. When the torch of rebellion
was finally extinguished reforms were immediately insti-
tuted in ' ictoria, and to the progressive measures then
adopted may be distinctly traced the liberal legislation
that followed in the other colonies of the Australa ian
group. The way was paved for constitutional govern-
ment ; the unpopular Executive Com c 1 resigned office ;
the Ballarat officials were either dismissed -or .transferred
elsewhere ; digger lilint# terminated ; manhood suffrage
was established ; a general amnesty wa'S-proolaimed ; and
a few months earlier than the time he had named when
midway between Britain and Australia, Peter Lalor was
elected first member for Ballarat in the Legislative
Assembly. About two years later he became a Cabinet
Minister, and afterwards for a long period held the
position of Speaker of the House.
In the dispassionate light that comes with mature age.
I am free to say, an eye witness of the disastrous
affair, that, while the Governor of the colony and his
subordinates were the greatest transgressors, the Eureka
tragedy would not have occurred had the principal
leaders of the Miners’ League displayed more discretion.

George Gordon McCrae writes:,; “I beg most
distinctly to deny having either written or illustrated any
Comic History of Australia. I mention this as I find it
imputed to me by‘F.M.’ (who evidently knows neither

The hyper-cultured American is a funny pro-
duct. In a sonnet to “The Man” in Dec. Bookman,
Elsa Barker remarks—
And what am I—a woman ! —by thy side,
Though 1 stand symbol of the spai ial sea,
The boundless gestatoriurn and bride
Of the Creator ?

RECEIVED
Katharine Frensham , by Beatrice Harraden
(Lond., Blackie ; 2s. 6 d.) English intellectuals against a
Scandinavian background. A clever, febrile book, with
art insufficient to stifle the sigh of Weltschmerz. There
is no objection to Weltschmerz on the grand scale of grief
or art, but when it merely gnaws like a decayed tooth,
Weltschmerz is depressing. One should dodge depres-
sion : it is devitalising. Real tragedy is tonic.
The YoUng Gerande, by Edmund White (Edin.,
Blackwood; 2s. 6/1.) A picture of middle-class people in
Berlin at the middle of la,s,t century. The atmosphere is
clear and human, and seemingly the picture is truthful:
one follows the proceedings of the English hero with
iriild interest. Mild, elderly interest is not enough.
The_ Boy Galloper, by “Linesman” (Edin.,
B ackwood ; 2s. 6 it.) Another imaginary war in England,
bv the military writer who chased De Wet vigorously..
The time is to-day : on this occasion it is the Germans
who land and; —surrender as per patriotic formula.
The story is Written for younger readers —gallant school-
boys who may dream of receiving, like ‘The Boy
Galloper.” a V.O. from the hands of affable King Edward
himself (how far faded from The Coming K —- of last-
generation satire !) It is better than well written, and
ethical Objection to the glorification of the military hero is
met by the scheme of self-de'ence, Probably useless
at the moment to object to the idealisation of the heredi-
tary monarch as dispenser of every good and perfect gift :
nevertheless, one objects pro forma.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Little House.
There’s a little house in Mosman that stands upon
the hill,
As it stood in the years-long ago ;
In the little garden so green, and grave, and still,
Seven shadows walk to and fro.
Little knows the good wife who keeps the house
to day
That she lives in a shadow-throng ;
She hears her brown-faced children laughing at
their play,
And she carols a careless song.
Little does she know that where red geraniums
grow
On the brow of the grassy height,
Where the baby sits and plays with his pretty
cheeks aglow,
There sat here, of old, Heart’s Delight.
O, Heart’s Delight was fair, and the blue enamored
air
Kissed her lips that like roses shone;
And the heart of summer glowed in her golden-
flowing hair
When the summer days were gone.
And there is a little summer-house, and round it
grows a vine,
And the sunrays around it dance,
And I see two shadows sitting there, and drinking
of the wine
And a-talking of old Romance.
Seven shadows walk there in sunlight and moon-
shine,
Seven shadows walk to and fro,
And I would, and I would that the Little House
were mine
With the Ghosts of the Long Ago.
N * s - W -
VICTOR J. DALEY.
Fruition.
You come to me, you come to me at last,
When youth and hope, and all that’s sweet, are
dead.
Even the beauty that was yours has fled.
The lustre of your hair is dimmed- o’ercast.
Your eyes have lost their fire, your kiss is cold ;
Afore those lips were full and warm and red ;
And what have you to give me in their stead,
You that have squandered with both hands Love’s
gold?
You love me still as once you did, you say,
My face has been before you all these years;
And do you think that now one fond word clears
The dark mists of forgetfulness away ?
You give me age for youth, for love a moek ;
You threw away the passion that I gave.
To-day, what cannot be, in vain you crave ;
That door your hands can nevermore unlock.
For ever young I held you in my heart,
I had forgot that time could touch your charm.
A dream you grew no evil chance could harm—
A dream of loveliness, remote, apart.
And now you come, Love’s beggar, destitute;
Of all the years of travail and despair,
Of all the years that Grief with Grief did share,
This is the bitter, this the withered fruit.
N.B.W.
WILFRID MAILLER.
The Skeleton.
He wandered on through oaverns dark,
Where Fancy’s flashes dimmed the eye
And hid the corpses cold and stark
That grinned a curse as he passed by.
Behind him as he strode along,
In mood of bitter mockery
Arose the burden of a song—
“ Oh, Passion’s feted guests are we—
“We sought the thing you seek, and found
Desire and Hate clasped hand in hand ;
With cord of Lust our limbs were bound,
Our brows seared with a fiery brand.”
He heard it not, but murmured, “ Love,
Come, bind me with thy snow-white arms—
Thine eyes, that shame the stars above,
Bespeak a thousand nameless charms.”
“Come here! ” ho oried, and all around
His path there grew a ghostly light,
And rose, as from the mist-dank ground,
A veiled figure, draped in white.
“ Beware! ” It stung like winter hail!
He turned, saw nought, and then ’twas done.
“My love ! ” he gasped, and raised the veil—
And, oh ! a grinning skeleton.
V.
L.M.D.
Farting is Such Sweet Sorrow.
Why did we meet,
If parting bring sorrow ?
Why is it sweet,
If we know it must end f
Glad is to-day—
Can we think of to-morrow,
Taking away from us Lover and Friend ?
Each meeting leads us
To that when we sever;
Each parting speeds us
How near to the last!
Gaily we deem
We may love on for ever !
Fondly we dream love will never be past.
Sweet to have met,
Tho’ the parting bring sorrow 1
Eyes will be wet
When they meet in Good-bye ;
Cheek pressed to cheek
For the last time to-morrow—
Lip 3 cannot speak for the break in the sigh 1
M.L.

MANY COMPLICATIONS LEFT BEHIND.
All Banished and Health Restored
by CLEMENTS TONIC.
The Case of Mr. J BARTLETT.
(BY ▲ SYDNEY REPORTER.)
It is to the interest of this country that
all citizens should be so educated that they
may become a self-reliant and prosperous
people, and to put this idea into practical
effect a reporter was commissioned to bring
before the community a matter here dealt
with by Mr. James Bartlett, of No. 5
Patten’s Buildings, Kent-street, Sydney.
“In the time of a great epidemic,” said
Mr. Bartlett, “ when influenza was ram-
pant in Sydney, I was one of the many
who failed to elude that terrible disease.”
“Were you in danger of meeting with
serious consequences? ” asked the wiiter.
“ There was every likelihood that I
would never get well again, as I was in a
frightfully low state for many months.
Matters went so badly with me that I
thought I was fighting a hopeless fight
against an overwhelming force, and as I
1 was laid up for weeks, and could not do a
stroke of work for seven months, I think I
had good cause to form that opinion. I got
so thin and ghastly white that everybody
thought I was going into consumption, be-
sides getting awfully weak into the bar-
gain <*T lie pains in my head were enough
to rui it! way with one’s senses, being of that
heavy, depressing nature which makes the
victim feel as if there is only care and
sunow in the world, It was not the in-
fluenza itself that I had to regret so much
as the effects it left behind, for my system
seemed so upside down that there was not
a sound part about me. My limbs were so
racked w ith aches that if I walked a few
yards I had to sit down and rest for the
purpose of easing the pain, but another
reason why I could not go far without
taking a spell was because I used to turn
very giddy. Through that cause I nearly
fell down stairs several times, so
that I was almost afraid to move in case
of meeting with an accident. I wondered
what was the good of living in such misery,
for I had no energy, no pleasure, no any-
thing that was worth having. For weeks
and weeks I could not get any sleep, and
there was a feeling in my eyes as if they had
swelled up. A dark haze was visible at
every turn, my sight being so bad that I
could scarcely see, and if I wanted to get
anything for myself I had to grope about
as if it were dark. I could not hear very
distinctly either, owing to a strange drum-
ming in my ears, so that I was not much
use to anybody. Indeed, I was a nuisance
to myself, and would like to have been out
of the way of the persecuting pains which
came to the small of my back and almost
paralysed me whenever I stooped. This,
together with the thickness of my urine,
told me that my kidneys were disordered,
and I was af erwards told so by the doctors
Whom I was treated by, with the additional
information that my liver was also dis-
eased ” 0
“ Did they give you any medicine ? ”
“ Of course they did, and I was never
behind hand in taking it, as my enforced
absence from work meant a great loss to
me, and I wanted to get better. But, some-
how, my condition remained stationary.
When I examined my tongue it was al-
ways furred and rough, while my mouth
had a foul taste as if I had been drinking.
Not a morsel of food could I enjoy, but I
am not one to give in till I am fairly beaten,
and I used to make myself eat; but it
would have been better if I had spared my-
self the trouble, as everything seemed to
decay in the stomach, and give rise to ex-
cessive torture in the form of windy spasms.
My chest also felt as if a piece of lead had
1 dged there for the purpose of stopping my
breath, and there was no relief to be gob
till I vomited everything up again. The
weakness of my stomach was so advanced
that I could not keep my food down, so
that accounts for my feebleness and the loss
of flesh I sustained. There was also some-
thing wrong with my heart, which pafpi-
tated as if I had been indulging in some ex-
cessive exercise, and with my nerves all
gone I was completely wrecked from every
point of view. A twitching pain below my
neck irritated me, but for that matter X
was always in an irritable mood, even the
sound of voices raised in laughter being
sufficient to annoy mo; but I was so
desperate and despondent that I did not
know what to do with myself. In any
short stretches of sleep I got there flashed
an awful picture upon my vision, and when
I woke up I could not help thinking that
something I dreaded would surely happen,
although I recognised that death would
have been preferable to any more suffer*
ing.”
“ Why did you not try a change of
medicine
“If I had not done so I would not be
here to-day, as I was sinking very fast after
the long and torturing captivity in the
hands of my diseases, when a fellow-work-
man prompted me to try Clements Tonic.
It was not till I carried out his advice that
the influenza was beaten, and now, after a
long and convincing experience, I am sure
there is no medicine on earth like Clements
Tonic, for after taking a couple of bottles I
was feeling a new interest in existence.
The improvement was then so marked that
I ventured to hope that a possibility still
existed that I would yet recover, and by
closely pursuing my latest course of treat-
ment my heartfelt hopes were gratified. I
started on the right track with a slight
appetite, which came through using
Clements Tonic, and which that medicine
continued to increase. Then my liver be-
gan to work more regularly, a fact which
afforded great relief in several ways. For
instance, my headaches slackened off, and
the sickly taste left my mouth, besides hav-
ing full use of my eyes in consequence of tho
disappearance of the haziness before them.
The pains in my back and shoulders were
lighter than before, and I had no trouble in
breathing, as my chest was quite free from
oppression. A feeling of vigour crept
through my system through th® medium of
Clements Tonic, and I was gaining in flesh
already, which showed that my food was
digesting well. I marvelled at the rapidity
with which my vomiting troubles were
stopped, while the action of Clements Tonic
on my nervous system could not be too
highly spoken of. When my liver and
kidneys had been relieved of all symptoms
of disease by Clements Tonic I hardly knew
how to suppress the gladness I felt, es-
pecially as no cause remained for anxiety
as to the state of my heart, which ceased
shortly after flatulence was
remedied. 1
“ And now your health is perfect, I
believe ?”
“It could not be better, for I took
Clements Tonic till I was cured outright,
and for the past three years I have scaled
over 13 stone. lam thankful to be able to
give this information, which you can pub-
lish in any way it pleases you.”

THURSDAY, JANUARY 21, 1904.
The Australian Sickness.
N.S. Wales Premier John See, talking a few
days ago, said that he did not regret the expendi-
ture of public money during the last year or two;
to borrow and spend was, he believed, the only
possible policy in the circumstances. In the same
spirit, members of the N.S.W. Labor party, while
confessing that they wish borrowing could be
stopped, have lately declared that they don’t know
how the last year or two could have been weathered
without it. Put into plain English, this means
that they don’t know how this country could have
lived during the last little while if it had not been
subsidised. In Victoria the situation has been
as bad or worse ; professedly in order to meet
it, the pensions of the needy pioneers of
the State were cut down, and the roads
and railways were allowed to go to ruin,
and even then the sinking funds established by
former Treasurers were stolen in order to
make the State s accounts look square. Queens-
land did no great deal of borrowing simply
bee use it couldn’t; it tested its credit, and found
that there wasn’t enough of it left to be worth
mentioning. Nevertheless it had to borrow some-
thing, or if it couldn’t borrow it had to either
steal or owe, for in the last four years its ordinary
revenue has failed to meet its ordinary expendi-
ture by £1,343,469. South Australia last year had
one of the most profitable times it has ever
known, and still, with the Northern Territory, it
ended up with a deficit. So did Tasmania, whose
position was much the same. In even an un-
usually good year, these two States had thus to
be subsidised, too ; and they got the subsidy by
pledging some of the proceeds of future seasons,
whether they be good or bad ; and the best of
them can hardly be better than the present.
Westralia certainly wound up its State’s financial
year with a small surplus ; and it promptly sent
across warnings to the eastern States not to be
misled by that circumstance and rush over there
for work, because there were droves of unemployed
already on the spot.
* * * *
While these things were happening, Australia
was told by its public men and its unpatriotic
newspapers not to worry. It was assured that it
was passing through a time of great depression—
such a time, in fact, as it had never had before,
and inighfc make pretty sure it would never have
again. So long.as the drought lasted this seemed
fairly convincing ; and, except here and there,
Australia did not woiry. It was content to
believe that what it was suffering from was
quite exceptional, and that the symptoms
would most likely never be experienced again.
But the drought is over long since, and to it has
succeeded the most bountiful harvest ever known
in Australia. So bountiful is it in N.S.W. that,
though last year’s was so much of a failure,
the average of the two is greater than
that of any other two consecutive years
in the State’s history with a single exception.
And still there is no sign of that prosperity
which, we were lately told, was bound to
return with the first good season. I hat fact
alone throws a shadow of doubt and sus-
picion over the comforting theory upon which
we proceeded to borrow throughout last year and
the year before ; and the more inquiries that are
made the more disreputable and leaky does the
theory become. In the first place, even if there
were a failure of the wheat crops in two or three
of the. States, there was such prosperity in other
directions as had not hitherto blessed the con-
tinent. The N.S.W. coalfields never had such
an output before. Newcastle’s export was more
than twice as great as it was 10 years ago. Every-
where gold-mining flourished. Many fields topped
all their previous figures. Down to 10 years ago the
Australasian production of this metal had averaged
for many years an annual £6,000,000; last year it
reached £18,250,000, a growth of over 300 per
cent, in 10 years. This total Australasian gold-
production is equal to about 13-times the value of
the average wheat crops in New South Wales.
Moreover, as to New South Wales, although the
wheat crop failed in 1903, in 1902 it was the second
largest on record, and in 1901 absolutely the
largest. In Victoria also the 1903 failure followed
hard upon a succession of record harvests. It
was the same in Queensland. The fact, then, is
that there was unusual prosperity in some indus-
tries in all the States last year, while there was
unusual prosperity in even the agricultural indus-
try in some States; moreover, the year, even as
to the worst industries, followed a succession
of the fattest years Australia has had; never-
theless it is deliberately stated that, had we not
been subsidised (by men who will want it all
back, with interest added), we should not have been
able to pull through. After years of unexampled
production, we ought to have put by enough for
a year of absolute failure; and yet, even when
the year was a failure in only one or two
branches of industry, and when it was a gor-
geous success in others, Australia had to borrow,
so it was pleaded, in order to fill its people’s
mouths, it had to cut down the poor pittance it was
allowing its pioneers, to steal trust funds in order
to square its ledger, and to let its public works
go to pieces for want of paint and putty. And
even then it didn’t square its ledger.
• * * •
The Bulletin asserts, therefore, that last year
and the year before that Australia was not suffer-
ing from any unusual depression whatever, and
that the borrowing boom and the other things
mentioned in the last paragraph were therefore
achieved under false pretences. It asserts that
the drought was not responsible for the tight place
in which Australia found itself ; and it also asserts
that the tight place still exists, and that Australia
is in it, even now that the drought has passed.
Further, it is clear that even something more than
an ordinary success in the harvest fields alone will
not get us out of the difficulty. Tasmania and South
Australia, as before remarked, had unusual success
in that direction last year, and still they were
very little better off than the other States ; each
had a deficit and unemployed, and those who were
employed got poor wages. A country suffering
from a burst of prosperity ought not to show those
features. What is the matter ?
* * * *
Mainly borrowing. Mainly the absentee.
Mainly, in short, the “subsidy” that it is
deolared we couldn’t have done without last year.
Mainly the drug that we debauched.on last year,
and the year before that, and years before that
again. Australasia has now returned to it so
often that, at the present time, it has to export
about £11,000,000 a year more than it imports in
order to satisfy its public creditor. For 10 years
past the wheat-crop of N.S. Wales has averaged
about million bushels. At the present price of
wheat it would take nearly eight-times that much
to pay the Australasian bill for interest and charges.
The weary old gag is that the money has been
“ spent on reproductive works.” We know that,
to a great extent, that is not true; but that does
not matter fcr the present. The point is that the
money has to be paid, and that, to satisfy the
public creditor alone, Australasia has to send to
him every year eight-times as much as the whole
of the average wheat crop of N.S. Wales is worth
at present prices! How much is necessary to
satisfy the private creditor it is almost impossible
to average; in his case the amount is not fixed.
In the allegedly bad year of 1903 the foreign-
owned banks made profits of just over £500,000.
That was Australia’s tribute to one small set of
private creditors. Take another set, the insur-
ance companies doing business in N.S. Wales:
there are 79 of them, and no less than 57 have
their head offices outside the Commonwealth. Of
the larger mortgage companies, and especially
those that have their claws upon the “natural
producing ” industries, there is hardly one that is
not a foreigner. In the mining industry, lately
so prosperous, there is the same drain of Austra-
lian life-blood. No front-rank Australian mine
to-day is mainly Australian-owned. Two-thirds,
certainly, and probably more, of all mining divi-
dends are paid and spent outside Australia. Add
to the tribute payable to the public creditor this
other tribute payable to the well-nigh innumer-
able private creditors, including the foreign
manufacturer of the goods we ought to be
manufacturing ourselves, and we shall get some
idea of what is the matter with Australia, and
why its back is bent and its head bowed in even
a fair season.
• • * •
But, as a matter of fact, we have not in the
past been in the habit of sending away this 14 or
15 millions’ worth of our products in excess of our
imports. We have simply exported an excess of
seven or eight millions, and owed the rest ; that
is to say, we have added the balance to our princi-
pal debt. While we could do this, the burden
was not felt ; it seemed a burden, not of 14 or 15
millions, but of seven or eight millions only.
Now, however, when our creditors say “Pay,”
and when all of the bill has to be provided for,
what ought to be prosperity is turned into the
other thing. So we throw it upon the drought.
We blame Providence instead of our own im-
providence.
The “Afternoon-Tea” Politician.
“Well, anyhow, I hope it won’t be Kingston.”
That was the summing-up of a talk on “the
political situation ” by a well-known daily-paper
reporter, who possesses the valuable quality for
one of his calling that he has no particular preju-
dices with regard to political principles. “ And
why?” “Oh, because he is such a surly beggar.
Newspaper fellows all hate him. He’s never
really civil.” That was a judgment on politics of
some significance. Australian public life—chiefly
because of the absence of large ideas, and the
neglect of high traditions—is to a very great
extent governed by petty little considerations of
personal amiability. The man who can provide
the right kind of drinks at the right time for
the right persons possesses a valuable political
asset. As a statesman he may be fool or rogue,
as a financier he may be reckless, thriftless, or
even dishonest; but if, as an entertainer, he has a
taking way with him, some degree—perhaps a
very distinguished degree—of success is open to
him. He can “shout” his way to a Premiership
and a title—taking “shout ” in its broadest sense,
of providing for this man whisky, for this man
flattery, for this man nepotic concessions, for this
man afternoon tea.
* * * »
For even afternoon tea is not to be despised as
a weapon of political warfare—especially in these
days when the word “ man ” in politics, as well
as in anthropology, denotes man and woman.
Indeed, the afternoon-tea politician is often the
most wily and assiduous in the art of making
small personal attentions take the place of large
political principles as reasons for public advance-
ment. Mr. Joseph Carruthers, for instance, is
at the present moment artfully afternoon-tea-ing
his way into the hearts of the women voters;
hardly a political woman of any importance can
come to Sydney from Bourke, Booligal or
Bathurst but goes, literally, to have afternoon-tea
(with two lumps of flattery) with the statesman
who leads the Opposition in N.S.W. And in dis-
pensing afternoon-tea, Mr. Carruthers is making
no precedent, only following the freetrade tradi-
tion in N.S.W. In his struggling Opposition
days Mr. G. H Reid used to give “ afternoon-
teas ” to Labor members, and he then cast his
macaroon biscuits on the waters with notably
excellent results to himself personally. Later, in
power, Mr. Reid failed to keep up his hos-
pitality, and in time Parliament gave him
the reputation of being a “dry push.” Setting
aside tne questions of public policy which brought
about his fall, and looking deeper, a great deal of
the explanation for the sudden dramatic going-
out of Mr. Reid’s light, after a long and laborious
kindling, was that he had forgotten the art of
afternoon-teaing, had neglected the little flatteries,
the personal pleasantries, and the concessions with
which he had wormed his way into power—had
forgotten them and had no real foundation of
ability and principle to fall back upon, and so fell
to make room for Mr. Lyne, the most artfully
affable man in Australia, who established a record
by winning over a whole newspaper proprietary
by the aftemoon-tea method. Of course, in that
case, the tea was metaphorical—billets for direc-
tors and staff-members, titles in prospective for
proprietors and the like.
• ♦ * *
But whether it takes the form of afternoon-tea
for the women-folk, cigars and whisky for the pro-
minent constituent, bland smiles and courteous
consideration for the reporter, promises of titles
and billets for the newspaper proprietor, local
works for the roads-and-bridges member, artful
flattery for the young and impressionable member,
this bribery—for it is, in effect, bribery—has a
huge influence in Australian public life. The
dull plodder, whose affable smile was destined by
nature for the ribbon-counter of a genteel drapery
emporium, goes into politics and finds that smile
a way to advancement. The instinctive “boss,”
whose only chance of disgracing public life should
be as a ward-politician, manoeuvring kerbing and
guttering jobs for his supporters, creeps and in-
trudes into large affairs of State, and shapes the
fate of the Government of a State or a nation,
by the concession of a bridge or a road. The
thin-haired, rabbit-brained religious fool or
schemer pushes into politics with the help of
creatures of his own type but of slightly inferior
intelligence, on the strength of promises of hell
on this earth as well as in the next world for the
followers of other brands of Christianity. (To
some types of men the most welcome “shout”
you can afford is to promise them perdition for
their neighbors.) Such affable nobodies. 1 ‘ bosses ”
and sectaries —necessarily of no special ability
and no real strength of character, since other-
wise they would not seek prominence by such
paths—crowd forward, and in crowding for-
ward often prevent men of real worth from
entering into public life, until pessimists fore-
tell the time when it will be a rarity to
see a quite honest man in politics, relying on his
honesty alone as a claim to power; refusing
to wheedle any sort of personages; not seeking
to conciliate with bribes or flattery: demanding
support on high grounds of principle. Still, it is
comforting to reflect, there are yet some such
men in politics; and, assuredly, there would be
many more were it not for a prevailing miscon-
ception that the voters really want the afternoon-
tea type of politician, and would prefer him to
men of ability and principle if given the choice.
Experience does show that the people are
sometimes so foolish as to give power to mere
schemers. But they are seldom so foolish as to
deliberately reject the better for the poorer type,
With a bold enough lead from men of tried
worth, the electorates are generally sensible
enough : it is the lead that is so often lacking.
* * * *
The history of such men as Ballance,
Kingston and Turner, who never worked to
power on afternoon-tea lines, does not suggest at
all the hopelessness of honesty and largeness of
mind as an equipment for a career in politics.
There is no such corruption of the public mind as
to make honesty hopeless. What does exist—an
evil truly, but not so great a one as that—is a stare
of public mind which allows men, dishonest or
small-minded, to get to power by arts of corrup-
tion and of flattery. There is a lack of that
austerity in the Australian public mind which
would greet with scorn the small tricks of the small
men to creep into power, and would reject with
contempt even able men who used such devices
to eke out their talents. There is no better task
for the Australian Nationalist than to preach ever
the need of that spirit. A democracy—especially
a democracy verging towards Socialism—must be
governed by austere men. A despotism or an
oligarchy may with safety have its friendships,
its corruptions, and its jobs. A social democracy
cannot. When the people rule themselves and to
a large extent employ themselves, when the men
they put into power are at once their servants and
their masters, those men must be of the type who
stand aloof from all temptations of courting
popular favor at the price of the popular welfare.
A democracy with rulers of the intriguing, pan-
dering type must drift downwards. In truth,
there could be no higher compliment to Kingston
than that he is an “aloof man,” with none of the
small graces with which smaller men eke out
smaller talents. It is the type the Commonwealth
needs.
For the Sake of a Bloated City.
Its choice as the Federal territory will, if wisdom
guides the Australian Parliament, shortly open
up the Monaro district of N.S.W. for the popula-
tion which it is fitted to support. In the mean-
while, it serves as a conspicuous example of the
evils of that policy of land monopoly and centrali-
sation which N.S.W. has pursued so consistently.
The Monaro, half a century or so ago, attracted
the notice of a daring speculator, Mr. Boyd, who,
if his ideas savored of megalomania, at least
founded them on an entirely reasonable basis.
He saw the capacity of the Monaro coun-
try, and he saw, too, the right path for its
development— a seaport at Twofold Bay, and a
road, ultimately a railway, into the interior table-
land to bring the products of farm and pasture
to the coast by a route extending not much
more than 60 miles. Boyd died after planning
and beginning to build a magnificent city on Two-
fold Bay, leaving behind a few fine buildings and
a valuable hint to future generations. The build-
ings are now perishing as rapidly as their solid
character will allow. The hint is still ignored.
Settlement has come to the Monaro, attracted by
its eternal streams and its rich pastures; but
the natural port of the district has been left
neglected, and the Monaro cut off from com-
munication with the outside world. Sixty miles
of railway from Bombala to Eden would have
opened up the finest tract of country in N.S.W.
to every Australian market. But it was never
built, never even seriously thought of by succes-
sive Governments, their thoughts always bent on
aggrandising Sydney, and smothering all develop-
ment in the country which would not serve to
further swell the bloated metropolis. Lately a
railway has been pushed down from Goulburn to
Cooma on the edge of the Monaro country ; in
time it will probably reach Bombala. But even
then it will not be the railway wanted. Goods put
on it must be dragged over 300 miles to Sydney,
though the coast is only 60 miles away, and the
coast is the door to the markets of Australia
and of the world. With a railway from the
Monaro to Eden 25 years ago, the district would
carry to-day, probably, a population of 100,000.
Farmers would have flocked to land which needs
no clearing, which is never drought-parched, and
through which perpetual streams flow.
* » * *
As it is, the vicious system of centralisation
has bred land monopoly, and smothered Monarc
development. Small settlers came to the Monarc
originally, but most of them were in time starvec
out. To grow wheat, oats, hay, fruit was useless
The produce would have to be carted to a far
distant railway, and then hauled to Sydney.
Carriage would thus eat up the whole profit, and
even more. So the fertile land fell into the hands
almost exclusively of graziers, who have put
it to the best use under the circumstances.
But at what cost to the country of population
and of wealth ! The vast Bibbenlulce estate on
the Monaro is said to regularly employ only
seven men. Its area of 65,000 acres could easily
keep 100 families in comfort with close settle-
ment. Very recently 1100 acres of the estate
were, by an exchange, thrown open for settle-
ment, split into three farms—average size less
than 400 acres. The land attracted over 200
applicants, and every man reckoned that to
succeed in getting, as a homestead selection, 300
odd acres of this land was to be “a
made man for life.” The areas offered were
of the average quality of the estate: so clearly
this great tract, which now keeps half a
score of workers in employment and half-a-dozen
absentee proprietors in luxury, might be support-
ing in comfort, at least, 100 families. But first
the Monaro must have railway communication.
At present a few small farms can exist with
dairy-farming—teaming the butter down to the
coast, 60 miles distant—but obviously if, instead
of a few, there were some thousands (there
is land for 10,000 farmers on the Monaro),
the transit conditions would have to be
changed. Land monopoly on the Monaro is
at present propped up by want of railway com-
munication, and the fact explains to an extent
why the residents of the district are anxious for
the choice of the Australian Parliament to fall
upon the locality as the site of the Federal
territory. The Federalisation of the Monaro
would kill land monopoly by a resumption, at a
fair price, of the large estates, and at the same
time give the district railway communication with
the coast.
• • •
Why the railway has so far been lacking would
puzzle a stranger who sees the rich deep soil,
capable of growing almost anything, and the per-
petual rivers fed by the snow-beds of the
Australian Alps. But to the man conversant
with N. S. W. politics there is nothing
mysterious about the smothering of this district.
Its natural path of development was towards
its own port at Twofold Bay, as old Ben Boyd
recognised. A_ railway to that port would have
been, comparatively, a small matter. But then it
would have possibly allowed some of the Monaro
trade to escape from Sydney. The Monaro wool
and wheat and meat might have gone direct to
Europe and America or—shuddersome horror—
some of it might have gone to Melbourne. The
Monaro man’s tea, sugar and shirt might also have
escaped paying toll to the Sydney importer by going
direct to him through his own port. That could
not be allowed. N.S.W. Governments have never
ruled for the State, but always for the one city of the
State. A natural development policy would have
sent railways out from Grafton, from Newcastle,
from Sydney, from Jervis Bay and from Twofold
Bay, and if railway construction had followed those
lines the population of the State would probably
be by now 50 per cent, higher than at present; but
the population of Sydney would be slightly less ;
and all political effort is directed towards further
bloating out an over-swollen city. It is the
tendency of Federation to combat, and in time to
destroy, that vicious policy of centralisation, which
in a measure explains the fanatical hostility
towards the Union which the ‘ ‘ Cabbage Tree
Mob” of Sydney and equivalent coteries in
Melbourne show.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Blackleg 1 of a New Era.
The worst wrench that the N.S.W. Arbitration
Act has had conies from Newcastle, the district
which for years past has been most in need of the
Act and has suffered most from its absence, and
which therefore might reasonably be expected to
guard it now. The men at two of the smaller
collieries up there went to the Arbitration Court
some time ago, and asked for more pay. It was
agreed that the common Newcastle system of a
hewing rate based on the selling price of coal, was
the fairest, and a schedule was drawn up by the
Court—the miner was to get so much for his work
when the owner got so much for his coal.
Both parties were apparently satisfied; anyhow,
neither kicked. The owners put in weighing
machines, and prepared to start the new system
and 1904 together. Obviously the court could
not say what the average selling rate was going
to be in a period win -h was not then completed ;
that much had 10 be .eft to some outside account-
ant with the owners’ books to guide him. But
the accountant’s first certificate disappointed the
miners ; the selling price had not been as high as
they had expected, and therefore, under the court’s
award, the hewing rate could not be as high, either.
Instead, in fact, of the men getting the rise they
had expected, they had actually to suffer a re-
duction. In their keen disappointment, and act-
ing, it is satisfactory to know, against the pointed
advice of the “district officers” of the miners’
association, they refused work. The award had
provided for a mixed committee to deal with any
disputes which might arise under it; but they did
not even seek its services. They simply took up
a position of open hostility to the award of the
court, and therefore to the court and the law


. And > incidentally, they did worse even
than this ; for they put into the mouths of the
opponents of Arbitration the gibe that workmen
don t really believe in arbitration, and will only
support it so long as it puts an extra few pence
into their pockets—that, in short, unless
they can play “ heads I win, tails you lose ” with
it, they will jump on it enthusiastically. And
vov! 1 * 1 * s P° ss i^ e to answer to this gibe ?
What those who want to wipe out Arbitration say
is true in this case at least—true, every word of
Had the award been in the workmen’s favor,
they would, have accepted it; because it is not,
they flout it. That the other side has, in some
cases, flouted and so far defeated the court and
the law, too—especially in the case of the shearers
is no answer. One crime does not excuse
another. And, anyvay, there is even less to be
said for the worker breaking the law and weaken-
ing it than for the other side. It is essen-
tially the workers’ law. The employers, as a
rule, did not ask for it ; the workers did.
It was the leading plank in their political plat-
form for years. The hostile employer may there-
fore be expected to be always ready to show how
weak it is, and how it can be defeated ; while the
worker ought to be relied upon to strengthen it
and to prevent its defeat. If he does not do that,
he is no better than the blackleg of the old days
of strikes and lockouts. He is, in fact, the black-
leg of the new era—the man who is careless of the
effects of his actions upon his fellows, and does
what he thinks will suit himself best, no matter
whether others sink or swim.
Throwing- the Bill into the Cradle. -
Tasmania has added something more to ita
shocking financial record ; it has lived beyond its
means in one of the most prosperous years that
ever came its way, its expenditure during 1903
having been .£877,000, or £18,792 more than its
receipts. This makes the 13th time in 20 years
when the same story of either dishonesty or in-
capacity has had to be told. In the last three
years the record has been as follows :
Revenue. Expenditure. Deficit.
1901 .. £826,163 .. £870,442 .. £44,279
1902 .. 734,663 .. 850,684 .. 116,021
1903 .. 858,208 .. 877,000 .. 18,792
Total deficit (three years) .. £179,092
The population of the State at the end of 1902
was 177,080; so that, in those three years, the
island has dodged taxation to the extent of over
£1 per head, and lias simply thrown the Bill into
the cradle for the next generation to wrestle with.
In other words, the Tasmanians of to-day are con-
demning their children to pay not only their own
taxation but a large share of their fathers’ as well.
Something can be said in extenuation of a
borrowing policy pursued for the purpose of
providing permanent public works. But nothing
'''™?’cr can said in extenuation of a “policy ”
of drifting into debt for the policeman’s pay, and
the railwayman’s uniform, and the scavenger’s
wages. A State which does that—which does it,
not under stress of most untoward circumstances,
but as an ordinary thing ; which does it even in a
year of unusual plenty, when its produce was
retching an almost unheard-of price in the neigh-
boring markets—comes perilously near inviting
the coming generation to repudiate the debt
or its cowardly, Micawber-like parent. And
almost worse than the figures themselves
I s the horror with which the prospect of any
iuture surplus is viewed. In the State itself acres
indignant protest are being printed because a
tax is being collected which, it is thought, may
next year bring in a few pounds more than will be
necessary to pay out-of-pocket expenses; and
pretty well every daily-paper editor in the small
island is pouring out his sympathy for “ the tax-
ridden people of Tasmania.” As a matter of factj
they are not at all heavily taxed now ; but if they
are “ tax-ridden,” as they say, to what are they
condemning the Tasmania of the future—the
Tasmania that will be expected to pay up its
father’s debts as well as its own !

A Death-Bed Repentance.
The Reform movement is quickening in the
N.S.W. State Labor party; but some members
are discouraged by the argument that a “death-
bed repentance” is a bad thing. It undoubtedly
is. A better time for the Labor party to have re-
pented was years ago. A still better policy would
have been never to -have sinned. But the question
is not now, in 1904, whether it would have been
better to have repented in 1902, or better not to
have sinned at all. The issue is between a death-
bed repentance in 1904 and a death-bed non-repent-
ance. And there is at least this much to be said,
that the Labor party’s reputation for sincerity
and truthfulness is still good. It has never, for
instance, gone into power as a Freetrade party
and continued to protect all its friends’ industries,
from Freetrade newspapers upwards. On the
whole, it would be reasonable for the electorates
to trust the N. S. W. State Labor party if it now put
forward a Whole Hog programme of financia
reform ; certainly it would be more reasonable to
trust that party than any other party. Finally,
irrespective of whether the step leads to a party
victory at once, or only at some future time, is it
not a good thing for the organisation which was
started with such glowing professions of honesty
and reform to get into the right path again ?
—_
Reduction of Members.
It is said that there is still some chance of the
N.S.W. referendum vote for the reduction of
members being ignored, or at any rate postponed
for a Parliament. The fact that 35 members of the
present House must disappear, and that probably
70 members have a secret fear that they will be
among the 35, is sufficiently good explanation for
the rumor. But it would be a very serious
mistake —even for the N.S.W. Assembly—to
attempt any hanky-panky move in the matter
at this juncture. The Referendum has spoken
and should be promptly obeyed. Almost
equally regrettable would it be if any
attempt were made, as is forecasted, to gerry-
mander the new electoral subdivisions to give
the country man a much greater representa-
tion than the city man. It is true that rural
interests have in the past been shamefully neg-
lected for the sake of the bloated metropolis ;
but the remedy is not to be found in a negation of
the democratic principle of one-man-one-vote-one-
value. It is true also that a fair re-distribution
will give a far greater preponderance to the city
vote than at present, and thus, to an extent,
strengthen the bad influence of the city news-
papers. Still the case for going against a vital
principle of democracy is not made out. The
people must be trusted, and it is their vested
and inalienable right to make fools of themselves
if they wish. The remedy is to educate them
better, not to disfranchise them. The logical
complement of reduction of members will be a
thorough system of local government: that will
do away with the grievance of the country-folk,
that with huge electorates their local interests
will be neglected; a distribution of seats so that
the country man’s vote would be of greater value
than the city man’s would not effect that end
nearly so thoroughly.
A Letter from Our Creditors’ Country.
Michael Flubsoheim, the well-known economist,
in a letter to The Bulletin, from London, goes
into one of the byways of the present fiscal con-
troversy in Britain, and propounds the rather
startling theory that the foreign debts to Britain
are a source of Britain’s misery
The immense excess of British imports over exports is
aocounted for principally by the rent, interest, and divi-
dends which England receives in the shape of foreign
products. But the State receives no interest or dividends
from abroad except for the Suez Canal shares owned by
the British Government. Not England, but English
individuals, capitalists, get these other dues. It may he
said that this is merely a play with words ; yet I main-
tain that this want of exactness in terms is the cause of
a most fatal oversight in the discussion. If it were the
State that imported merchandise for tribute dues from
abroad, as it did in old Borne, such imports would benefit
the people, provided they were evenly distributed. But
the matter is entirely different where the income is due
to individuals, as is actually the case. Here all depends
on the spending of this income. If it is realised in the
shape of imports consumed by the owners of the incomes,
no deleterious effect is had on the producers of the
country to which the capitalists belong, though it would
be better for them if the incomes were paid in money,
and that money were spent at home. But the case is
entirely different when more goods are imported in pay-
ment of the interest than are consumed by the capitalists
at home, because in this case the excess is bought by the
people who otherwise would consume home products.
Every pound’s worth thus purchased will deprive a cor-
responding number of home producers of employment.
We have no statistics to guide us in the estimation of
the amounts thus absolutely taken from the employ-
ment fund. I only know that the amount is much larger
than we might judge at the first look, for most foreign
investments are made by parties who put aside a great
part of the money thus earned. Many of them live
entirely on their home incomes, and re-invest abroad their
foreign incomes. After making allowances for freight,
insurance, commissions, etc., and adding the amounts
spent abroad by English tourists (Switzerland alone
obtains four millions a year, of which perhaps one-half is
paid out by Englishmen ; Italy probably quite as much),
and then valuing that proportion of the remainder which
is imported without having its equivalent consumed by
the capitalists who draw the incomes from abroad, I do
not think I am far out if I count the actual deficit as
amounting to £100,000,000. This amount is really taken
off from the legitimate employment fund due to the
English producer. We may well count the total not
spent in England, but in foreign markets ; and we may
safely say that over 5,000,000 people are thus deprived
of work. In reality it is mostly not a case of total unem-
ployment for the million families, but partial unemploy-
ment for many millions.
My reasoning does not at all stand in the way of the
old Freetraders’ axiom that “ products are always paid for
with products.” This is only a question of date and com-
pound interest. The investments of England abroad (to
use the usual term) have been valued at £2,500,000,000 by
Thorold Rogers. As this was something like 20 years ago,
the increase through compound interest of the uncon-
sumed portion of the income will probably have increased
the amount since. How did this enormous amount origi-
nate except by the postponement of the importation of
the product—that is to say, by letting the debt, or part
of it, remain at compound interest ?
What compound interest is capable of Proudhon
showed : 100 francs, invested at 5 per cent, compound
interest during 600 years, would reach the enormous sum
of 107,854,010,777,600 francs —a sum 20 times as large as
the value of our globe, with all its moveable and immove-
able wealth—at the time he wrote !
This is from the point of view of England, which
lends the money ; The Bulletin looks at it from
the point of view of Australia, which owes the
money :
(1) It may be bad for England to get paid in goods ;
but it is worse for Australia tehave to pay. In England
they can’t get the work to do. Australia can get the
work all right, but somebody else has to get the product.
(2) Flurscheim says, truly, that many English capital-
ists “ live entirely on their home incomes and re-invest
abroad their foreign incomes.” That is to say, after
Australia has paid interest (in goods) the English
capitalist sends back other goods—he “re-invests his
income.” Thus the English workman, who makes these
goods, does gain something by the re-investment. He
supplies us with steel rails and other things which
Australia ought to make for itself. So if the English
workman is done out of one lot of production, the
Australian workman is done out of another. And he was
also done out of the manufacture of the goods repre_
sented by the original investment.
(3) But, though money is re-invested, repayment in
products is “ only a question of date and compound in-
terest”—and £1 at 5 percent, becomes in 100 years £l4O !
The £1 borrowed to-day becomes an appalling thing a
few years hence, when deficits follow —in other wo ds,
when the interest bill is not kept paid. And, as Flur-
scheim says, this is how the enormous debt has grown
up—“by the postponement of the importation (to Eng-
land) of the product.” In plain words, we don’t pay up,
and the bill grows amazingly. But “it is only a question
of date,” and the product will have to be paid. That or
repudiation. Flurscheim shudders at the effect on the
home (English) population of the payment of the £l4O in
goods. The Bulletin shudders at. the prospect of Aus-
tralia having to pay it. And yet shuddering won’t do
much good.
(4) Flurscheim evidently suggests the protection of the
English workman from these payments in goods. Whereat
The Bulletin shudders some more. Australia, with its
vast debt, its huge interest-bill, must find a market; and
England, under Freetrade, suits it very well. It may
be very hard on England’s own people ; but if England’s
own people got the work instead of Australia, it would
be very hard on Australia. If Australia hadn’t got chin-
deep in foreign debt, and if it hadn’t to pay interest,
Britain might shut its door and protect its own people,
and Australia would say that it was doing the sensible
thing; but, as matters stand, once Britain closes its
doors—once it decides that its own people shall have em-
ployment—Australia will have to work harder than ever
in order to meet its enormous obligations, unless (which
is unlikely) it can find equally good markets elsewhere.
And yet, in spite of the fact that this will probably
happen in the future, Australia, instead of reducing its
obligations with all the speed that it knows how to put
on, goes on increasing them !

His Honor’s Liver.
“ Burglar Bill ” : I am waiting for an up-to-date
member, who understands the weighty influence
the liver exercises in determining verdicts and
fixing sentences, to bring in a Bill called “ The
Prisoners’ Fair Dinkum Bill.” The liver is a
pessimistic organ at any time, and it generally
throws its weight on the side of the prosecution,
which is not fair to the prisoner on trial. One
never recognises its power till he is in the dock
before a Judge, whose yellow, bilious-looking skin
proves that his liver is on strike just at present. A
conviction will be a safety-valve for his spleen, and
he imagines, as he reads up the maximum penalty
before the case opens, that it is his keen percep-
tion that tells him of your guilt. One can almost
imagine his Honor’s liver making lunars at the
prisoner through his Honor’s bloodshot eyes.
The first clause of the proposed Bill should read :
Every Judge before taking his seat shall make a sworn
declaration that to the best of his belief his liver is in
good order, that his digestive organs are performing
their duties in a commendable manner, and that he has
no brown taste in the mouth or specks before the eyes.
This is especially necessary because a man, as a
rule, doesn’t become a Judge till he reaches that
period of life when the liver goes on strike. A
minor clause might be drafted to include
gout, toothache, lumbago, and other devilish
ailments which upset the proper administration
of justice; but the liver is the most important.
After his Honor has thus testified that the pri-
soner has a good chance of securing a fair and
impartial trial, he should be allowed to take his
seat. Every juryman, before being sworn, should
be called upon to give his liver a character upon
oath. In the case of jurymen I should insert a
special clause asking them to declare that the
fact of being empanelled on a jury does not in
any way interfere with their business—that the
old woman, with the help of the boys, will be able
to run the “public ” or the grocery until the court
is over. The thought of the old woman being
unable to serve the rush of thirsty beer-drinkers,
or sugar-buyers, oftimes brings about hurried
verdicts.
♦
Some Tasmanians have taken up the English
Nonconformist-conscience cry of “passive re-
sistance,” and propose to meet the income-tax
collector with a martyr’s air of resignation, but
no cash. The situation has its humors. Tas-
mania threw Lewis out from the Premiership with
a violence unparalleled in the Speck’s political
history, and put Propsting in his place. Lewis,
recognising the State’s hideous financial drift in
the past, had proposed to collect enough taxes to
keep things square; while Propsting talked re-
trenchment and reform, and proposed to remit
the tax on personal-exertion incomes, and to put
the incidence “on a more scientific basis.” And
the Dodery House, whose business it is to keep
the scientific basis off the premises, laid low and
said nothing. Then Propsting brought in his
measures—and Dodery promptly threw them out.
The Speck had forgotten its Dodery. Even now,
when it has been called upon to pay the very tax
which it so enthusiastically kicked Lewis out for
proposing to collect, it can’t bring itself to
seriously think of kicking Dodery out. Lewis
may be kicked; but Dodery represents something
too sacred for that. Heaven knows what it is;
but it is something. Therefore, as Dodery mustn’t
be kicked, the only protest it can think of offer-
ing is “ passive resistance.” Apparently it mis-
takes itself for a cabbage, or a codlin moth at the
most.
