Copt of characteristic agreement between land-
lord and tenant in S.W. Victoria, where condi-
tions tend to the growth of a haughty squirearchy
and an enslaved peasantry :
The tenant shall once in every year, immediately after
harvest, and before any wheat shall be removed from the
land, but not in any case later than the 28th day of
February in each year, pay to the landlord the rent (viz.,
10s. per acre).
The tenant shall destroy and keep down all rabbits,
thistles, and noxious weeds on the land.
The tenant shall not burn off any stubble, straw, or
waste without the previous consent of the landlord.
The tenant shall not be entitled to graze or feed any
stock upon the land except the working horses used by
him in cultivating the land and in harvesting the crop
thereon.
The tenant shall not keep any dog, or allow any dog to
be kept or to be on the said land.
The tenant shall not make, construct, or put any gate
or gateway in or through any of the fences surrounding
the land, except at such points as may be agreed upon
and approved of by the landlord. Any such gate shall be
good and substantial, and shall be made and erected by
the tenant at his own cost, but to be approved of by the
landlord. Upon the termination of the tenancy the
tenant shall within one calendar month remove the gate
and restore and make good and perfectly stockproof the
fencing at the point where any such gate shall have been
constructed.
The landlord shall in every year, immediately after the
harvest and until the next ensuing ploughing, be solely
and exclusively entitled to the right of grazing over the
whole of the land, and to the exclusive use and enjoy-
ment of all stubble hay and straw for the time being on
the land. The landlord shall be at liberty at any time to
enter upon the land for any purpose.
The tenant shall, immediately prior to the termination
of his tenancy, plough a strip of land at least 15ft. in
width round the whole of the land hereby demised so as
to form an effectual fire-break.
A heavy moan from Launceston Examinee
The Tasmanian Parliament has brought its session to
a close, and we think we are within the mark in saying
that never in the history of the island has there been
such an absolute waste of time and such a travesty on
representative government. Tasmania is governed by a
little coterie of about ten men in a Legislative Council
elected by a few thousand people. Like the old Venetian
Council of Ten, they constitute an oligarchy of the worst
type. A short six months ago the electors, by an over-
whelming majority, returned an Assembly pledged to a
policy of reform, a repeal of the personal exertion sections
of the income tax, and a general system of retrench-
ment. They ignominiously dismissed a Ministry
which had refused to carry out this policy. The
Propsting Administration took up the reins, and
initiated a policy in accord with the expressed wishes
of the people. That was carried through the Assembly,
and although in doing so Ministers made some errors in
Parliamentary tactics, they adhered honestly to their
pledges. But the “Council of Ten” stepped in and
blocked every progressive measure. Arrogating to them-
selves a position which is at variance with the spirit of
the constitution, and contrary to the principle
of the bicameral system of Government, they
contemptuously ignored everything the Ministry
had initiated, threw the measures into the waste
paper basket, and practically told the representa-
tives of the people they were a set of idiots. The
“ Council of Ten ” went further. They took the financial
administration of the State into their own hands. They
imposed what taxation they thought necessary. . .
The session has been absolutely wasted. Everything is
where it stood before the last election took place. For
five months one Chamber has been framing measures in
accord with the express wish of the electors, and the
other Chamber has been as industriously rejecting them.
Not one measure of importance has been placed on the
statute book, and we have arrived at a legislative impasse
when one House or the other must give way. We do
not think the electors will be content to see the Assembly
and the Ministry sink into mere marionettes, to dance at
the bidding of ten men, nor do we think they are agree-
able that the Council, some members of which are getting
too infirm to attend to their duties, shall arrogate to
themselves the position of rulers of this State.
And then the stodgy old paper solemnly warns
the people not to do anything violent to the
blessed system of having two Houses of Parlia-
ment, and keeping a Council either t® say ditto
to the Assembly (in which case it is useless) or to
flatly contradict the Assembly (in which case it is
noxious and disastrous).
4
It is to be hoped that the efforts to revive the
agitation for a Greater Sydney will reach success.
The wasteful extravagance of the present system
is only equalled by the futile showmanry of the
battalion of gawky fellows who ape dignity
while acting as asses. The multiplication of
municipalities is the multiplication of mayors,
most of them noodles; of aldermen, most of them
duffers; aud of clerks, auditors and inspectors,
most of them creatures of a noodle or a duffer.
Over 40 mayors make the area of what should be
a single municipality a scene of riot and refresh-
ments, and the 700 aldermen would have killed
the municipal idea long ago—they are incom-
petent enough and viperish enough to do it—but
stupidity is so ingrained in the bulk of them, and
pompous exhibitions of importance keep their
brains so windy, that their most strenuous work
ends in puff and blow. Citizens should back up
heartily any scheme promising a central com-
petent authority responsible to a wide public
opinion, and not to a mere clique here and fusion
of personal interests there. A Greater Sydney
must mean better men; it could not mean worse
or as bad. Even if the benefits were not visible
or immediate, the satisfaction of bundling out of
office the present unwholesome crowds of arrant
humbugs would be something to go on with.

Victorian M.L.A. Roberts had hardly taken
liis seat before he followed M.L.A. Wilkins
in laying several charges against the Melbourne
police force and demanding an inquiry. Irvine
and Murray asked for specific complaints, with
names of witnesses, etc. Two specific charges
were promptly put before Murray to go on with.
First concerns a constable in the metropolitan
force alleged to have been found, when on
duty, drunk in a house of pleasure. Second
charge is against an official, that he accepted a
bribe—specified amount £3ooo—to stifle a prose-
cution for burglary. The, as yet, not particularly
specified charges, as to which the witnesses want
further guarantees of immunity, are even more
serious—including, as they do, sharing proceeds
of robberies, blackmailing, suborning to commit
felony, and suborning to conspire to illegally con-
vict. Evidently either the Melbourne police force
is very bad or it has a very bad lot of slanderers
out after its reputation. In either case the neces-
sity for a clearing-up inquiry is obvious.
J. G. Goddard, in Westminster Review, criti-
cising ecclesiastical justification of the late Boer
war:—
To glory in England’s record as the fear of the
oppressor and the hope of the oppressed is, no doubt,
highly patriotic, but when Englishmen themselves are
the oppressors what becomes of the hope ? The Boers did
to some extent interpose barriers to the gratification of
the greed of the capitalists; and surely there is an un-
conscious irony in the suggestion that we are to guard
the natives from exploitation- we who exploit more than
any other nation ; we who exact from India five-six-
teenths of her prodnce as the price of our government,
and who refused to make her a national grant when
stricken by famine beoause we were spending two
millions a week in extending our territory and empire ;
we who grant charters to conscienceless companies, and
strike bargains with mine-owners, whose sole concern for
the native is what they can get out of him. Yet the
prognostication was confidently made as a justification
for the forcible suppression of the Boer regime
of “ inequality ” and “ privilege.” We who, having
been “ led to believe ” that the treatment meted out to
the natives was unworthy of a civilised power, and having
now uttered our recantation, are appealing to the Kaffir’s
love of finery and extra wives as a mode of inducing him
to spend his days in the bowels of the earth for the white
man’s benefit—we it was who were to protect the native
from exploitation, and then vindicate the war to that
section of our people who regarded it with grave appre-
hension. Oh, the pity of it! Oh, the shame of it! What
can equal the smug complacency which sits in false judg-
ment upon others, and is blind to the existence in our-
selves of the very vices attributed to them !

Sydney D.T., in a spasm of candor, com-
ments on the cause of the municipal-reform spirit
in Sydney Council:
The new Municipal Act, which enfranchises everyone
even to the casual lodger, has altered all that. The land-
lord interest is no longer supreme, and the Lord Mayor
who undertakes to compel property-owners to keep their
places in a sanitary condition has not the same powerful
influence to restrain him now as his remote predecessors
had. This fact has been marked by a noticeable improve-
ment in civic administration, which Mr. Lees will have
no difficulty in following up.
When, however, the Labor party sought to apply
to the whole State in a Local Government Bill the
same franchise conditions that have worked so
well in Sydney, G. H. Reid, with the approval of
the D.T., dropped the Bill in shocked horror,
arguing that municipal government must be left
in the hands of the property-owners. The plague
was the result of property-owners’ government in
Sydney, and gave the first great lift to the Labor
party argument for adult suffrage in municipal as
in State politics.
Scaring Away Capital.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Thk cove wot ’as the capital is grindin’ out a
curse,
’E’s goin’to cart ’is cash away, for things is gettin’
worse,
’E’s goin’ to pack ’is tram-lines in ’is little carpet-
bag,
And stow away ’is batt’ries in ’is jolly little swag.
An’ if ’e goes to foreign lands—whatever shall we
do?
A race like us who boast we came of ’eroes ’cross
the blue.
We sha’n’t know ’ow to feed ourselves or wipe our
little nose,
If this ’ere cove wot ’as the cash gets up ’is oash
and goes !
Tiie cove wot ’as the capital—’e thinks a lot, does
’e;
’E reckons ’ow the likes of ’im is lords of you an’
me;
’E reckons if ’e left the land there’d be a pretty
brew ;
’E reckons if ’e took ’is ’ook—why, we would ’ook
it too!
I think I see ’im goin’—with ’is vengeance in ’is
eye !
The crowds a-weepin’ on the wharf an’ wailin’ out
“ Good-bye ! ”
’E’s got the railways in the ’old—the railway
shortage too—
An’ several stacks of paper that are branded
“1.0. U. ” !
’E’s bin a good old friend to us—the Man who’s
got the Bag;
’E’s let us sweat and labor since our Fam’ly was
a Lag;
’E’d let us sweat and labor still, and never make
a moan,
But ’e cannot stand us thinkin’ that the Country
is our own !
So ’e’s off to good old Yurrup, and ’e’s off to
London town,
And e’s off to see the Kaiser and to tell the British
Crown,
’Ow the fellers in this country, with the hard and
horny paw,
They are meddlin’ with the makin’ of their own
pertickler Law !
For ’e knows the land is settled when ’e waves
’is last adoo ;
And ’e knows the day’s a-comin’ when the Land
will ’ave to rue ;
And when all is sunk in Ruin ’e will cry—“l
told yer so—
Yer drove the Cove wot ’ad the Cash ter get ’is
Cash and Go !
Q.

RABBITS!
ARE THEY TO BE
A PROFIT OR PEST?
It rests entirely with our settlers whether it shall cost them and the State a large amount
annually to continue the war which has been waged against “ bunny ” for years past,
whether they will take the trouble to turn bim into an asset which will bring a steady
revenue to the country and provide employment for THOUSANDS of its people. The
Rosewarne Packing Company, Ltd., is now receiving rabbits at its works in Young,
Narandera and Sydney. The highest market price is given in return for freshly-killed
rabbits free of any commission or charges. F „ further particulars, address—
THE ROSEWARNE PACKING COMPANY, Limited,

“ For the Sake of a Woman," though
which woman is still in doubt at the end of the
piece, winds up Charles Holloway’s six months’
season at the Criterion; and those who haven’t
seen this company of mostly Australian and
mostly young players are hereby encouraged
to go and do so before it takes its passage in about
a fortnight, by Maoriland boat, to the modern
Mecca of theatrical managers, where The Bulle-
tin trusts Holloway will find a profit of great size
awaiting him. Also those who saw the co. when,
six months ago, it first appeared in “Two Little
Vagabonds,” might do worse than turn up, if only
to note what great good half-a-year’s hard work
alongside Holloway, and Alfred Dampier, and
finally C. 8.. Stanford, has done for the infants
of the crowd. Miss Holloway is now quite able
to open her mouth when she talks, though some-
times she still forgets to do it; while Vivian
Edwards, the other “juvenile lead,” doesn’t always
talk as though he were competing with a violent
thunderstorm. Altogether, in fact, it is a better
co. than started the season; for, though Miss
Conroy has lately gone touring with one of Ander-
son’s co’s., Miss Gresham has come in with Stan-
ford ; and Miss Irby Marshall, 1903’s most suc-
cessful amateur, has been admitted to full citizen-
ship in Poverty Point.
• » * •
This time Stanford takes a rest from
villainy, though, curiously enough (it may be
from force of old habit), he does the only killing
in the piece. Lady Clare has married him—
Heaven knows why ; the man who wrote the
piece didn’t, and he didn’t make the mistake of
attempting to explain the inexplicable. He just
married them, and left it at that. Lady Clare
has, of course, a past ; Edwards, who looks about
23, yet owns and controls a great London bank, is
mixed up with this portion of the lady’s career.
After her marriage she has some more past; but
Edwards, being a very moral young man who
doesn’t take married ladies out on a motor-car, is
innocent. It is really Cass, the villain, who isn’t
able to draw these fine distinctions. Still, Stan-
ford, having proof that Edwards has had some past
with the lady before she was married, naturally
concludes (at Cass’s instigation, Cass being, as
usual, after Edwards’s new girl—the highly
respectable daughter of the vicar this time) that
Edwards is the scoundrel who has broken up his
happy home ; so Stanford nearly kills the wrong
man. Meantime, Edwards is married on the
O.P. side, while Cass and Clare artd Cass’s
jackal plot in the centre of the stage. Miss
Holloway is the vicar’s daughter, Hesford
the vicar, and Miss Marshall some other member
of the family with not much to do. J. P.
O’Neill provides most of the “comic relief”;
he wears a Hebrew name and nose, a Scotch cap,
and the Irish brogue that was born with him, and
won’t wash off.

About a lady who is now in a better and
more tuneful world :
Antoinette Sterling is now counted among the silent
voices. When she toured Australia and Maoriland in
1893 she was already broken down as a singer. Twenty
years of over-energetic work as a boisterous balladist in
England had taken nearly all the charm out of a once
ruggedly-beautiful voice. Of the vocal art Madame Ster-
ling knew little or nothing, and her style of singing was
singularly eccentric. Her strong personality and her
“ sentimental ” temperament as a bawler of homely bal-
lads carried her through. She had plenty of soul, but no
silence. Sensible of the decay of her vocal powers, the
contralto, whose cuteness as a business woman compen-
sated for her lack of culture, ran the “ religious ” ticket
for all it was worth in Australia. At her concerts
she cleverly combined the ballad and the tract.
As a Quakeress the late Madame Sterling carried her
quaintness into her singing. She refused to be bound by
the ordinary rules of music. Once she was rehearsing
“The Lost Chord” with organist Weigand, at the
Sydney Town Hall. Everything seemed to be at sixes
and sevens. The organist could not keep pace with the
erratic singer : she was either half a bar ahead or a bar
behind. At last Weigand lost his temper, and, stopping
short, turned round to express himself with charac-
teristic candor. “What is dis for singing?” asked the
burly Belgian. “ I play dis song for Mess Adder
Crosi-lay. It was all right. She sing the music.
Now I know not what you sing. Everything
wrong. So wrong I cannot play. If you sing
so to-night it is imposseeble for me to play—impos-
seeble! ” Madame Sterling gasped, for breath. She
held the music defiantly in the face of the foreigner and
scorned him : “ You are an ignorant and an insolent man.
Let me tell you that I sing this song as the Lord taught
me to sing it. The Lord is my guide, mv teacher, and
my friend.” Wiegand, the unsentimental savage, smiled
and said in a tone of deep sarcasm: “Your pardon,
Madame. lam very sorry for me. I did not know that
your friend was a musician.”
When Antoinette Sterling toured Australia as a
“ musical missionary ” ten years ago she made a heap of
money out of the pious folk of Sydney, Melbourne and
Adelaide, who regard the ordinary concert as an irre-
ligious entertainment. Madame’s audiences were very
properly described as “ congregations.” In the day time
the “soulful” singer made a point of attending and
addressing the Women’s Christian Temperance Union
and meetings of the Social Purity Society. “ Daddy ”
Lohr and Tommy Hudson were her managers. It
was a very trying period for Lohr, who had
to sport the blue ribbon and a white tie. Madame
Sterling dressed her hair in the style of the
eighteenth century, and she didn't wear corsets, although
unusually plump and extensive in her proportions. On
the platform this despiser of conventionalities always
appeared in a loose flowing robe. When singing she
never held her music. The words of her songs were
printed in a richly bound book, not unlike a missal. With
this book in her hands, Antoinette was always able to
strike a “prayerful” attitude.

As the result of recent bold advt., Cirnoc,
who laughs at locksmiths, now provides the turn
at the Tivoli that you mustn’t miss. The other
night a Sydney sporting man undertook to chain
and handcuff him so effectually that he could not
release himself ; but 35 minutes after he had been
cast into his cabinet, he threw off the iron-
mongery and presently appeared, breathless, but
free. Since then, every night, somebody or other
has tried to chain him up tighter, but with the same
result so far. Dewar, the dog-star; Sims Reeves’
widow, and the others last mentioned here are
still on the bill.

Mel. B. Spurr still monologues at Queen’s
Hall, and of late the local musicians have found
out that he has a most uncommon pretty gift on
the piano. This discovery has raised him in their
estimation far above the rank of mummer, and
increased his audiences too.

Great World of London” with its railway
express murder and Salv’army doss-house

will be staged “ Saturday Night in London,”

** # m
With the aid of clever stage management,
the pantomime at Sydney Royal keeps an air of
perpetual youth ; new “ business ” in the comedy
line is introduced almost nightly, and the local
jokes are—-well, jokes. Another strong feature
of the show is the collection of pretty girls with
more or less leading parts—from Miss Olive
Lenton and Miss Elsie Moore down to the Ama-
zonian chorus. Seemingly, most of the clever
little children and fine-looking tall women in
Sydney have been pressed into the Royal ser-
vice ; you may see a ballet of girls averaging 3st.,
and then a march of others averaging 12st. or
thereabouts. This variety shows a praiseworthy
desire on the part of the management to please all
tastes.
In a plain prose way of speaking, these
are the last two performances of Musgrove’s
Shakspearean co. at Melb. Princess’s. The
“Dream” will be played in its entirety for the
last time to-night (Thursday), and to-morrow
the co. will farewell in sundry acts and scenes
from divers plays, whilst the orchestra, which is
a very handsome feature of the entertainment
contributes, overtures, marches, and all kinds of
music to the proceedings. In addition to the
extracts, from “ Twelfth Night,” “Dream,” and
“As You Like It,” there will be the balcony
scene from “Romeo and Juliet,” with Miss
Kerin as the yearning spinster and Miss Rignold
as the misguided youth who let her rush him to
his doom. It is not generally remarked (by such
as take their Bard medicinally) that gentle'Juliet
talks matrimony to her victim before he has
breathed a word on the subject. Most of
Shakspeare’s women do the same.
I** * #
An unexpected performance of “She
Stoops to Conquer,’’ for one consecutive night,
has been announced to happen at Melbourne
Princess’s next Saturday. Denny’s Tony Lump-
kin is the principal reason given for this revival
of Goldsmith’s comedy, which has not been staged
in Melbourne since the days of Brough and Bouci-
cault at the Bijou. On that occasion Mrs. Brough
played Kate Hardcastle to the Tony of Robert
Brough. On this occasion the leading ladies will
be unknown to fame, but Norman Partridge and
other male members of the Shakspearean co. will
assist Denny to put the performance through.
** * *
These are the final opportunities for wit-
nessing the picturesque Australodrama at Mel-
bourne Royal. Bland Holt has had four weeks’
hard wear out of the thunderstorm that slays the
wandering lunatic, and he now proposes to give
the angry heavens a rest. Real rain, provided by
the local Bard of Works, will fall no more after
Friday. The programme for Saturday is a re-
vival of “In London Town,” a drama composed
by Sims and Shirley.
** * *
“ Little Red Riding Hood,” at the Bijou,
continues to encounter the wolf on her way to the
new Federal capital, where the good fairies
come from. The attendance at last Satur-
day’s matinee was so large that a photo-
grapher was called in to flash-light the mul-
titude, and the majority of the multitude, being
juvenile, thought that they were getting fireworks
with their panto. Seems that Saturday was the
first anniversary of Tom Perman’s birth as a
Melbourne manager, wherefore his loyal forces
presented him with an impressive diamond ring
after the evening curtain had fallen. The event
they celebrated was Perman’s opening of the then
sorely neglected Gaiety, with a good enough
variety co. and a reduced scale of popular prices.
It is an interesting sign of the times in Melbourne
then that, although the old-established theatre
managers found business very hard to get, there
was a steady flow of customers for the new show
at a “ tray,” a “ tanner,” and a “ bob.”
** * *
At Her Majesty’s, the best items in “A
Country Girl” are still received with the pro-
verbial roar of applause, except on very warm
nights, when it is only half a roar. Also, the
periods between the best items are generally con-
sidered to be bright and lively. There are two
opinions about these matters, but the “Noes”
are in a large minority, and the ‘ ‘ Ayes ” argue
the point with enthusiasm. Every heated dis-
cussion is another advt. for the comedy, anyhow.
** * *
At the Opera House last week’s bill was
renewed on Saturday. Clarke and Hamilton are
there, likewise Winton, and the unremarkable
’cellist who endeavors to imitate Chirgwin more
or less. The biograph is still taking a Trip up
the Thames, and the muscles of Miss Vulcana’s
back deserve the same particular approbation as
before. Vulcana and her brother, Atlas, give a
quick and lively turn of its kind, and when their
heavy weights come down whump upon the floor
the question as to whether Pagel’s weights would
vhump any louder is one which doesn’t seem
worth raising. It is too heavy.
His run for the Senate as one of “the
Argus Four” killed “old John” M‘lntyre—he
died in a Melb. suburb on Monday night. Forty
years ago he was reckoned a goldfields Radical,
and as such Sandhurst sent him to Parliament in
’77. Later he moved on to Maldon, and sat for
that district for 15 years. In his younger days he
was one of the “ jolly-good-fellow ” type of poli-
ticians ; later he lived on his reputation as one
who knew a lot about the.mining laws; finally
he degenerated into a knight and a Tory, and all
that was left of the “Jock” MTntyre of old was
his personal popularity. The Bulletin’s picture
shows part of him in kilts. Years ago he was
Vic.’s best-known amateur Scotch-character
actor.
Bulletin, 14/l/’O4 (Sundry Shows), dis-
cussing “Twelfth Night,” refers to “The
spectacle of three drunks stretched upon the
floor, two of them ‘ blind speechless ’ whilst the
third man hath music in his soul,” etc. Mark
the difference between the days of indiscriminate
guzzle and these comparatively sober times.
D.C.L. Whisky _ taken moderately gives the
maximum of convivial enjoyment and the sweetest
strains of “music in the soul” without affecting
the legs of the imbiber. Shakspeare didn’t
christen D.C.L. the Whisky of the Wise, but
doubtless the Bard would have been the father
of that happy phrase had he lived three centuries
later.*
Drs. Freeman and Wallace’s new Institute in
Melbourne was opened last week simultaneously with
several cases of champagne—the other cases for medical
advice were probably waiting at the door. There was a
large and festive gathering present, and if the Drs. F. and
W. treat their patients as effectively as they treat
their guests they must indeed he benefactors of
humanity. The Institute, which has commodious and
comfortably appointed rooms, is in the heart of Melb., at
the corner of Elizabeth and Bourke Streets.*
“ As You Like It ” let it be
Tuned to “Twelfth Night” revelry:
Dreams of Summer, drowsy Mid,
Hover nigh thy shut eyelid.
Nonny ninny, stout or skinny
Every Womsel and his Winny
Wackling well the fairy grot
(This is baldest tommy-rot—
Bard-like, but devoid of reason)
Mourn the end of Shakspeare Season.
Sing its praises ere it closes;
Seasons come and seasons go;
Perfumes of the fading roses
Linger on the winds that blow—
Dainty fancies, songs and dances,
Called to mind by fickle chances,
Vex the brain,
Joy is pain
If it come not back again.
The Wild Cat Column.
During the next five years Australasia has
£21,500,000 of loans falling due. N.S. Wales must have
£1,000,000 next year, Victoria must get £4,000,000 in
1907, and little South Australia over £7,000,000 within
three years of that. Not one of these has a sinking fund
to meet Oohen with. This present threatened war has a
significance for Australia that no other ever had.
Following is the record of the 15 Australasian
fire insurance co.’s, as disclosed by the balance-sheets
issued, during the past year. There is a 16th co., but
the 46th, though nominally Australian, is in reality
English. The profits are less the amounts distributed as
bonuses to policy-holders. In many cases the dividends
are really much larger than are shown hereunder, past
profits having been transferred from reserves to capital.
Thus the Australian Mutual’s 16J per cent, is really worth
132 per cent, to the original shareholders, because, though
the capital is now £50,000, only £6250 was actually sub-
scribed, the balance having since been provided out of
undivided profits:

The net profits of the Australasian fire co.’s last
year were £261,306 ; in the year before £284,474, a
shrinkage for last year of £23,168. The worst streak of
the bad luck that sooner or later comes to every fire and
marine office fell to the North Queensland, which
made a loss of £4369 instead of, as in the previous
year, a profit of £11,629—a difference of £15,998. But
though profits generally were rather smaller, the reserves
were made to suffer, and not the dividends. The Mer-
cantile Mutual dividend went from 18£ per cent, to 20,
the United from 10 to 12£, the Victoria from 27 to 36, the
N. Zealand from 13| to 15, the National (M.L.) from 12J to
16, and the Tasmanian Mutual from 7 to 8. Only two were
reduced ; the NorthQueensland’sfellfromBtonothing,and
the Derwent and Tamar’s from 10 to 7£. One Maoriland
office (the N.Z.) made the largest profits and paid most in
dividends; another (the South British) made the largest
additions to profits ; while the only two co.’s which distri-
buted more in dividends than they earned were both
Victorian. But, on the other hand, a third Victorian
co., the Colonial Mutual, distributed the smallest pro-
portion of profits and put the largest to reserves.
Neither Queensland nor progressive Westralia has yet
decentralised the insurance business and established a
co. of its own (the North Queensland being now a N.S. W.
co.), though little Tasmania does very well with two.
According to the latest count, no less than 42
co.’s are competing for the fire-and-marine insurance
business of Sydney.
* * • *
Sydney Evening News the other day dis-
covered that N.S. Wales holds the world’s taxation
record, and thereupon severely lectured the Labor party
for “ talking glibly about fresh taxation.” The News
was surprised that more people didn’t know N.S. Wales’
position. The simple explanation is that the News’
figures are all wrong, and that N.S. Wales doesn’t hold
this position. Mulhall as a statistical authority is pro-
bably equal to the Evening News ; so it is as well to
bring the two together, and then people may take their
choice (the figures show taxes per head of population) :
News’ Mulhall’s
Figures. Figures.
N.S.W £3 6 0
Great Britain.. .. 3 0 6 £3 3 0
Victoria .. .. 2 15 9
France.. ... .. 212 0 3 14 0
Portugal .. .. 117 3 113 0
Spain .. .. .. 110 6 2 1 0
Denmark .. .. 18 3 1 15 0
Italy .. .. 1 6 6 214 0
Belgium *. .. 019 9 116 0
Greece .. .. .. 0 18 6 14 0
Germany .. .. 016 3 2 5 0
Russia .. .. .. 0 11 8 016 0
The News’ figures as to N.S.W. and Victoria are approxi-
mately correct. But Mulhall omits revenues from Crown
lands and forests, as well as from services, and this
greatly reduces the figures as to Germany, Russia, and
some other countries. Further, whereas in Australia the
State does almost the whole work of government, in
other countries an enormous amount is left to the local
bodies. Mulhall shows that several English cities spend in
taxes over £5 per head of the population annually, and
many European and American cities over £4 ; and even
then his figures do not cover the period of the recent great
municipal activity. Finally, there is the consideration
of the wealth of the people. Coghlan says that there is
in N.S.W. £265 worth of property per head of the popu-
lation. This is vastly ahead of the average of the
countries in the News’ list. Portugal (Mulhall’s figures
still) has only £B7, Russia only £6l. If Portugal’s
taxation increased according to its wealth, it would
pay £5 os. 7d. per head when it was as wealthy as N.S.
Wales. Spain, on the same basis, would pay £4 os. 6d.
Italy would pay £7 2s. Bd. In other words, N.S.W., in
proportion to its wealth, isn’t taxed half as heavily as
Italy, and not nearly as heavily as most other countries
in the world. The Labor party, if it’s honest, will
therefore continue to “ talk glibly about fresh taxa-
tion’’—enough fresh taxation to allow this country to
pay its way honestly, and wipe out some of the old debts,
and construct new works without leaning upon any
foreign support for the money.
m * * *
There are shadows over N.S.W. breweries, too.
Toohey’B, with last sales at IBs. 3d. when ’Change closed
for Xmas, are down to 16s ; Tooth’s, then at £1 Bs. 6d.,
are now at £1 6s. 3d. The outlook isn’t bright, either.
First, the co.’s have to pay on a prodigious load of capital
represented by nothing but names (goodwill, they call
it) ; then the retail trade is restless, and talks co-opera-
tion, and already the co.’s have had to go in for heavy-
trade discounts (15 per cent, on bulk beer) ; also, with
women in politics, even if prohibition isn’t probable, it is
a good deal more possible than it was, and more restric-
tive legislation is pretty certain in any case. There are,
in fact, numerous flies in what the horse reporters call
“ the amber.”

The Freehold Assets Realisation Co. (Melb.),
which, as mentioned in the B. 31/12/’O3, hasn’t lately been
able to buy up the deposit receipts of the defunct Free-
hold Investment and Banking 00. at the old alarming
sacrifice, proposed therefore to distribute some of the
money in hand pro rata, among deposit-receipt holders.
But, for some reason which doesn’t show up conspicuously-
on the surface, the co. has been advised that the court
would not sanction any such scheme ; so the old plan of
using the money to buy up such deposit receipts as are
offered at the lowest price will be continued.

Ore without sulphides in which the gold values
are finely dessimated throughout the gangue, is one
amenable to cyanidation or chlorination. Should this
ore contain sulphides it could not be so simply treated.
It is now recognised to be good practice to first concen-
trate out the sulphurets on Wilfley Tables and to treat the
tailings thus cleaned by the proper lixiviation process.
Should the ore be a telluride that would require previous
roasting, the tendency to “bead” or “shot” in the
roasting is surmounted by passing the leached pulp over
Wilfley tables, by which the values, too coarse to be
dissolved by the lixiviation process are concentrated,
together with any unroasted ore, sulphides or foreign
matter. The Austral Otis Engineering Company of
South Melbourne, are sole agents and manufacturers of
the Wilfley Concentrator.*

* * * *
British manufacturers have been beaten in an
open competition by an Australian engineering firm. We
have ascertained that the Mond Gas Co. of Staffordshire
have accepted the tender of Mephan Ferguson, of Mel-
bourne, for the supply of about 13 miles of Ferguson’s
Patent Lockingbar Steel Pipes, from 18 inches to 36 inches
diameter. The machinery for manufacturing these pipes
has all been made at Ferguson’s General Engineering
Works, Footscray, and shipped to Tipton, in Staffordshire,
where the branch works are being erected. The same
firm have also erected engineering and pipe works at
Wanganui, New Zealand, and have contracted to supply
the Wanganui Borough Council with about 20 miles of
Spiral Riveted Steel Pipes for the town water supply.*

Though for the previous half-year the dividend is shown
as at the rate of 6 per cent., 1 per cent, of it was dis-
tributed as a bonus. It got there just the same, but
with the difference that shareholders were not led to
expect it to continue. The last half-year’s results,
though they will not safely allow of any more than
5 per cent, dividend (for the past has shown that the
risks in this class of business are heavy), are nevertheless
much better than the average ; and the business gener-
ally shows good features. Twelve months ago the co.
had £6,647,149 of estates in its hands; it has now
£7,324,006. This ought to mean an increasing profit.
The shareholders’ funds as at the commencement of the
half-year (£90,000 capital and £11,044 reserves) earned
profits at the rate of £6 7s Bd. per cent., as compared
with £7 7s. 3d. for the previous half-year, and £6 7s. 3d.
for the half-year before that.

In the 12 months the bank has considerably strengthened
its position ; of all new liabilities to the public (£21,412)
no less than £12,551 is represented by cash and liquid
assets, and only £5BOO by advances, etc. The cash and
liquid assets represent altogether 36 per cent, of the total
liabilities to the public, which is an unusually large
proportion. Almost necessarily, as a result of maintain-
ing this high proportion of liquid reserves, profits are
comparatively low ; on shareholders’ total funds (capital
and reserves), as at the commencement of the half-year,
the recent earnings have been :

• • * *
Cobar has q[uite recovered from the shock which
followed the failure of the Joseph syndicate to purchase
the big mine. The energy being displayed by the Reid-
Longworth party, who are now the sole possessors of the
property, offers the best assurance which the public can
have that there is to be a progressive policy which will
mean steady prosperity. The Joseph negotiations have
had a good effect. The elaborate reports, plans, and
estimates prepared by the visiting experts (reports which
placed Great Oobar in the front rank of the big copper
mines of the world) really opened the eyes of the
ex-tributors to the magnitude of the proposition with
which they had been toying. Preparations are being made
for working the mine on a much more extensive scale,
and it ought not to be long before the output
is doubled. The old 80-ton furnaces are to be discarded
in favor of furnaces which will treat up to 400 tons each
per day. Mr. Blakemore has satisfied himself that all
economic considerations are on the side of the large fur-
naces. Each furnace is to be provided with an indepen-
dent set of blowers, thus ensuring even pressures which
can be easily regulated, and preventing the waste in-
separable from the long air-main system. It is under-
stood that matte-refining at Lithgow is to be discon-
tinued, a converter plant being installed at Oobar. The
electrolytic plant for separating the precious metals from
the copper is likely to be retained at Lithgow. The ex-
tensive alterations in contemplation are not to be effected
in a day, but the end of this year should see them well
advanced towards completion. Water is the essential
requirement which gives the most concern. It is pro-
posed to make a very large excavation in the flat below
the site where the original reverb, furnaces were located.
Lake Blakemore, as it will inevitably be called, will be an
institution of great practical utility, and a thing of beauty
and a joy for ever to the sun-scorched, sand-scored,
sulphur-saturated Cobarites.
• * * *
North Cobar co. is taking a rest. It spent some
thousands of pounds in sinking a 500 ft. shaft on the
wrong side of the lode formation. The cap of the lode is
as conspicuous on this block as are the plumes in a
matinee hat, and the slight dip which exists is to the
east. The co. started its shaft a long distance west of
the outcrop, so that every foot sunk took it so much
further away from where the lode might be found. As
an example of misdirected effort this shaft has no equal
in N.S.W. Next time shareholders feel in the call-paying
humor they are advised to sink a new shaft as far east as
the present shaft is west of the lode-ridge.
* * * *
Local report has it that Cobar Gold Mines
(Fort Bourke) as a free milling and cyaniding proposition
will be worked out by the end of the year. There is
abundance of ore below 200 feet richer in gold than that
in the upper workings, but there is so much oxide and
carbonate of copper in it as to render it unworkable by
present processes, and so much silica as to make direct
smelting an impossibility ; concentration by water is
equally out of the question. No effort has been made to
test the sulphide zone, and there does not seem to be
any present intention to do so. Co. must have abundance
of funds with which to do exploratory and experimental
work, but advices from London indicate the utmost in-
difference as to the future. There may be a lot of method
in this assumed indifference, as the smaller shareholders
may find out to their cost later on. So far Cobar Gold
Mines Co. has existed solely for the benefit of Cobar
people, and their gains out of it have been considerable.
Shareholders have been disappointed where they ought to
have been pleased. On actual returns the mine has
proved to be a better one than it was represented to be
by the original vendors and the experts who reported on
it. The initial mistakes of supposing that a reasonable
percentage of gold could be saved by amalgamation alone,
and of locating the plant so as to save, say, 3d. per ton in
handling at one stage, and necessitating an unnecessary
cost of, say, Is. per ton for handling at the other stages,
are to a large extent responsible for the seeming failure.
The Bulletin asserts that the Fort Bourke mine, even
now, is one of great probabilities, and the smaller share-
holders would do well to combine and arrange for an
investigation by impartial men into the causes of failure
and the chances of success.
* * * •
Cobar Chesney shares are on the up-grade in
Melbourne market, consequent on the acquisition of a
large parcel of forfeiteds by a syndicate. There can be
no other explanation. The mine is no better to-day than
it has been any time these five years past. There has
been another report—John Warren being the lucky fee-
collector this time—which gives no further information
than has been contained in o Jier reports. The problem
presented is no nearer solution. It never will be solved
while the directors are content with the policy of paying
fat fees three times a year to get a repetition of an oft-
told tale. The position is this : ore in sight, hundreds of
thousands of tons ; average value at ruling prices of
metals, about £2 15s. per ton ; ore too siliceous for direct
smelting, and for that purpose iron-flux must be brought
to the mine or the ore must be sent to where flux is abun-
dant ; ore specially suited to concentration by water; abun-
dance of waterin mine; large concentrating plant erected;
if this plant be perfected and worked, shall theconcentrates
be smelted on the spot, or elsewhere, or be sold ? The
latter is really the problem which has to be determined.
An experimental plant, to cost at the outside £2OOO, and
a man of commonsense to run it, would settle the
question. Ohesney people surely have had enough of
the fads and prejudices of mechanical engineers and
metallurgists. Of the original concentrating' plant all
that remains exactly as it was in the beginning is the
housing ; and, as for the metallurgical business, each
expert has been at special pains to proclaim the man
before him an ass ; and so it will go on ad infinitum. It
is amusing to be told by puff paragraphists that Chesney
has £21,000 callable capital. Nothing is ever said about
the co. being in debt over £25,000. However, that would
be trifling if commonsense were allowed to prevail.
» » * *
The syndicate that purchased the heap of cu-
priferous tailings from the Chesney co. is said to be meet-
ing with great success. The exact process of treatment
has not been divulged, but it is understood that the
copper is leached out with sulphuric acid after which the
tailings are cyanided in the usual way. This successful
work ought to be a cue to the mineowners who are con-
fronted with the copper difficulty.
* * » *
The Broken Hill co.’s have made but very little
way yet in the matter of zinc-production ; after seeing
what the chemical processes are capable of (four machines
of this pattern are installed at Block 14), the Sulphide
Corporation has declined to adopt them and has decided
to rather more than double the magnetic-concentrating
plant instead. This plant gets out from 69 to 71 per cent,
of the zinc in the middlings and tailings ; and of this 70
per cent, less than 50 per cent, is saved by the zinc-
smelting or distilling process at Cockle Creek. The final
extraction doesn’t therefore pan out at more than about
a third of the zinc. And yet the Sulphide Co. has decided
that even this is better than the chemical processes.
* * * *
The Broken Hill companies are squeezing the
very fat Silverton Tramway 00. again, threatening once
more that if it does not reduce freights they will build aline
for themselves through Pinnacles and Thackaringa to
connect with the S. Australian system at Cockburn. On
its actual paid-up capital the Tram Co. pays divs. of
anything between 32 and 81 per cent, (these figures re-
present the two extremes), and the mining co.’s .contri-
bute by far the greater part of this. Naturally, when
things are not too prosperous in the silver-lead mining
business, the mining co.’s drop into the habit of thinking
that something a little more modest ought to satisfy the
carrying co. The threat has, however, been made so often
hitherto, and nothing has come of it, that the co. probably
isn’t very much alarmed now, especially as it knows that
the mining co.’s, as a rule, are by no means flush of funds.
Under the Tramway Co.’s Act, the State can only take
over the line on the basis of past profits ; and that means
a figure at which the Department will not look at the
business. Once it gets there, the deadweight of a Private
Enterprise scheme of this sort takes a lot of shifting.

» * * •
Lucknow was evidently considered a name to con-
jure with in London when the promoters of a co. to work a
mine near the Canoblas, at Orange, many miles west of
Lucknow and in a different belt of country, named it the
Great Lucknow Consols. The mine was described as one
of fabulous richness, but The Bulletin dubbed it “ the
Great Lucknow Joke.” It has panned out just as was
prophesied, or worse. Results have been so bad that
workmen have not been paid. The defenceless manager
was sued the other day, and ordered to pay up within
three weeks or go to gaol for three months. The lot of
the manager of a non-paying mine owned by an English
co. is not enviable. London directors are prompt and
prolific in suggestions which attest their ignor-
ance, and correspondingly remiss in making necessary
remittances of money. The Great Lucknow Consols is
the mine in which some prominent railway officials were
said to be interested.
* * * *
The W.A. December gold yield (202,0700 z.)
brings up the total for 1903 to 2,436,3100 z. This record
should easily be beaten this year, as very much machinery
is being erected, which should easily bring up the
monthly total to 220,0000 z. bullion. The word “ bullion’
must not be overlooked ; a law has been passed that all
returns must be given in fine gold in future, and the
true position of such mines as the Perseverance and
Boulder will now be seen. The actual figures will show a
decrease, but the value should for a while show a 10
per cent, monthly increase in another three months or so.

As here mentioned as likely, the Ivanhoe ha 3
declared a ss. div. for Jan., meaning a distribution of
£50,000. This brings the W.A. Jan. total to £189,539.
The Ivanhoe co.’s new 30-drill air-compressor has now
got to work, and the main shaft will be sunk much more
vigorously. A good many surface improvements have
been made recently, and it is very questionable if any
other mine in W.A. has lower costs—to the great credit
of manager Nicholson.
* • • •
Burbank’s Birthday Gift (W.A.), after a run of
years, has closed down its mill indefinitely. It has
been a strangely managed concern. There used to be
no less than five main shafts, with five winding engines,
which meant five times as many engine drivers and
stokers as there need have been. At first the mill was
hand-fed, but eventually rock-breakers and automatic
feeders were installed, which entailed the erection of
another hoist, another engine and more boilers, and
another batch of drivers and stokers. Had the mine
not been a marvellous one, it must have been
closed down long ago.. Developments were never
very forward, as can be seen from the fact that the
deepest workings are only 500 ft. Some three years
ago the mill was doubled (from 30 to 60-head), but there
has never been sufficient ore to keep more than 30-head
going. Recently the control of the mine passed into the
hands of Bewick, Moreing and Co. ; but what they think
they are to make out of it, it is hard to tell. The leases
were all pegged out with the view of getting length with-
out breadth. For this reason, although the co. only
holds 71 acres, they are comprised in eight leases, because
the mining laws of W.A. insist that a prospector must peg
out double the length of fh» width of his 51001.-; therefore.
if a man wants length, he pegs out a lot of small areas In
preference to one big one. The Gift is now in the
unenviable position of having no deep levels, and, unless
Bewick, Moreing and 00. can buy up the Lady Robinson
and other holdings on the east to take in the underlay,
the prospects of the co. are black
« * * *
Kalgoorlie Amalgamated is looking sick; in
spite of a lot of boring, nothing of any value has been
located. Recently the bore on the True Blue West was
carried from 900 ft. to 1000 ft. without payable result. The
extra 100 ft. must have cost a nice bit, as it practically
meant reboring the old hole, which was abandoned
months ago. The Brown Hill people must know to a
nicety where the underlie of this lode is, and whether it
is any good, and manager Salmon would have done better
for his shareholders by consulting 8., M. and Co. than by
sinking a bore-hole. Although, by the way, a directors’
circular recently arrived from London stating that the
co. had still £30,000 in hand, the wages remittance is
nearly always behind the mark. As a result the men are
discontented and the staff disorganised.

White Feather Main Reefs, Kanowna, has put
on an extra spurt, and last month treated no less than
1738 tons for 13370 z. The seventeenth div of 6d. will
shortly be announced, bringing up the total since Feb.,
’9B, when co. was reconstructed, to £OB,OOO. For an
outside show it is one of the best; the manager (N.
Harper) has done well to keep things going so smoothly
The main shaft is down 750 ft., and passed through the
main reef at 480 ft. Two winzes with air winches have
been sunk 110 ft. below this level, and both the main and
east reefs have been proved to a depth of 860 ft., vertical.
For some reason or other the Bhares are quoted at a
rubbish price. Owing to a misunderstanding of a state-
ment made by the shift boss, it was recently published in
the press that the mine was to shut down, but this is far
from being the case, as the mine is looking better than
usual
• * * *
Great Boulder, Kalgoorlie, has started sinking
anew main shaft, 14ft.x5ft., on lease 102, north of the
Ivanhoe. This ground was recently tested by diamond
drill, and proved to contain the Ivanhoe East lode, and
should also have the Boulder main lode further east;
though this has not yet been proved. The north end of
this property has lain fallow so far, and is covered with
miners’ humpies, and, on the surface, looks a most un-
promising proposition. In fact, anyone walking over the
surface of the “Golden Belt” mines to-day would won-
der what instinct ever induced prospector Pearce to peg
out such apparently hopeless country. There is
absolutely nothing on the surface to indicate the wealth
below. Great Boulder will probably greatly increase its
output this year, and may again occupy the position it
held for years, of premier producer of the fields,
* * * *
The Kalgoorlie mines, during November, con-
sumed 18,164,000 gallons of water from the Forrest
scheme. The chief consumer was the Golden Horseshoe,
which took 3,082,000, and treated 11,380 tons of ore ;
while its neighbor, the Ivanhoe, only used 2,445,000ga1.
while treating 11,966 tons of ore. The great consumption
on the Horseshoe is accounted for by its enormous
boiler power. The co. has nine Babcock and Wilcox and
12 other boilers, a great deal more than appears neces-
sary ; but, for some reason or’ other, it takes the lot to
keep things going. This accounts to some extent for the
very high costs on this mine, which are very much above
those of any pf the other big mines, and about 50 p.c.
above those of the Ivanhoe. No great progress is being
made with the extra 50 head of stamps, and a long time
must elapse before they are going.

Golden Link’s Eclipse lease (W.A.) December
return w r as 13580 z. from 4655 tons, an average of s‘Bdwt.
only. The two previous returns were 16330 z. from
3968 tons, and 24520 z. from 2646 tons. Manager Haig’s
excuse is that, “having stoped up to within 30ft. of the
surface, the material, consisting of mullock and quartzite,
is so soft that the whole weight rests on the timbers, and
must be stoped out at once to prevent falls and conse-
quent accidents. All oxidised ore above sdwt. will be
put through the mill.” So far, no stoping has been
done below 100 ft. The main shaft is well below 300 ft.,
but sinking is suspended till stoping at 100 ft. is com-
pleted and workings made safe.
* • * •
Hainault, Kalgoorlie, is issuing its 20,000 re-
serve shares, in order to pay off liabilities. This will
bring the capital to £130,000. Of the new issue under-
writers take 11;000 on condition that they get a two
years’ option at par over the remaining 9000. This seems
to be a good deal for the co., as it gets 18s. cash down
for 11,000 shares, or 3s. above market price. This means
£lO,OOO cash, which will pay off all debts, and free cur-
rent profits for payment of divs. The profits amount to
£B5O monthly, or £10,200 yearly. After deducting de-
preciation and London charges, this will barely provide a
div. of 7 per cent., which would make the scrip dear
even at present price. It is hard to see how the position
can be improved without increasing the treatment plant,
and this again involves suspension of divs. to provide the
needful.
* * * *
Golden Ridge (W.A.) mill is being duplicated,
a cyanide plant is on the way, and very shortly returns
should be doubled. J. Bull abandoned this mine, which
is now owned by a local syndicate.

From Charters Towers: Band of Hope is still
sinking straight to cut the Victory Reef, which is ex-
pected in another 100 feet. Present blocks are unlikely
to maintain dividends ; it is intended to prospect several
formations cut in the straight. Bonnie Dundee is under
exemption and awaiting reconstruction. Brilliant P.C.
fossicked out a ljoz. crushing (3d. div.) ; the end of this
great mine is near. Brilliant Freehold has recon-
structed in 150,000 shares of ss. Columbia has aban-
doned crosscutting and driving, and will drill 500 ft.
from the bottom of the vertical shaft, at present 1972 ft.
down. Queen Cross still looks good. It also is to bore
1000 ft. from bottom of present shaft East Queen Cross
follows suit with a bore 1000 ft. from surface.—Vesuvius is
under exemption for reconstruction ; it struck a pro-
mising indication on the eve of hanging up This co.
also may try boring. Queen Central is driving on both
854 ft. and 954 ft. formations. East, on the former, they
have picked up a small vein of fair stone ; west, they are
stoping on Bin. of good stone ; and at 954 ft. they are
driving in a promising formation. Queen Central Block
put up what is claimed as an Australian sinking record
of 140 ft. in four weeks. The shaft is down 530 ft. An-
other Queen Cross pup (the Extended) has 480 ft. of
sinking to its credit. The Ruby looks more promising ;
and the United has picked up a reef of 20in., going well
over an ounce. Three other of the Ruby litter, the
Extended, Macdonald, and the South, are pushing down
shafts. This poi tion of the field Is padstly a gamble at
long odds. LonHohjrSiydfoejMsilfi local holders in Vic-
tory are squabbling over' a reconstruction scheme;
London holders want the head office. The state of the
exemption market under Minister Browne should not be
forgotten in this connection. Vic. and Queen is holding
its own—paid £44,750 in divs. for the year—and will push
on exploring untried country. Vic. and Caledonia
Blocks have neglected exploration. Generally, in fact, too
much ore has been gouged out lately, and there may be
several slumps in consequence. /

INFORMATIVE SMALL BOY : “ They’ve passed that
Bill up in Parliament to stop us blokes
smokin’ ciggerettes.”
HIS PAL : “Does they want us all to smoke
pipes?"
Pearl of wisdom from a Sunday-sehool
scholar: “ Sins of omission are those which we
ought to have committed and have forgotten to
commit.”
• • * t
Woman is fast usurping all man’s little
prerogatives. A Port Germein (S.A.) lady has
been badly injured in an otherwise trifling trap-
accident, by a wound from a corkscrew that she
carried in her pocket.
An observing traveller on the two great
oities of Australia:
The Melbourne man hustles in an artificially alleviated
dust-hole. He is alert, energetic, and the slave of his
wife.
The Sydney man dawdles in a Paradise, with the sum-
mer climate of a damp Hades. He is nonchalant, tired,
and the slave of some other man’s wife.

• • » •
Examination of the body of the woman
doctor, who disappeared mysteriously in London,
showed traces of morphine in the stomach —weeks
after death. .Quite a number of wives are reckon-
ing that if things get a little worse, there is con-
solation in the fact that morphine doesn’t, like
arsenic, linger in the stomach—as evidence after
death. Just as well to remind them that that is a
fallacy.

“ H.” : The wages of sin for a woman
nowadays is her photo, in the papers and a full
report of the divorce proceedings. She seems
satisfied with the remuneration, too. Met a lady
the other day with a sack of photographs. She is
shortly to figure in court on her husband’s appli-
cation for a rule nisi. Asked what on earth all
the pictures were for (there were five or six of
them, all depicting her in her best ball dress), and
was told that they were “ for the papers.”
“ X.8.T.” writes :
Have just met a man who wants me to get him a job
up country. At present he is driving an Alexandria tram
at Ba. a day. I asked his reason for wanting to throw up
a steady Government job. “It’s my health,” he said;
“I’m frightened." He went on to say that he had just
seen a mate of his burled at Rookwood—the third driver
within his knowledge that had succumbed to consumption
of the throat within the last six months. His own throat
had been “ tickling ’’ ever since, and he was afraid. The
man “in front,” he averred, can’t last. His eyes go, or
he contracts disease from the constant rush of dirt and
dust. The men want glass fronts to the trams, but the
Commissioners object. One of the M’s.L. A. who nurses
the railway vote might interest himself to find out if
there has been any extra mortality among tram-drivers
from throat or lung consumption.

Bent’s blackleg engine-drivers, whose ex-
perience of a locomotive dates from the strike,
have been putting up records. Last
month, on the Beach Forest line, a
train allowed the Westing-
house to waste, and the train
on an incline began to run
back. At last fireman and
guard crawled along the
train and put down the
truck brakes. A couple
of women are in the
hospital as the result of
another accident on the
Colac line. The War-
burton train daily-
supplies amusement
for onlookers; it
stops outside or in-
side the station, but
seldom at the sta-
tion platform. The
Maryborough dis-
trict reports a
couple of boilers
burnt and fire-
boxes spoiled.
The Department
in the old days
either sacked a
man for this
offence or else put
him back firing for
life, but blacklegs
can do pretty well
anything without
penalty.
* •
The Outtrim(Gipps-
land) miners have now
been 12 months on strike
(or locked out). They have lost about
, £120,000 in wages, and have lived on £14,000
strike pay; have fought three actions at law,
and have an appeal to the Privy Council pend-
ing. The companies declare they are satisfied
with their new arrangements. They send away
930 tons of coal weekly now, in place of 2800 last
year. Surely an Arbitration Act would prove
cheaper than that—to owners and men.

“ Viv.” writes from S’Africa
The moment the first Chinese coolie sets foot on
S African soil will be the beginning of the end of British
rule there, for the anti-Chinese sentiment is deep and
widespread.
* * * *
Nice-looking girl, seeking employment in
Sydney as a governess, answered an advt. in
person. The lady of the home asked the usual
questions, appeared satisfied, and added, “ I shall
expect you to be up in the morning and attend to
my husband’s breakfast, as I never rise before
10; cut his ham and pour out his coffee,
but do not sit down.” The governess: “Your
advt. was for a matriculated governess, not fora
housemaid-waitress, and I know that in a fort-
night I should be sitting down, and Mr.
would be cutting the ham, so that discretion is the
better part of valor.” The interview closed.

Remarks :
All roads lead to rum.
Olanliness is next to godliness. (Scotch proverb.)
The man who “ goes to the ant ” is likely to get bitten.
Honesty is the second-best policy. The best is a large,
paid-up one in a good office.
It is sacrilege to hit a policeman.
In the Ruination Stakes Wine wins in a decanter;
Woman in a hansom cab.
• • • •
Prohibition is leading to some strange
results in M.L. At Ashburton it has made
to rise in its majesty the “Brotherhood of
Beer.” Members are united in a sort of
club, subscribe equally to the funds, and
indulge in the beer that they buy as fancy
prompts—no charge being made for drinks.
. There is no attempt made to deal with the pro-
blem of unequal thirsts, except a provision that
any member who takes more than a fair share is
liable to expulsion, after two warnings, from the
club. From expulsion there is no redemption; a
man must gnash his parched tongue for ever in
the outer beerlessness.
• • • •
“Petramos”on an Adelaide institution!
One glass of “ pinky,” the cheap colonial wine sold in
Adelaide, would be sufficient to knock many an efficient
boozer oat, but some people are hardened to it, and
drink it by the bottle. It is sold at a beer-bottle-full for
6d. Why it is called “ pinky ” is as great a mystery as why
people drink it; but “pinky” it is and “pinky” is in
great demand. The local S’norton has a column, “ The
Pinky Club,” in which the doings of well known pinky
drinkers are noted.
* • • •
Whilst the Australian is having such a bad
time of it in S. Africa just now, we don't seem to
hear of the Englishman being so intensely unpopu-
lar, though he did take some small part in the late
war. It has been said that the English officer dis-
played more tact in little affairs of arson than the
Australian, and no doubt it is being remembered.
The English gold-lac-er would say, “ Haw, pardon
me, awf’ly sowwy,” as he daintily applied a lucifer
to the kerosened farm-house. But the Australian
put an axe through the family piano with an ex-
hortation to “Gallagher” to “ let her go.”
* * * *
No doubt, as the D.T. found out one dull
news day, liquor is an incitement to many
tragedies, but some of the worst crimes in
Australia have been committed where the available
evidence—as in the Gatton case—indicated strict
sobriety. Generally, when drink has been demon-
strated to be the evil influence, the circumstances
denoted _ low-class, poisonously concocted liquor.
Good drink rarely promotes crime.
• • • *
What Sydney News calls the “Voice of
the People ” attributes increased immorality to
early-closing. The inference is that to work
employees the whole 24 hours would conduce to
perfect religion and complete morality.
• • • *
A Tambaroora (N.S.W.) Chinaman six
months ago neatly trimmed off the branches of
two contiguous saplings, and erected a crossbar
between them. Two months later he was observed
sitting beneath the structure contemplating it
with critical eye. Three weeks later a rope
dangled from the crossbar, and then five days later
again John dangled at the end of the rope
—the final touch to his careful preparations.

• * *
There were five copies of Queen Victoria’^
“My Life in the Highlands” in gaol.
It was wrongly catalogued “My Life in the-
Islands,” author’s name omitted ; and many
prisoners, thinking it was some scarlet'
account of pirate daring in Southern Seas,,
placed it on their library-list. The prisoner-
librarian, who was sour-tempered and vicious,,
brought it along at once. As a prisoner
could only change his book twice weekly, once be-
got the “ Life ” he was locked up with it for three
days. It was once found in the cell of a prisoner -
who tried to commit suicide.

A wholly scandalous reply was that given
by the Public Service Board of N.S.W. to the
query of a teacher as to his status in the
Superannuation Fund. “Teacher” had sub-
scribed to the Fund for 30 years; it is now
bankrupt, largely owing to the swindle of throw-
ing as a burden on its income the provision of
pensions for civil servants retrenched for so-called
economy’s sake. The existing Government —the
bankrupt state of the Fund was more due to G.
H. Reid than to anyone—did something to protect
the rights of contributors, and passed an Act
dealing with the subject in October last—a par-
ticularly complicated Act. “Teacher” wished
to know how this Act affected his position. The
Public Service Board “declined to furnish legal
opinions.” Emphatically, though, it is the
Board’s business to explain to “ Teacher,” who
ha 3 been paying to a State pension-fund for 30
years, “how he stands”; no private life assur-
ance co. could, without serious loss of credit,
refuse such an explanation.

THE OIOADAS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The shrill cicadas’ ceaseless cry
With keen, persistent, poignant steel
Stabs through the heart of day—and I
Sharp in my heart the stabbing feel.
The great sun spills his golden blood
And bathes me in his beams—
But I walk always through a flood
Of dark and evil dreams.
I had a passion in the night
That burned me like a flame ;
Mine eyes detest the fair sunlight,
For my heart is black with shame.
The darkness caught me ; I was thrilled
With rapture—l was free 1
The chalice of the night was filled
With wine of ecstasy.
The Dawn came in her robe of blue.
With clear eyes virginal
And scornful scarlet lips. I knew
That she, too, knew it all.
The shrill cicadas’ ceaseless cry
In cynic chorus goes ;
And I will hear it till I die,
And after that—God knows.
V. J. DALKY.

and Troubles
The law makes no effort to instruct its
policemen in the rudiments of knowledge as to
mental disease (and the policeman, anyhow, is
kept too busy at non-police work to make any
response to such an effort possible on his part).
Hence some avoidable tragedies. A man and his
wife walked arm-in-arm into the Newtown Police
Court the other day.
hushed tones they informed the constable in charge
that they were surrounded by enemies, and that wicked
people at the Glebe had connected their house at New-
town with a wire, by means of which they were able to
turn on nasty smells whenever they liked.'
The police could not grapple with the problem,
which was, evidently, a dangerous form of griev-
ance dementia. _ The people were not drunk;
they were not using bad language or conducting
themselves in a disorderly way; so they
were allowed to wander out again. Three days
afterwards the man murdered the woman and
then committed suicide. Their blood might have
been saved if the police had been able to recognise
the danger signals.
• • • •
Several morbid Sydney females took their
lunch-baskets down to Circular Quay to wait the
arrival of the Wallaroo victims. “ How many? ”
one asked the first man ashore. “ Four dead and
three injured,” he replied. “Is that all!” she
said disappointedly. Recalls the heroine of the
Mount Rennie infamy, who also remarked “Is
that all ? ” when the cabman was sentenced to 14
years and 50 lashes.
“C.”: Re the recent Bundaberg Kanaka
riot, a Sydney daily, recalling a more serious
Mackay scrimmage of 20 years ago, says that on
that occasion the white men were armed with
revolvers. Untrue. The whites had nothing
more deadly than stirrup-irons to oppose to the
bombardment of empty bottles ; but, even at that,
they drove the niggers off the racecourse into the
surrounding cane and laid out over 40 of them.
Had the gentle Kanaka won the day, Australia
would have been horrified at the fate of the women
and children. The S.M. Herald correspondent
at the time wrote with apparent sympathy for the
savages—“ Several of the islanders exhibit marks
of the most brutal treatment. ” As if any treat-
ment could have been too brutual for a horde of
savages who had planned a wholesale massacre of
defenceless white women and children.
* • • •
Ifc . was originally proposed to style the
President of the United States “ His Highness, ”
or “His Excellency,” and “Protector of the Liber-
ties of the People.” Washington, who was a
monarchist at heart, favored the Dutch style,
“High Mightiness.” The more democratic mem-
bers of Congress, however, laughed the proposal
out of court by suggesting that the Vice-President
should be styled “His Superfluous Excellency.”
Which suggests a. new title for Australian
Governors.
THE SPOILS OF WAR.
“ Your last mistress gives you an excellent
character.”
“My word she had to, or give me another week's

The “butcher’s bill” of the Broken Hill
mines was a shade lighter than the average last
year. In the past 10 years 173 men have been
killed, while the total for 1903 alone was 16.
Apart from the unhealthiness of the silver-lead
mine, the chances of sudden death there are
greater than in a coal mine. On the average,
2.24 in every 1000 N.S.W. coal miners are killed
every year; in the Broken Hill mines, 2.85 in
1000. .There are at least four Broken Hill victims
whose bodies have never been reached—they lie
under confused masses of ore and timber and
mullock in different parts of the mines ; exactly
where nobody knows, for in most cases they fell
with the moving mass when it broke through into
a lower working.

down dropped the dog, dead. The spectacle of
the butcher standing over the dead dog, with the
ticket in his hand, on which I read, “ Small goods
fresh daily,” was too much. I left the spot hur-
riedly. Too many events, with shadows attached,
were happening for my liking.

Most squatters have taken advantage of
N.S.W. Government’s relaxation of the regula-
tions dealing with shearers’ accommodation. The
huts are mostly as bad as ever.

“ Demos ” : Englishmen’s method of
softening the lot of condemned criminals by good
feeding (8., 24/12/’O3) isn’t up to the American
method of supplying cheering fluids ad lib. during
the last 24 hours. Have been told by gaolers that
every subject of electrocution is too drunk to
know what’s happening to him. It is the inten-
tion of the prison officials to avoid “scenes.”
When a reporter says “ condemned was so over-
come that he required assistance to mount the
scaffold, ” it generally means overcome with rum.

• • • •
First N.S.W. Arbitration Court (constitu-
ted under a voluntary Act which Mr. Barton
fathered) struck a little trouble in a coal-mining
dispute. The Metropolitan Colliery and its em-
ployees were at variance; the case went to the
court with the consent of both parties, and the
award was against the men. There was keen
dissatisfaction, but the award was loyally obeyed.
• * • *
The Tasmanian centennial celebrations
were postponed in September, because of the
smallpox in Launceston. By present arrangement
they are to be held in February ; but the public
resolutely refuses to donate, and the celebrations
are likely to frost. Hobart only wakes up in the
summer—just sufficiently to fleece the recuper-
ating tourists ; and celebrations of any sort that
interfere with that serious business are not wel-
comed with any particular ardor.

Sydney D.T. issues an alarmed cry over
the proposals to restrict newspaper power in
politics. With a fair press such proposals would
never be made, but, obviously, democracy cannot
allow itself to be paralysed by the unfairness of a
press monopoly. The ultimate outcome of present
press tactics will probably be a Government news-
paper. The public must have news (news is as
much necessary, almost, as bread) and it has a
right to claim that it shall have the news fully
and fairly. If the daily newspapers won’t supply
that, the State may have to step in as their com-
petitor, with journals strictly confined to news.
* * * *
The Six Hatters, Petriana, Stelling and
like affairs suggest that the mare’s nest industry
might be systematised by the newspapers. The
Tory papers should frankly join together and
home-manufacture in every damning detail their
own awful political scandals, instead of, as
now, importing some atoms of fact around which
to spin a romance. The mare’s boudoir that is
left to be constructed on the spur of the moment
is almost sure to prove defective. A special cast-
steel she-horse’s lair, constructed by experts,
would serve better. A proper enormity foundry,
manned by efficient liars, could turn out
political scandals and outrages by the gross ; and
when the S.M.H. or D.T. or Argus wanted one
in an emergency it could drop down and take its
pick from a large stock.
“ H.P.” : Another old historical record
knocked endways. Said that Edward Henty was
not the first settler in Portland (Vic.). Stated
that old “Paul the Whaler” was there some con-
siderable time before November 19th, 1834 —the
date of Henty’s landing. Urged further that
“Old Paul” was only a common man (though
a good whaler), hence his claims to distinction
were pushed overboard by the more aristocratic
Henty family. Do any hoarders of ancient
records know aught of this ?
“Boree”: Tarraville, in South Gipps-
land, is enjoying a brief season of remembrance as
the birthplace of a star. Distance has apparently
lent enchantment to her native village in the mind
of Miss Ada Crossley. M.A.P., a year or so ago,
published an interview with the singer wherein
her home was vaguely and gracefully alluded to in
terms that suggested an old baronial hall, some-
what after the pattern of the “stately homes of
England,” standing beautifully in a proud park.
She recounted how her earliest grief had been
losing her pet porcelain doll by dropping it from
the balcony. When this touching statement had
soaked in, Gippsland, I should think, smiled a
large, slow smile. The picturesque was omitted
when “the Tarra” —as the township is called—
was founded, and there isn’t a baronial hall within
50 miles. There is one street, where the houses
are dropping to pieces across the footpath, sheets
of galvanized iron fall into the gutter, and nobody
marks them. There is but one balcony in the
and it belongs to a pub. which had legends
attached, and which the godly passed on the other
side with prayer and fasting. There is also a
little shop, leaning out now across the footpath,
and long closed. The whole shop-front has
become loosened at the bottom, and it waves in
the wind in a somewhat ghostly fashion. Over
the door shaky letters proclaim “E. W. Crossley,
tinsmith.” There isn’t any balcony. Perhaps
this isn’t the correct shrine, but I always regarded
it as such, and worshipped there. If I have
worshipped in vain I will go and slay that shop in
cold blood.

The evil ol good intentions ! Place :
Queen’ll Bridge, Melbourne. Time, 2 p.m. Tram
bound for South Melbourne. Old gent, drops
walking-stick. Another tram passes towards city.
Young man, idling on bridge, rushes to help;
picks stick up and rushes eagerly after the wrong
tram. Meanwhile, old gent, stops car, alights,
sees young man running after other car with his
stick. Frantically chases young man. Young
man catches up to back-platform of car; finds no
one there to receive stick, drops it on platform.
Walks back with an “ Alone, I did it! ” air. Meets
irate old gent., who feels that he could not do
justice to the occasion without risk of dropping
dead. Blasts young man with one look. Last
writer saw was old man chasing tram, and young
man looking after him with a bruised expression.

“G.R.” writes re the declining birth-rate :
It should not be forgotten that the death-rate of
children is much lower than it was under former insani-
tary conditions, and a high birth-rate is not needed to
make up the deficiency that would exist if the former
proportion died. Also, a much larger number of persons
now live beyond the child-bearing age than formerly, and
the rate must be lower on this account, in which respect
the following figures for the returns for England and
Wales are very instructive :
1851—Deaths per thousand, 22 Births per thousand, 34"2
1901— „ „ „ 16'9 „ „ „ 28-5
Thus, although the births show a falling-off of 57, the
net decline in the rate of increase is less than two-thirds
of one per cent. Through much blind stumbling we are
learning the foolishness of a large production, much of
which would have to be sacrificed. We are getting
beyond the doctrine that a woman’s mission is to produce
and rear a large number of human targets for the rifle-
balls of militarv-crazed rulers.
NO BUSINESS DONE.
“ Got any 'bacca, Bill? ”
“ Yes, and I've got sense, too."
The registrar of births, deaths, and
marriages in a certain Melbourne suburb has a
peculiarity which sometimes upsets the blushing
bride. As soon 'as the business is done, he hands
the marriage lines to the wife, and remarks with
ceremonial pride: “This makes you an honest
woman.” Some day, an irritated lady will rise
up and tell him that she never stole anything in
her life, and-then her newly-acquired Bill will hit
the Registrar, and he will be sorry for his joke.
EVICTED EVE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
From richly-clustering fruits and fragrant flowers,
From murmuring fountains lulling her to dreams,
Eternity of happy sunlit hours
In velvet meadows fed by rippling streams.
Alas 1 in one fell moment to be hurled —
Poor, foolish Eve ! in all her wayward charms,
into the desert of this stern old world,
Where shrubs have thorns to scratch her soft white arms*
And roses canker-worms to eat their hearts,
And apples codlin grubs inside the core.
Adown the cheek the pearly tear drop starts,
But in a little while she smiles once more,
Drying her tears forgetful of her woes,
She finds her compensation in her clothes.
VIC.
MIMOSA.
WHY?
[For Thb Bulletin.]
When Effie flirts with other men
And I show anger clearly,
She says “ You are a silly goose,
You know I love you dearly ! ”
And holds her lips up to be kissed,
That satisfies me—nearly.
When Effie flirts with other men
And I show no resentment,
But talk and laugh with other girls
In calm and sweet contentment;
I get no smile—l get no kiss—
I get a look of scorn,
And Effie says she wishes that
She never had been born.
