A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, January 19,1904.
My Dear Arint.—
By the Moldavia which brought our new Gov.-
Gen. to Australia arrived also Mrs. and Miss
Venables Kirk —sister and niece of Lady Rawson.
They are going on to Moss Vale, and will stay
three months with our Excellencies.
By the Ormuz came Commander and Mrs.
Lacey. He went to Melbourne to take his man-
o’-war Mutine to Hobart. Mrs. Lacey, who is Aus-
tralian born, is with her sister, Mrs. Hotham, of
Double Bay—an infant son is also of the party.
What a lot of the Sacred College the Ormuz
brought along this trip. Three R.C. prelates and
some Anglican clerics. Captain Coad himself
looks like a High Church cleric when in his
shore clothes, and in uniform like an academic
chaplain.
Minister .1. G. Ward, of Maoriland, with his
dame and only daughter, will be in Sydney in a
few weeks time en route to the postal conference
at Rome. Daughter Eileen, who promised very
well in the matter of looks—-brunette pallor, dark
eyes, and enviable etceteras—in her long-haired,
three-quarter-frocked stage, is said to possess an
uncommonly good contralto voice. A voice, that
deserves to be written with a capital S. or C., is
as valuable as a sheep-station afterlO good seasons.
Ada Crossley will bank something like £15,000
from her Australian tour. All her audiences con-
sider that they have had good .value for their coin.
The contralto, who is in the West, just missed, on
arrival, the 105 in the shade temperature that
would have made her welcome warmer than that
which the Perthites accorded her.
A Sydneysider, returned from M.L., says he
never stumbled over so many titles during a ten
years’ residence in England as he did during a six
months’ stay in M.L. Providence has been very
good to M.L. in the matter of hot mud and
scenery, and these attract the titled folk. My
tourist ran up against the Chermsides (who
Excellency Queensland), Sir Donald and
Dame Robertson (who do something British
at Mysore); a Right Rev., who is also a lord,
and who smoked a short briar pipe and slept
with loud emphasis after dinner. Then there
was a peeress accompanied by a Lady Somebody,
who travelled with their own physician and
servants, including a Chinese “boy.” The last
wore his national dress, wide skirts, sandals, two
yards of pigtail, and used to stand behind his
owners with hands folded inside his wide sleeves,
and his narrow eyes fixed and unwinking—just
like those of a tuatara lizard. These persons
were all doing the Hot Lakes, or slithering down
the Rimutaka incline, or fording the famous
Wanganui River. Which reminds me T must ask
the M.L. Tourist Bureau if the Wanganui is
fordable.
Further, my informant took care never to be m
the Bureau’s offices within an hour after passen-
gers ex the English mail steamers had landed in
Wellington for fear he should learn to say
“ Haw.” For within that hour the place would
be full of tony British women talking at the tops
of their voices to men who responded with
“Haw.” No wonder M.L. is becoming a
rendezvous for Australian Society desirous of
meeting, or just looking at, English ditto. Or,
for that matter, desirous of bringing its speaking
voice up to fashionable English currency. Poetess
Barrett Browning writes somewhere of “voices
low with fashion, not with feeling,” bub now if
you want to appear as those who form the
inner circle of the Preserved Nectarines, vou
must not be low—in the matter of speech.
A beauty-lover of this city who fell in love
with the portraits of the Princess of Pless,
determined to see the original when in London.
Succeeded. Walked close to her coming out of
church. Disappointed. She is very florid, not to
say red-faced, very blue-eyed and fairly tall, and
she was barely outside the “sacred portals”
before she “began to talk and laugh in a loud,
screaming, cockatoo voice.” Worst of it is that
when plain Miss Brown talks like a cockatoo
people say she doesn’t know any better. When
the gilded tourists that infest M.L.’s hot mud
beds make themselves heard like a sohool of
kookaburras, the plain Browns listening reveren-
tially and say, “How. natural and unaffected.”
And they say truly. It is to-day fashionable to be
natural, just as it was fashionable for Lady Mary
Carlyle and her friends (“Monsieur Beaucaire”)
to be unnatural.
What a lot of men profess to be shocked at the
transparent, or pneumonia, blouse for street wear !
I have met only one man who frankly owns to an
admiration for it. All the letters I get abusing
the fashion are from men. White violets! Here’s
one of them:—
The newest caper of giddy block-walkers seems to. be a
tight-fitting under blouse or chemisette of creamy white
just the tint the wearer’s bosom is supposed to be.
Over this she wears a lace-topped blouse—preferably of
black —and as she knows her skin isn’t visible she wears
the lace very decollete, but the casual observer isn’t so
well informed and naturally concludes the damsel is air-
ing her hide, at least as. much of it as the necessity for
keeping her dress on her shoulders will allow.
Christinas, the time of the open heart and re-
laxed purse, is,past, but now is always an acceptable
time to. help a worthy cause. Women’s Hospital,
Crown-street, Surry Hills, is the worthiest cause
for charity that I know of for the moment. If
you give it a sovereign the Government will add
another, for it has only private charity and the
Government subsidy of “same as you give’-’ for
support. I am sure some one of our brilliant
amateur dramatic clubs will see a way to help
with a performance an institution that succors
poor women at a time of greatest need. This
hospital is still in the same cramped quarters-
some two-storied terrace houses. The most minute
calculation of economy in space must be prac-
tised to allow the reception of 30 patients —
some of them surgical cases. There is a little
cottage at the back where the laundrying is
done, and where some of the waiting patients sleep.
The rent for these houses runs to £4O a month, and
the absence of a hot-water service makes the gas
bills very heavy. So great is the pressure on the
limited space that expectant mothers are given
temporary beds in the ironing and bath rooms of
the cottage. District nursing, averaging 40
cases a month, is carried on in the large and
very poor area around the hospital. The
nurses tell painful stories of the miserable
homes they visit—often not a vestige of furniture —
and the mother lying on a mattress or a heap of
rags. And these are, they say, not necessarily
the homes of drunkards. Naturally no payment
can be expected by the hospital from such
patients. Nor do those admitted, except in rare
instances, contribute.
Any bacilli entering the Women’s Hospital
would get a sterilising reception. No wonder it
has never had a septic case. Even the cotton
wool, which is already supposed to arrive microbe-
less, is tied in sheets and boiled, and once a pot of
vaseline is opened it is put away until it can be
re-sterilised. Miss M'Leod, the matron, looks a
real vital spark, and is thin as a willow wand.
She is the originator of many inventions in
hospital appliances: for instance a baby’s bath—
an oilcloth well hung on four slender iron legs—
and a bath and bed combination, as well as
wonderful arrangements and improvements in the
matter of baby’s kit. One of the committee of
the Melbourne Women’s Hospital (Mrs. George
Godfrey), who visited the institution lately, took
back with her to her own gorgeous hospital
several samples of the inventions. In the mean-
time, remember that he or she who gives quickly
gives twice, and money is very urgently needed.
Committee of hospital has been waiting and
hoping that the inscrutable ways of Providence
would give it the School of Industry building, and
so enable it to benefit more poor humanity. The
property belongs to Mr. Mark Poy, and is under
offer to the committee for £12,000, or the very
reasonable rental of £3OO a year.
Someone signing with the alias of “ Amor ”
(why not “ Calf ” ?) writes to know why girls close
their eyes when men kiss them. All the damsels
that “Amor” (or “Calf”) thus salutes in Sydney,
Hobart, and Melbourne ( what a naughty vagrant
calf it is !) shut their eyes. He wants a scientific
explanation. Note the vanity of the frisker—he
wants to put it under the label of science. As if
the general appearance of the Australian youth
wasn’t reason enough. I take it that “Amor’s”
numerous girl shuts her eyes so that she shall not
see the kisser. However, “Amor” may console
himself with the thought that if women don’t love
him, God will.
“Pomona loves the orchard and Ceres loves the
vine, and Pales loves the straw-built shed warm
with the breath of kine.” I started mumbling
that as I prowled amongst the stacks of wheat
and barley (beshrew me but the room was full of
the fragrance rare of new-mown harvests !) that
with other cereals are doing the Harvest Home
act at Sydney Exchange. Wool is also there,
and butter and tinned fruits, so the quote is not
so very inapplicable. At least, if there isn’t actual
butter there’s a table showing that in 1902 we
had 23,600 boxes of it and in 1903 there are
125,000 available. So there oughtn’t to be any
dry bread in this year 1904. Pine pumpkins,
which please cows but vex the soul of man when
appearing too often as a vegetable, are also present,
and there are photographs of some of the huge
grain and wool-growing stations in Dubbo and
other districts. Owners must have been meek
and, therefore, blessed, so much of the earth have
they inherited. Some scrolls are on the walls,
bearing singular statements in semi-biblical
phraseology : “The harvest is plenteous, but the
laborers are few and “Nature hath done her
share, do you yours.” What is the moral?
The Exchange shouldn’t scroll in parables.
Opening of State Parliament as a Society Show
has been waning dull and duller for the last few
years. Federation put the extinguisher on its
brightness, and now if a woman had to make her
choice between visiting her grandmother’s grave
and attending opening of Parliament she would
unhesitatingly choose the first. State Legislature
inet last Tuesday and there was the usual Governor
and military demonstration. For the rest—those
who are not out of town visited their grand-
mothers’ graves.
Other night at “Mama’s New Husband,” th'e
funny American piece at the Palace, Charles
Stine, in trying to stroke down the terrible Mrs,
“Pearly” Brood and make her purr on his
shoulder (and give him a cheque), did a bit of
Julius Knight that made the audience choke with
laughter. “ Turn to me ; turn to me.” The out-
stretched arms, the raised chin, and the tremulous
voice, low and vibrant with passion : no wonder
the lady turned and purred—and chequed.
Most fashionable winter wear in London and
Paris is velvet, so I suppose we shall put some of
the same strokeable material into our apology for
a winter. The velvet of to-day—the to-day of
the northern hemisphere—is not the thick, heavy
stuff of some years ago. It has all the softness
and richness of the velvet of other days, but it is
known as chiffon velvet, and is light and graceful
as panne, has a deep pile with a slight rib.
Popular colors for day wear are black, mole-
mouse, and brown, but for evening there
are rose, sapphire, cream, lilac, or any
other gay shade. Little Emily Soldene, in
chronicling a very be-titled wedding, mentions
that every second woman wore velvet—one was
in sapphire shade. And a Sydney girl who has
got married in London sends me a half-mile of
panel portrait of herself with the assurance that
the gown is oyster-white velvet. In the picture
it looks a cross between crepe de chine and satin,
and the train swirls down into the foreground and
lies around in soft folds and curves that have a
sleepy, rather fascinating effect. All I remember
of “ Moths,” the Ouida book, is that the beautiful
Countess wears a white velvet dress on the night
that her noble Sergius Alexikoff (or some such
name) waddies her. Olga Nethersole played the
Countess part in her M.L. tour, and as she wasn’t
in white velvet I didn’t think she did it well.
But the velvet of the old days must be as gunny-
saoking compared with the ethereal velvet de luxe
of the present. I hope every woman who has
good credit at her draper’s will wear velvet dur-
ing our alleged winter, and so add to the gaiety of
our nation.
Some weeks ago the cableliar told us of the
elopement of a Princess of the House of Bourbon
—Princess Alice of Schonburg-Waldenburg—with
her coachman. Now the yarn is contradicted,
and the Princess given a clean bill of moral health.
The coil in which the Princess involved herself by
assuming an innocent disguise reads like the plot
in a Family Herald story. She had considerable
reasons for leaving her husband’s home, and, until
she could get the Vatican consent to a separation,
she went to live on the Riviera, assuming the
name of her maid, Materni, in order to avoid
public attention and annoyance to her relatives.
Recognised by old acquaintances whilst walking
in the neighborhood, it was inferred and noised
abroad that she was in reality the mistress of the
husband of the maid whose name she bore. It is
a dull day on the Riviera when its dwellers can’t
chase an aristocratic scandal —which mostly proves
phantom.
Lady Hamilton, once an Excellency of Tas-
mania, has read a paper on the Uganda railway
before the London Austral Salon. A paper on so
gritty a topic would want punctuation with tea
and cream cakes. Lady Brassey has been pre-
sented in Paris with a gold medal by Mdlle. Irma
Dreyfus, on behalf of the Alliance Framjais.
Wonder does Irma still keep her 18-inch waist and
40-inch bust ?
Mayoress Lees’ first reception held in her room
at the Town Hall last week didn’t glitter quite so
much as a comet. It had a butt-end-of-the-
season appearance. Mrs. Lees will hold a series
of how-do-you-do’s at Easter. By that time
society will have got its resurrection aspect.
Miss Sylvia Blackston’s farewell concert at
Sydney Towrf Hall was not the financial success
it would undoubtedly have been if held at any
time other than that between Xmas and Easter.
No artist can fill or half fill the Town Hall in this
period. Concerts are out of season and most
concert-goers out of town. Yet I see that Miss
Maud Dalrymple is going to have her farewell
song on Feb. 17th. Miss Blackston, who was in
fine voice, gave her hearers a charming programme.
She leaves for London shortly. By the same
vessel go Mrs. Burdekin and her daughter and
son.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent
“ The top-notchers of the official world had to
hurry over, their morning chop on Monday to
reach Spencer-street in time for Lady Northcote’s
coming. Mesdames Turner, M'Eacharn, and
Hutton toed the line mostly in morning rig and
an eager spirit. The Mayoress toyed with a great
cluster of red roses and appeared to be rehearsing,
sotto-voce, her impromptu words of greeting.
Treasurer Turner, flushed and painfully well
coated, bent his spectacled eyes on the railway
lines and accounted as nought the clatter around.
He was plainly uncomfortable. Commissioner
Tate, limping on a stick (a knee fracture), was
spruce and sweet and well content with things till
he saw the Northcote party dumped down at the
shabby end of Spencer-street. Irate at the bad
management, he had to join the reception mob,
herding fast from the prepared place of the
palms.
“ The Northcote dame is a slight lady with the
sort of pallid complexion that comes from India.
She’s not imposing or dashing, but she has an
expressive face and a charming manner. People
who know her describe her as a ‘ gifted conver-
sationalist.’ She arrived in Melbourne wearing
pale-blue, with filmy cream vest, hat with
feathers (some black, some white), and pearl ear-
drops. Her friend, Lady Margaret Knowles, is
not a fashion-plate beauty, and at first, with her
unclassic nose and cool, Tussore silk gown, obser-
vers pronounced her less impressive than the usual
Government House girl, but she holds trump-
cards in her fine eyes and creamy skin, and like
her friend, the Gov.-Generaless, looks brisk and
bright. Anyway, she is careless of local criticism,
as she is only booked for two months in the
Commonwealth, and ‘ wishes to see something
of all the States ’ in that time.
“Captain Balmain, A.D.C. to the Northcotes,
was at the station to meet his liege-lady, and
looked pretty tired and tanned after his work in
taking over from the Federal Government
cutlery, linen, etc., to the value of £17,000. In
Sydney he’ll have to inventory household stuff
worth £20,000.
“ In the printed lists of Gov. Northcote’s staff
no mention is yet made of ex-Aide Nevill,
although, up to a few weeks ago, it was under-
stood and given out that the amiable lordling was
to be on the staff. Society chroniclers are so
beholden to Dicky for his amiability and obliging
little ways that they’d be more than sorry to hear
that he wasn’t going to stay.
“The Western and some of this State’s papers
fell into the error of imagining that Mayoress
M'Eacharn travelled as far as Perth as a sort of
honorary body-guard to the departing Lady
Tennyson. The truth was that she was clinging
to her only son to the last. He was on his way
to Eton.
“The M'Eaeharns mean to be in front with the
entertainment of the Northcotes. They have a
feast listed for Friday, the night after Lord North-
cote’s arrival. The Northcotes have, anyhow,
reason to be grateful that they are spared until
Friday. They might have been paraded on
Thursday.
“Of all the affecting farewells vouchsafed to
‘the big-hearted contralto,’ as the well-informed
(ahem !) portion of the press persists in dubbing
Ada, she owns that the Tennyson brought her
quickest to her favorite tears. Theex-Governor-in-
Chief can wear a lugubrious visage at times, and
it must have been turned on when he gripped her
palm in the strenuous manner and murmured,
‘Good-bye, child, and God bless you!’ Child!
One hears Malvolio gurgling delightfully over
‘Fellow. Not Malvolio! not Steward! not
Friend even, but Fellow ‘! I know an advanced
spinster with wrinkles and whitening locks who
is still addressed as ‘ Kid ’by a bachelor rake who
was once nearly spoons on her. Kid ! That is her
romance—it never fails to produce a thrill. It
has a subtle, sweet enchantment to her ageing
ears.
“Such an ingress of globe-trotters to this corner
of the Commonwealth ! And such a return of
gadding Victorians ! Mrs. N. Thornley and her
daughters, rich with rustle and full of ideas of
bustling Toorak to a smarter pace; the Misses
Jackson, daughters of the oculist, with ideas
ditto and improved looks; and many others.
Mullen’s was as a Babel, the other day, when a
lot of these gadding ones converged at the Book
Exchange counter and talked fortissimo, as if their
voices had still to fight for ascendancy with the
waves. News is brought along of residential
Victorians in Mayfair—of their swagger ways—
their parties—and the nobby names on their
visiting lists. ‘We live in London,’ is quoted of
one dame. ‘Australia means existence merely.’
Rather it means the means of existence only.
“The little brown boys who were petted and
dangled by the big white girls of Melbourne
when the Japanese squadron lay out in Hob-
son’s Bay last year, have been explaining,
in letters to the said girls, with mis-spelt
enthusiasm their joyous anticipations of a war,
and expressing in acrobatic syntax their fervent
faith in a grand Jap triumph ahead. One of the
Hashidate middies looks forward to the conflict
because ‘it makes so good a sensation after nice
hospitality in your kind Australia.’ Another
specimen dismisses the war subject with an
epistolary shrug of the shoulders as much as to
say, ‘ Well, the idiots brought the trouble on
themselves,’ and goes on to declare his undying
love of Miss Nora Kerin. ‘I love Kerin,’ he
states, ‘but I have not told her so, and never met
her. Her photograph (sweet Titania !) is with
me. I will never depart from it. Please send
me a big photograph of nice Kerin.’
“When Miss Edith Newton marries Mr. Lang-
loh Want, of Sydney, it will make the third match
from the ‘Are You aMason?’co. There seems
just now to be a slump in the comic-opera ranks,
as far as wealthy matches go. One of the recent
C.O. gilded engagements, by the way, doesn’t
exactly gallop to matrimony.
“ The Pursey Chirnsides are not the only people
hereabouts adopting the customs of the English
squire. The S. F. .Staughtops, at ‘Staughton
Vale’ (Western district), supplied cakes and ale
to the tenantry, don’t you know, and other country
yokels this Xmas tide. They say that the said
tenantry pull their forelocks quite prettily when
the squire notices them.
“Latest news is the engagement of Mr. C. J.
Roberts’ eldest daughter to one of England’s
landed gentry—an elderly Lincolnshire potentate.
Miss Roberts has just passed through Melbourne
on her way home to Sydney.
“The late Madame Antoinette Sterling is
remembered in Melbourne for many things, but
for none more by her intimate friends than the
pattern she gave them of her divided under-skirts.
The stout singer was an enthusiast over the
rational dress theory, then just showing its head
timidly above Fashion’s horizon. I recollect an
afternoon-tea rally of Antoinette Sterling’s friends,
at Menzies, when she hoisted her skirts and
exhibited her ‘bloomers.’ As trousers they were
entirely satisfactory. The trouble with Madame
in her later years was that she allowed her trousers
to get on her voice.
“ There are no Turkish baths at present in Mel-
bourne. It will be months before the new baths
go up. In the meantime the electric wash at a
Oollins-street establishment is largely attended
as the best substitute.
“Garden-curator Guilfoyle is gleeful over a
novel collection of brilliant flowering shrubs
known to the aesthetic as * Flower of the Morn-
ing,’ but to the botanic erudite as ‘ Mesembry-
anthemums,’ in which there seems a useless
redundancy of the ‘ m’s.’ Over 50 specimens of
the bloom were landed a while ago as a gift from
the Lord of the Scilly Isles (sounds all right), and
their riot of color intoxicates the flower-loving
sense. They are hardy and enduring, and, unlike
most showy flowers, triumph on hillsides and rear
lustily above the hard bed of a rockery in the face
of the sun and the winds with equal unconcern.
Just now the Gardens are resplendent with blooms.
“ The pert and very interesting society charmer,
whose appearance on the boards in the front
ranks of the chorus was bo suddenly made and so
unceremoniously ended, is now taking a demure
look on the stage from the circle.
“ Governor Le Hunte, of the State alongside,
led the demonstration at the Orossley concert in
Adelaide. He punctuated the applause by stand-
ing up and making several profound bows to the
singer.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
C. C. Kingston has gone to the M.L.
sulphur baths in search of health improvement.
Sulphur? It sounds ominous.
Profit Dowie is said to want to buy an
island for Zion. Goat Island, in Sydney Harbor,
should just fill the bill for his disciples, and Shark
Island for the Profit himself.
Mr. Willis, N.S.W. M.P., threatens, ’tis
said, to abandon local politics and enter the
House of Commons. Well, the threat is to
England, not to Australia.
Gladstone Dowie (age 38), just arrived in
the Commonwealth, “has never yet,” according
to Father Elijah, “kissed a girl in his life.” It
is a freak family, that of the Zionider.
Premier Jenkins (S.A.) was, until New
Year’s Day, only a naturalised British subject in
his own State, but now the limited Australian
citizenship which he acquired some years ago will,
under the Commonwealth Act, be made general.
Tom Mann, who did sterling work for the
Victorian Labor party as organiser prior to the
late elections, talks of giving up politics for agri-
culture, but an effort is now being made to secure
him as general secretary of the Vic. Political
Labor Council.
Mayor Cohen, of Adelaide, expects to be
put on an equality with the gaud Mayors of
Sydney and Melbourne at the next distribution of
Imperial baubles. Governor-General Tennyson
practically promised something of the sort in a
public speech the other day.
Q. lawyers don’t, as a rule, aspire to the
Federal Parliament. Only three tried at the
recent elections. One, barrister Groom, had a
walk-over for Darling Downs ; and two others,
Mac Paterson and Morse, were beaten by Dr.
Culpin for the Brisbane seat.
A Methodist clergyman prayed over the
body of John Farrell, laid in the R.C. portion of
the Rookwood cemetery —Rev. W. H. Beale, an
ex-president of the Methodist Conference, who
attended the funeral as a friend. No R.C. priest
attended at the grave-side—through a misunder-
standing, it is explained, which was remedied, as
far as possible, later on.
OUR POLITICAL CAT-FISH—SENATOR NEILD.
Dr. Todd is about to add to his other
avocations that of author. He will compile a
guide book for Sydney Zoo. He is already coroner,
medical doctor, and barrister. In accordance
with the best coronial traditions, his book will
probably be light and flippant.
There is hope yet of seeing Johnaynes
fighting his secession fight behind a bayonet or a
blunderbuss. “ Men follow true leaders to
death,” he writes in the D.T. Still the men
whom John is addressing may reflect that any
fool can lead them into that mess.
The Bulletin is soon going to lose an old
political “property.” Cornelius Job Ham, who
has served to point many a moral, will not seek
re-election to the Yic. House of Landords, of
whose incapacity to represent the people he has
been a conspicuous example for 20 years.
Thomas Edwin Hill, of Melbourne, pro-
bably possesses the champion pair of brittle legs
in the Commonwealth. He has had his legs
broken in all eight times—four times to each leg.
They began breaking when he was seven years of
age, and they are still at it and doing well.
Chief Justice Darley, of New South Wales,
is due to resu me duty early in next term. There
are rumors, however, that, in consequence of the
death of his son-in-law (leaving his daughter with
an infant son and an £BO,OOO estate) Mr. Darley
will not remain in office but will return to
England, after winding up his own affairs, to look
after the interests of his daughter and grandson.
Labor member Holman, some years ago,
called to see John Earrell, and found him busy.
“Oh, I won't disturb you.” “ Don’t moind. Go
on ; I was only writing a leader.” To Mr. Hol-
man, then, a leader suggested awe. He said so,
and stated how he imagined that to write a leader
was a task of serious responsibility. “ Well, no,”
said Farrell cheerfully, “if a mon knows a good
deal about a matter, or if he knows nothing at all
about it, it’s all the same, it comes pretty easy.”
Icy Irvine’s threatened retirement from
the Vic. Premiership, for financial reasons, was a
“gag” which his legal brethren in the House
refused to take seriously. The fact is that
barrister Irvine has become richer rather than
poorer through dropping into his present billet.
Representative Jimmy Hutchison, the new
Labor member for Hindmarsh district, spent his
Christmas week in camp with the Scottish corps
at Strathalbyn. He is a full sergeant in the
kilted regiment, and probably, like Kingston, he
will decline promotion beyond that democratic
rank.
Artist Julian Ashton went over to see
Henry Parkes just after little Cobden was born.
“Cobden? Cobden? What an extraordinary
name,” he said to the old man ; “ you’ll have ail
the boys at school calling him Cobby.” “Well,”
replied Parkes, “ I intend, you know, to call the
next one Julian.”
Ex-Senator John Downer is tipped for the
next vacancy on the S.A. Supreme Court Bench,
expected as a result of the retirement of Judge
Boucaut. Downer still feels rather hurt because
he neither got a Federal Judgeship nor the
Ministerial office in the Senate vacated by Mr.
Justice O’Connor.
The Times snubs Seddon for his protest
against the introduction of coolies to the Rand,
alleging that “ the Transvaal knows its own mind
best.” That’s what Kruger thought, but Seddon
backed up Britain in insisting that outsiders were
better fitted to administer the laws. Now Seddon
is beginning to feel what Kruger felt.
John Douglas, whose retirement from the
position of Resident at Thursday Island was
recently mentioned, has returned to duty, though
now 76 years old. He sat in N.S.W. Leg.
Assembly for Darling Downs before Queensland
was a separate State ; he held portfolios in several
Queensland Ministries. He has been Resident at
Thursday Island since 1885.
James Dalgarno, who retires from the
N.S.W. Deputy Postmaster-Generalship, and G.
P. Unwin, who succeeds him, are Sydney natives,
and representatives of old families. The former
is the son of Captain Dalgarno, who was contem-
porary of Capt. Dibbs, father of G. R. and T. A.
The other’s father was a solicitor, in large prac-
tice, in Sydney, and one of the earliest directors
of the A.M.P. Society.
A. W. Hendry, the blind manager
of the S.A. Industrial School for the Blind,
has just had a trip to Europe, and seems
to have got as much pleasure out of it as
any man who can see. Hendry, very keen
in business himself, admired most in that
line the progress of a totally-blind Bir-
mingham merchant, who began 35 years
ago with a capital of one sovereign, and is
now in the first flight. In other directions,
about the most remarkable thing he struck
was a blind man’s reporting of the whole
of Chamberlain’s Birmingham speech in
Braille shorthand.
The dignity that doth hedge in a
Premier came pretty nigh to the breaking
point on a recent Ministerial junketing.
Premier See had for one of his party a
member who never before had the honor
of accompanying the head of the Govern-
ment, and who now rose to the occasion,
and shadowed his boss affectionately. It
was “ Sir John ” on every side on the first
day. On the second day familiarity re-
moved some of the awe, and the member
joked with the Premier whom he now
called “See old man.” The third day
saw the persistent M.P. dig the Pri minis-
terial ribs and address their owner as
“John.” The fourth day brought the
climax, when the familiarised legislator
publicly alluded to the Premier as “My
friend Jack.” Then something broke.
Capt. Purefoy and Brigadier Finn,
at the recent Highland gathering, dis-
claimed being Scotch, but boasted of Scotch
wives. Old Sydney will remember the big,
bluff, beer-drinking Sergeant Dalton, who
was in charge of the guard at Government
House under several vice-regal personages
up to the time of Loftus. He claimed to
be a Londoner though he had a brogue
you could cut with an axe. When asked
how he came by it he always said: “I
served fifteen years in an Irish regiment
and I married an Irish wife.” Dalton
was all through the Crimea and charged
with all his brogue at Balaclava.
“G.H.”: I like Australian artist
Percy Spence’s big men, who stick their
arms akimbo and spread their legs out all
over the landscape. Judging by the
physiques that all the other local black-
and-white artists give their subjects, one
would think (wrongly) that we Australs
were a low-chested, consumptive lot of
beetles, about 4ft. high. I don’t know
what size Peroy Spence is, but notice that
pretty well all the other Australian artists draw
men, physically, replicas of themselves. And
that’s not fair to Australia.

At recent Federal elections a woman,
asked to vote for Vida Goldstein, retorted, “ Vote
for her ? Not me. A person who could stand
and get photo’d with only her stays on, will get
no vote from me.” The old dame had in her mind
a well-known corset advt., with which she mixed
up the Senate candidate.
In the Tasmanian polling, the once
popular Hartnoll was badly beaten by Protec-
tionist Storrer, a Launceston undertaker—the
man who, it was alleged, “ broke up ” Melba on
her memorable visit by allowing her to open the
carriage door, on their arrival at the Brisbane
hotel, with her own hands !
It is a slow process Australianising the
imported cleric. R.C. Archbishop Kelly, in an
address to a lot of Sydney children, recently,
asked them what it was they caught fish with,
and when he answered himself by saying “a
little fly” there was a general snigger, and the
intellectual value of an archbishop slumped sud-
denly in several children’s minds.
It would appear that Miller, of the Anglo-
Australian prospecting party, perished quite
within coo-ee of help. W. Carr-Boyd, old “ Pot-
jostler,” with a party, was among the ranges, and
the scene of the disaster was within 30 miles of
the water discovered by John Forrest in 1874.
Miller’s earlier illness had probably a lot to do
with his succumbing in such country.
A popular Victorian M.L. A. owns a “desir-
able brick villa ” with half an acre of garden in
front. Embowered among the foliage are three or
four plaster statues which the politician has care-
fully painted in “ real life ” colors. One statue of
Innocence specially attracts; the lady wears
auburn hair, an extra-vivid blush on her cheek,
and a pale blue gown with green frills.
The Maoriland Baron Von Mueller medal
for original research in natural science referring
especially to Australasia, has been awarded to
Mr. A. W. Howitt, of Victoria, for good work in
ethnology, geology, and botany extending over 40
years. Mr. Howitt is a son of William and Mary
Howitt, writers, and was leader of a party sent
out to search for the explorers Burke and Wills.
Lieutenant-Colonel James Macarthur
Onslow, of —haw —Camden Park, who has dodged
from one constituency to another in a vain effort
to get into Parliament, has evidently given the
game best. The Deformer intended, if defeated
for the Gwydir, to fight John Kidd at Camden,
but his ignominious licking at the hands of a
Labor candidate has disheartened him, and he’s
not going to be a candidate any more.
Shiels’ home is one of the least conven-
tional in Melbourne. His children, three
girls and a boy, are being brought up on
free-and-easy lines. After posing in the
limelight of politics, Shiels goes home and
becomes a boy again with his children.
They talk to him just as they talk to
one another, express their opinions
about religion, life and death, and don’*
hesitate to tell IShiels the parent and
playmate what they think about Shiels
the politician—which, if they are wise
children, is not much.
Ex-Justice Foster, pensioner of
N.S.W. Supreme Court, still struggles
with bad health. He might on the

stuck to his farm on-the Upper Hunter
and let law and late' hours ,in Parlia-
ment slide. When Mr. Fo tir can.e to
Australia it was with the hope of pur-
suing an outdoor life as farmer and
grazier in a sunny land, but a few
seasons went against Him and he turned
the ploughshare into the legal shears.
Easy-going “ jaunty-Johnny ” Gordon,
the new S.A. Judge, was expected to follow the
line of least resistance; wherefore he staggered
the bar when, in his very first case, after Senator
’Siah Symon had triumphantly produced a ruling
of Senior-Justice Boucaut’s, he smiled, and re-
marked : “But I don’t agree with'Mr. Justice
Boucaut, and therefore shan’t follow his ruling.
I rule against you. Please proceed with the evi-
dence.”
A dreary wreck drifted into Adelaide
“Benevolent” about ten years ago, and,'being an
expert tailor, was set to work, a'nd giVeri half-a-
crown a week as pocket money for special ser-
vices. The other week he flabbergasted the
authorities by stating that he was going home to
Germany, and showing them a huge belt worn
under his clothes, and containing every half-crown
he’d received for the last ten years ! The money,
over 400 pieces and weighing nearly 131 b., was so
blackened that the shipping people refused to
accept it until it was tested.
An English correspondent who was present
at the recent banquet to the Governor-General in
London, writes: —
The first glimpse of Lord Northcote was very dis-
appointing. He is a little man with no claim to good
looks. He appeared to be both shy and nervous and
created a rather unfavorable impression altogether. In
speaking he has a sermonic method, a drawling tone, a
level intonation, and a curious catch, suggestive of inci-
pient sort throat or prolonged vocal strain. He is a very
correct personage and he makes up in self-confidence for
what he lacks in stature. But he has money and hia
wife has more, so that perhaps his deficiencies will be
overlooked.
A Tasmanian M.L.A., who upbraided
the members of a political association for not
bringing their wives to the poll, is a bachelor.
The audience told' him to take his own medicine,
and he replied that he did ; that is to say, having
no wife he took another man’s wife to the poll.
Here, a voice full of gleeful vengeance and
jealousy : “I’ll tell —The orator quailed,
and when the applause ceased, the chairman
announced an impending division required Mr.
’s presence in the House.
Inherited instinct breaks through the
veneer of European civilisation in the Jap occa-
sionally. Coming down the Lane Cove river
(Sydney), the other day, a high official of the
Japanese consulate was reading the war-cloud
news, and, deeply absorbed, he unconsciously
folded his legs under him and squatted on the seat
in primitive Jap fashion. The excitement of the
news passed, and the Jap almost blushed as he
thought of his top-hat, his patent-leather boots
and his flogger coat, and sat again in strict accord-
ance with his European clothes.
Broken Hill's gaoler, Sidney Neil de
Montfort Bromley, who suicided the other day,
was the dandy of the N.S.W. prison service.
Stories of mighty relations hung around him ; he
claimed direct descent from Simon de Montfort,
the 13th century fire-eater, who married a king’s
sister and came very near being a king himself.
When Deputy-Governor at Goulburn Gaol Brom-
ley wore qavalry gloves and carried a cane—he
had been in the army—but a hint from head-
quarters made him discard them. He was hypo-
chondriacal, and the dull prison service is no
place for such a man.
A link with the long past of Australia is
James Hassall, of Matarai, Corinda, Brisbane
(Q.), an Australian native now in his 81st year.
Grandpa Hassall came out to the South Seas in
the missionary ship Duff in 1796. But the natives
showed their teeth, so the missionaries came to
N.S.W., where most of them took to secular
pursuits. Thomas Hassall, the father of James,
was then a child of three. He married the
daughter of Samuel Marsden. J. S. Hassall was
born in 1823. He went to school at Mulgoa,
where he had as schoolfellow the late Mr. King,
P.M. and Resident at Norfolk Island. Quite
recently the two met —the last of the Mulgoa
schoolboys—and ten days afterwards Mr. King
was dead. G. W. Rusden, an acquaintance since
the 30’s, died recently. Mr. Hassall’s recollec-
tions of Sydney go back to the year 1830.

Aboriginalities.
Dear B.—None of your aboriginaliars
know what a real damper is—the damper of the
bush. If you asked an “old hand” to make a
damper with baking-powder, he’d take a fit. —

“ Dal.” : A sheep-killing pig has turned
up in Gippsland. So far 13 sheep slain in one
night is its best average. The farmers are taking
shifts to watch for the cunning porker, but so
far without success.

A turtle weighing 3cwt. was, last month,
captured, after several hours’ tussle, by a party of
men near Freebum Island, Clarence River. It
was shipped to Sydney, by the Kyogle, alive.

Nat Gould, in “ Who Did It ?” speaks of
Munda as being “an immense station of some 40
square miles of country.” An average shearing
was “130,000 sheep.” Forty square miles of
country is 25,600 acres ! Over five sheep to the
acre on this station !

Three boys of one family, residing near
Deniliquin, are minus one finger. Each was
bitten by a snake in the finger, and each promptly
chopped the finger off.

Re Colonel Templeton’s massive “ shout ”
(8., 7/l/’O4). They had a custom, called “locking-
in,” on W.A. goldfields in the uproarious ’9o’s
that entailed some vast drink bills. The custom
decreed you must stand “treat” for the whole
house if found behind the bar, and if you didn’t
drift behind the taffrail of your own accord you
were generally helped there—that is, provided you
were “flush.’

“ Elmore ” : Ever see a gohanna stalking
game? I watched one, t’other day. He went,
one foot at a time, cautiously and silently, his
tongue darting in and out, expectantly; every
yard or so he would slowly raise his head, and
immediately lie flat again, just like a oat. About
30yds. away was a small patch of scrub, and as he
neared it he appeared to go slower than ever.
But at last, when about 10yds. off, a streak of
gohanna shot out, and there was a great disturb-
ance for a couple of seconds. Going over, I found
he had a full-grown hare. It took him 34min.
to go about 10yds., and about 3sec. to go the other
10yds. and get outside of puss. He swallowed her
whole, head first.
• • * •
“ Courabyra ”: In the Upper Murray dis-
trict stringy bark, peppermint, messmate, etc.,
have tempted bunny with their bark, though it is
of such a dry nature. In places where rabbits are
very thick, the above-mentioned timbers are being
ring-barked wholesale by them. Cattle are also
on the ring-barking job in Upper Murray and
Murrumbidgee districts. During last summer in
many places cattle in good condition where grass
was fairly plentiful started eating the bark off
stringy bark trees and various other timber with-
out, seemingly, doing themselves any harm.
Perhaps the Australian beast is being taught by
successive droughts to live on calico, wood and
other such substitutes for green stuff.
* • • •
“ Eiddal ” : Can’t understand why, in this
age of destructive insect pests, the Department of
Agriculture should tantalise the suffering cookies
and fruitgrowers. One of the principal attractions
at out-back shows a few months back was the
series of exhibits pertaining to different branches
of husbandry, such as flax-growing, fruit pre-
serving, etc., etc., shown under the auspices of
the Department. Round the sides of the Depart-
mental marquee were beautiful specimen plates of
the different kinds of insect pests, fungus, etc.,
etc., that worry “the man on the land”—but all
the letterpress available was printed in French or
German ! Never felt more aggravated over mis-
applied art in my existence before. The powers
that be may take a hint from this, and, while
their “pest” plates are printed in Germany, let
the information be given in English !
Instructive pen-picture of the return of a
recruited Kanaka to his native island, from Frank
Patou’s just-published book
™• , '
™ aß CO I“ In JC do d vn , m torrents, and a heavy sea
ws setting in. As the boat neared the shore we saw a
r ecrmt on board with his box. He looked wet and miser-
able in the boat; but when he was put over the side he
presented a ghastly sight. Emaciated and racked with a
fearful cough, he was too weak to walk out of the water,
but stood helplessly gazing about him. There were no
friends to welcome him, so we agreed to take care of him
an d return him to his village. .. . I had to walk very
slowly, and presently Notiang (the recruit) stopped and
said, “ Me sore,” and would have fallen had I not caught
hold of him. We had to half carrv him to the house. By
this time he was in a state of collapse, but Mrs. Paton got
him a cup of hot tea, and his eves fairlv gleamed as he
saw it We got him to bed, and every two hours we
gave him nourishment, and he came round wonderfully.
... All night long his cough sounded through the house,
...For two or three days he lingered on. Then his
brother came and took him on his back and carried him
home - It was only that he might die in his old village.
Notiang’s story is only too common in these far lands,

“San Remo”
sends a tale of sin
from Queensland :
When I was a lad
learning my trade the
Army used to perform
outside our shop every
Saturday night. ’Twas
in a certain Queens-
land town where the
cullud pussun was
mightily respected, so
a nigger walloped the
drum and a Chow car-
ried the banner of
blud. Said Chow also
acted as treasurer and
tax-gatherer. Charlie
Soo was a pillar of
the Army, an example
to hold up for the be-
nighted “ White Aus-
tralian ” heathen’s dis-
comfiture. But fate,
per medium of Borne
small boys, brought
about Charlie’s down-
fall. One night the
Army was going
strongly, the collec-
tion hummed, and
souls were gathered in
at a great bat. Busi-
ness being slack in the
shop that night, we
lads proceeded to have
a lark with Charlie.
With some pincers we
heated several coppers
over the gas jets till
they assumed the
Army’s hall-marked
fire-and-brimstone
temperature. Charlie’s
eagle-eye sighted the
coins ere they touched
ground, and he pounced on them with his usual thanks,
“ Glory, friends, glory! ” When the Celestial struck
those hot coppers he went heavenward with a yell,
making frantic efforts to shake a penny off his hand.
When Charlie got back to earth the language he
used was painful—almost sinful. “ Whaf for ! whaf
for: you blelly lallikin. Whaf for ! ” The nigsrer got
a fright and fell over the big drum. Recovering
himself with a severe look at Charlie, he went for the
cash, drumstick in hand. Wonderful what an instinct
the Army has for bullion. Tommy Tanna yelled when
he struck a hot’un, and let his drumstick fly off at a
tangent. The bewildered captain stopped it with his eye,
his surprise being as great as Charlie’s. Then a buxom
lass took a hand. Saying to Charlie, “ Pray with me,
brother,” she flopped on her knees. Unfortunately she
knelt on a hot copper, and hopped up with a squeal.
But when an old lady with a tambourine, convinced the
devil was afoot, fainted on the spot, we got scared and
left. We missed the rest of the performance, but heard
afterwards that the Army rescued the coppers with the
assistance of the fire brigade. Charlie repented, but he
was never the same old Charlie again.

“Myrtle”: At Tiaro (Q.), near Mary-
borough, some years ago, I secured two young
butcher-birds from their nest and reared them.
Finally they escaped. Cold weather of following
year saw them return. They came from the bush
(often when whistled for) regularly twice a day
to be fed, flying down to my shoulders, head or
hands, and, after a song in the fruit-trees, return-
ing to the bush. Caught also other young
butchers, and after feeding them well for some
considerable time, finally let them go. They
returned and remained months with us. Find
this a very effective method of rearing bush birds
without any wing-clipping. Only once did one
fail to return. For devilment I showed it itself
in a looking-glass, and it never was friendly with
me after.
“ Pyalla ” : Does any “ Aborigi-
nally” man know anything of “Jimmy
the Rambler?” The character in ques-
tion, a station and shearers’ cook, died
about ’93, and, as his sobriquet denotes,
was chiefly known by his frequent and
sudden exits. He is said to have cooked
at every station in N.S.W. After
working a few months or weeks at a
station he would quit without the
slightest notice. His manner of going
out was after this fashion. Suddenly,
as the shearers were filing in at meal-
time, he would yell out: “Here, y’are
boys ; bread and brownie in the trough,
and here’s the blanky cloth in the oven,
and be to you all! ” Then Jimmy
would take up his swag, and with long
strides disappear along the track.

“W.W.”: “Tag’s *’ information re
growth of trees (B. 3/12/’O3) is not cor-
rect. The main branches of almost every
tree are formed when <irhe tree is only
a few inches high, and yearly, until the
tree has reached full height, the distance
between the earth and the main branches
increases. Therefore the barrel must
grow upwards in same proportion as the
other,] limbs do. Surveyor’s marks, &c.,
don’t travel upwards, for the spot
chopped, or bored with a fencer’s auger,
remains dead even after new bark grows
over the marks, as dead wood is life-
less ; and that’s why such marks remain
stationary.

War. E.” : The clerical calling
gathered a rich store of opprobrious
appellations from irreverent Australians. Some
that I have heard : Sky-pilot, devil-dodger, gospel-

crow, pulpit-puncher, never-touch-it, spirit-mer-
chant, thrum-scragger, copper-chaser, snuffle-
buster, fire-and-brimstone ; there are others, but
they would make a religious paper like The Bul-
letin blush, and burn the Red Page to cinder.

“N.D.W.”: There is no doubt many
horses lose their lives through farmers’ ignorance
about broken limbs. I never saw such a case as that
mentioned by “Stan P.” (8., 10/11/’O3), but I
once saw a very fine colt's leg broken in the
branding-yard. The youngster was allowed to go
back into the bush with its dam, on three legs,
the fourth (off-fore) flopping. When it was
brought in again three years later it had between
the knee and the fetlock two large, callous lumps
and the foot was half-turned out. This deformity,
however, did not seem to make any difference to
the brute’s activity, and the leg was perfectly
strong. The colt was broken-in and proved a real
good one.
* * * *
A tourist who was walking, with a com-
panion, in the Waikato district (M.L.), between
Waikare Moana and Te Arolia, lately, witnessed
an exhibition of tohungaisrn. An elderly Maori
tohunga, coming towards these two, met a young
native on horseback carrying a bundle of eels.
The old man asked for some, and on being
refused raised both hands above his head and yelled
in Maori, “May your heart die and rot!”—the
deepest curse a Maori can use. The young man
galloped off, howling dismally. Three days later
the same tourists were returning to Waikare
Moana, called at a Maori pah named Rawhiti
(Rising Sun)„found a tangi in full progress, and
learned that the deceased was the victim of the
curse. He was a man about 25 years of age, of
splendid physique, and apparently in perfect
health. On his return from fishing he told his
wife of the curse, lay down on his mat, refused
all food, and died on the second day. An inquest
followed, but medical evidence assigned no cause
of deat*

WOLFE’S
SCHNAPPS
A CURE FOR KIDNEY AND BLADDER TROUBLES.
Bush telephony is an institution now in
the Poverty Bay district of Maoriland. For a
start they tried using a wire of the fences, as
Australian way-out stations do; but it didn’t
answer, for there is too much wet in M.L., and
consequently too much leakage of current at the
posts. Now, therefore, they strap light manuka
poles on to the fence-posts to carry the wire, and
still the whole cost doesn’t run to more than £3 a
mile. Nearly 200 farms are connected, and
can pass remarks to one another about the weather
and the last litter of pigs without any of the old
bother.
“ Havelock ” Watches.— The demand for the
famous advertising premium of the British Australasian
Tobacco Co., Ltd., known as the “Havelock” Watch, is
so great that although 30,000 were landed at the begin-
ning of the year, they are rapidly disappearing. We are
requested by the proprietors to inform people in the
country that, if they are unable to get the watch from
the local tobacconist, they can write direct to the British
Australasian Tobacco Co., Ltd., Elizabeth and Park
Streets, Sydney, and enclose 4s. 3d., made up as follows :
—Watch 2s. lid., plug of “Havelock” tobacco Is.,
postage 4d. No watches are sold, except to buyers of
the tobacco. Smokers are further reminded that they
can get the watch for 100 tags of “ Havelock ” plugs pur-
chased in New South Wales.*

PITT STREET.
“Hermit”: Re treatment of snakebite
(B-, 12/11/’O3). When the bite is below the knee
°. r the elbow joint the ligature should be tied
tightly above the joint, as one below does not pre-
vent the blood from circulating. If the victim be
bitten above the joint, ligature between thebiteand
the heart. This applies to the bites of all Australian
snakes except the death adder, bite of which is
best treated by incision, sucking and hypodermic
injection. My authority for most of the fore-
going is a Melbourne University professor whose
lecture on snakes, delivered a few weeks ago, and
published in the Australasian, should be in
every country home.
* * * *
“Thurla” : Recently saw in the Manila
River (N.S.W.) a fierce fight between two jewfish.
I hey had nests in the same waterhole, which was
very shallow, with gravel bottom. I scared one
from its nest, and it darted away and stopped
close to the other nest. The other fish savagely
attacked the intruder, swimming rapidly past
it with its side fins and spike rigid, and attempt-
ing to rip the enemy open. Failing in its attempt,
it would dive and come up under the other,
which, to avoid being impaled on the back spike,
would rise rapidly to the surface and leap out of
the water. Watched them until they got too far
away, but could still see the splash of the water
long after the fish were lost to sight.
♦ * * *
“ H.” : How is this for tired ! A man,
discharged from the Shepparton (Vic.) gaol after
serving seven days for insulting behavior, went
along to the village settlement just outside the
town, sat down on his swag, and continued to sit
there for several days and nights in spite of
heavy rain. Eventually the police took notice of
the man’s stagnation and arrested him again for
“ insulting behavior.” He was sent to the hos-
pital for treatment.

“ Harry S ” about a battle :
I once witnessed a fight between two aboriginal tribe*
in South Australia, about 150 miles west from Adelaide.
It was a pre-arranged affair, heralds having gone in
advance.to fix matters up. The senders of the challenge
were painted with white lines on each rib, and broad
white rings around their eyes, and the hair of many of the
most distinguished warriors was adorned with plumes of
thin acacia twigs, peeled so as to closely resemble
ostrich feathers. They advanced first in a long, extended
line of crescent form, which, every now and again,
became two lines, one man directly in front of another.
The stamp of their feet upon the ground could be heard
for a. long distance, and was as regular as clock-work.
Their opponents were also painted (but not
after the same fashion as the challengers), and they
also came on in single file and crescent formation ; then,
suddenly changing into closer compact they sent up their
shout of defiance. So sudden, distinct and shrill is this
cry, and so accurate in time, that it sounds as the voice
of one. man. Then both sides once again changed into
open line, and an equal number (about 20) of each
advanced until within about 25 yards of each other.
They expanded their chests to their fullest capacity, bent
down their bodies the while the great muscles of their
thighs shook and jumped in an extraordinary manner,
shouting “ya-yooa (“yes, yes”) and “ purdni, purdrii ”
(“ come on, come on ”). Suddenly they stood erect, and,
taking a half-step back, each man hurled his spear at the
enemy directly opposite to him. Soon the air was alive
with spears passing each other and those at whom they
were aimed in the most remarkable manner. A shoulder
would be dropped, a head turned, the body wriggled, a
foot lifted, or the danger glanced off by the weapon held
in the left hand. During the engagement probably 500
spears were thrown and only one man was hurt, and that was
through the spears colliding anddivertingoneintendedfor
another foe out of its proper course. Some of their
movements during this combat were so marvellously
adroit as to surpass belief if notpersonally witnessed. No
fear, no hesitation ; eye and hand acting in perfect
unison, and with a grace of motion possessed by no other
living race.

verbal agreement, to have a look at before he

“ Lindon ” : “ Ciro’s ” two-headed snake
(8., 3/12/’O3) accords well with what are called
“stumpies,” or “two-headed lizards,” up Wim-
mera (Vic.) way. Their tails are the same shape
as their heads, but smaller. They are harmless,
but strangers to them might easily be alarmed by
the way they open their mouths and dart out
their blue tongues.
* * * *
“ E.H.P.” : As a thirst-quencher prickly
pear beer takes a bit of beating. As the niggers
in Cape Colony make it, it’s a hair-raising and
stomach -blistering swizzle, and a man who has
had a carouse on it won’t forget it in a hurry,
Before fermentation it is a sickly sweet liquor;
after, and when it has been strained, it is more
acid, and when not too concentrated it is palatable
and pleasant enough.
Re “Nymsie’s” inquiry as to
age of white cockatoos (8., 31/12/’O3). My mother
has one 40 years old, which is a well-plumaged
bird still, and early last year laid two eggs.
* * * »
“ Rhaupo ” : Most noted Western charac-
ters get into The Bulletin, but the coast has
its hard cases as well. For over eight
years on the Great Northern road,
from Dungog to Walcha and back
again via Singleton, there has
tramped, with unswerving -
regularity, a diminutive
and ancient whaler,
always wearing a
straw boater and
neither moustache
nor beard, though his
garments are the
orthodox. He leads
a pony as well known
as himself, and as old.
First met him eight
years ago, damning
capitalists generally,
politicians individually,
and parsons collectively.
He affirms the country
folk are in league against
him, and attributes to this
their refusal to give him
work. Have met him every-
where since then, and he still
damns capitalists, politicians
and parsons. But work with
him is a dim memory, and the
north road folk are good to him ;
so he goes his round, year in and
year out, damning everything.
• • •
“ Uloola Recently came
across what I thought was a
novelty in the way of a kooka- ""
burra’s nest. Away up the
trunk of a huge mahogany
was a knob of white ants’
dirt, a couple of feet across,
and protruding about a foot
from the butt. In this the
“Jacks” had scooped a hole
sufficiently large, and there,
when I discovered it, the young
ones were ensconced. A native
of the district told me it was
common—the goburra won’t build if
he can find a suitable ants’ nest. The
king-fisher acts similarly. Young “Jacks”
are the noisiest things in the bush. There are
two near my hut, and throughout most of the
day they make a row like a soore of fowls arguing
over one worm.

“ Old Tom ” writes :
“ R.H." (B. 10/12/’O3) is not correct in all he says re
swagmen. Have seen diggers (I was one of them) travel-
ing, in N.S.W., from one diggings to another, and the
majority carried walking sticks. I affirm, with a bang,
that in mountain country a good stick is a wonderful
help. In the flat country ’tis not necessary. Again,
“ R.H.” says that swagmen do not carry tucker-bag on
same shoulder as swag. He is half right, or thereabouts,
for, since his par., I have taken stock of swagmen who
called where I dole out the orthodox “ banjo ” of matton,
and have noted that of 29 callers 18 carried “ nose-bag ’’
over the same shoulder as swag—ll over opposite
shoulder, as “ R.H.” suggests. Artists, please note. By
the way, the digger loves his bowyang. Have seen a
dozen of him wearing them in church ; also have seen lots
of him asleep in them —after a wash-up.

“ Argo ” : A boundary rider on a station
out-back makes a specialty of dyeing emu skins.
Latterly he has taken to dyeing his poultry and
pets. You can see a blue sheep, ohased by a green
terrier in and out of a flock of magenta fowls. He
is considered quite sane, too I
A Chinese gardener at Kew (Vic.) re-
ported recently to the police that sundry mis-
creants had raided his house at midnight, and
robbed him of the only £lO he possessed in the
world. Investigation, however, showed that Ah
Pang, wishing to send £2O to his aged parents in
China, had trumped up the robbery yarn, in per-
fect confidence that his local compatriots would
subscribe the money intended for a purpose which
so strongly commends itself to every Chinaman.
“ I wantee gettee money for my poo’ ole faler an’
moler,” whimpered the dutiful heathen. “ I pay
im allee back; me welly solly.”

“ A.K.C.” re damper :
The “gummy” fraternity, who had teeth once upon
a time, prefer the soft, flabby, not to say “puddingy”
concoction. Broken into lumps and well soaked in
strong black tea, it has much to recommend it. Those
who have managed to hang on to a molar or two
generally take on the iron-clad damper. This sort is
mixed up stiff and baked in the ashes. Time, three to
nine hours, according to thickness. When cooked, and
about half an inch of charcoal chipped off all round, it
should be well flogged with a hat to improve its appear-
ance. To Ml up with iron-clad would require the
protracted labor of a ruminant. Hence great economy.
Turned flat side uppermost, and with the necessary
holes punched in with a nail, a cribbage board and table
combined is the result; and, taking care to cut all round
equally when using, and always to turn the flat side
down when sitting on the iron-clad, a long and useful
career for it is assured. I’ve given up dampers now,
and have taken on scientific cooking with yeast. I take
time, and do the thing properly. Struck a cool Change
last batch. Followed the sun round the tent with the dough
for the first three days, brought it in out of the rain
4th day, chanced it the fifth day (thought the fire would
raise it). Sixth day had to make a feed on gruel, and
gave bread away to a German (they’re terrors on sour
tack). Bested 7th day.
• • * •
The real lunatic, as distinguished from
the crank of fiction:—
“ A.P.R.” : A queer crank struck our camp one evening
about dusk. He was a small, whiskered man with a
gentle voice and a mirthless laugh, and was attired
chiefly in a green mackintosh. He didn’t know where he
had come from, but it must have been a long way, as he
devoured all our spare tucker. Then he asked if we
liked fish. We liked fish, so he offered us a fiver to buy
some, though we were five miles from anywhere.
“ Where’s this war ? ” he asked suddenly—“ it upsets me.
I've tried to find it. Is it in this country?” He turned
in for the night on a few bags we spread for him, after
requesting us “not to touch him” during the night.
The thermom. was very low, and we heard him presently
say, “ It’s awful cold. I think I’ll make a fire.” He kept
on saying this at intervals for several hours, and then he
got up and left We didn’t see him again.

A few Victorian place names :—Goora-
madda, Bonegilla, Baulkamaugh, Boorhamana,
Yaughar, Drumanure, Jeeralang, Buckrabanyule,
Eurack, Wonwondah, Boomanoomanah, and
Pirronyalloak.
Visited the scene of Gordon’s
much-boasted horsemanship at Mt. Gambier
(S.A.) recently, and came to the conclusion that
it wouldn’t take a very massive wager to tempt me
to do it on my bike.

“ Hiphen ” : Can any Bulletin contri-
butor inform me how it is that once a cat haß
taken to eating locusts or grasshoppers it never
puts on flesh again, no matter how well fed, but
remains thin and looks famine-stricken for all
time ? That it is so lam positive, from personal
observation as well as hearsay, yet I can find no
reason why.
♦ * * *
“C.P.”: In a Victorian backblox settle-
ment, where the residents spend most of their
time in not keeping sober and chasing the flies,
a little old maiden lady keeps a school. Her
pupils comprise 10 boys and 12f girls, the other
quarter having been amputated as the result of an
adventure with a fox-trap. To keep the boys
amused on “rag day,” when the girls are
learning to tuck and hem, she formed a cadet
corps. “Eyes front 1” she yells in a field-
marshal tone of voice. “ Right turn ! —face
to’rds the public house! ” —they all know
where that is and swing round like regulars.
“Left turn! —face to’rds Patsy Brannigan’s ! ”
“ Quick march ! ” —and she conducts them into
the bush about half a mile away, and commands
each “cadet” to arm himself with a rifle. For a
few moments a sound of ripping and tearing
greets the ear, then each youth appears with a
substantial lump of timber on his shoulder. Back
they march to the school-yard, and go through a
few more exercises from the old drill-book.
“ Present arms ! ” The boys present with vigor.
“Ground arms !”—with still more vigor. So the
old lady gets firewood for the rest of the week.

“ Pyalla ” : Moree to Inverell railway ;
slowest travelling in N.B.W. ; open road, no
fences, flat country, sheep lying asleep on railway
line, firemen throwing coal at them, engineer
getting down at intervals to kick jumbucks off
the rails. It makes me tired.
* * * *
“ H.S.” : Very curious are the pecu-
liarities of the flight of the boomerang, and the
implement requires to be understood before it can
be successfully manipulated. There is a differ-
ence in every skilful thrower’s style ; so the native,
when fashioning his weapon, adapts it to suit him-
self. Some boomerangs go from the thrower’s
hand in an almost direct line and nearly parallel
to the ground for up to 60 yards, then suddenly
rise to a great height and come almost directly
back to the thrower. Others go for nearly the
same distance on a gradual rise, and then as
gradually fall on their return after describing an
almost complete circle; others take an almost
similar flight to the last, but “ dwell ” or
“ sleep ” in the air when near the thrower,
whilst others again form in their flight almost the
figure 8. The wind is a great factor in
boomerang throwing. Should it be gusty and
irregular, coining from different directions, the
most expert will fail to do himself justice. The
aborigines hardly ever throw where little hills
divert the wind, for the reason above stated.
N.S. Wales, Queensland and Victorian boomerangs
are mostly flat on the under side and slightly
rounded on the upper side. They are made from
the black ti-tree, acacia pendula, mulga, gidyea,
myall, raspberry jam wood and tire she-oak tree.
Return boomerangs measure from about 13in. in
length up to over 30in. The return boomerang,
when made of heavy wood, can inflict a very ugly
wound with its keen inner edge, the force caused
by the rotatory motion being very great. It is
said that a few African tribes use a kind of flat
throwing stick much like the Australian boomer-
ang, and the ancient Egyptians and Mexicans un-
doubtedly had very similar weapons. On Cooper’s
Creek, Central Australia, the Dieyeri tribe use an
immense boomerang, often measuring over five
feet in length, several specimens of which I
possess. These weapons are used with both
hands, and, though true boomerangs in shape, are
in reality two-handed clubs or swords.

Business, Robbery, etc.
The cane-cutting season just finished on the
Clarence (N.S.W.), lasted 24 weeks, the longest run for
many years. Estimated- that 70,000 tons of cane were
put through the mills. The rebate on white-grown
sugar in the district amounts to £12,000. The cutters’
wages panned out about £9OOO. .
* * * *
Victorian Government Analyst has just given
his version in support of the sterling honesty of the soft-
goods importer. In a report to the Board of Health he
says that, after testing 25 samples of clothing sold to the
inspectors as pure woollen, he finds that but seven were
of the material alleged ; the other 18 containing cotton in
amounts ranging from 5 per cent, to 66 per cent., the
average amount being 44 per cent.
* * * »
Shark registry offices are fond of advertising
for “ educated young gentlemen ” as assistant teachers,
tutors, and secretaries. The shark has found by long
experience that “ the educated young gentleman ” is
more easily victimised than hard-faced Bill or Jim who
wants a job tearing up the ground or milking cows. The
“educated young gentleman,” after paying his five
shillings registration-fee, is told to call again, and again,
then the shark curses him or lets a chair drop on his toe
and he doesn’t come again. He merely fasts for a week to
make up for the stolen five shillings, whereas the ordinary
Bill and Jim, whose stomach is a rebellious organ, is apt
to go down to the shark’s office and break his window or
his nose.
* * * *
Some dealers in secondhand boots and shoes in
Sydney employ respectably-dressed women, who canvas
families in the suburbs for cast-off boots and shoes,
ostensibly for charity’s sake. The articles, after a little
repair, are sold to needy customers.
* * * *
The drought, apparently, has no lesson to teach
the average Australian. Recently thousands of acres of
waving wheat, oats, barley and luxuriant native grasses
enclosed in the N.S.W. ruilway fences have been pur-
posely burnt-off as a precaution against fire. Almost
within the municipal boundary of Wellington (N.S.W.)
they recentty burnt off their lucerne and grass. But of
course there will be no more droughts.
* * * *
“ Old Cove ” : Bad job for Australia that India
and Ceylon ever started tea-growing. If tea were three
times its present price and none but China variety were
available it would be a lot better for us. As a suitable
drink for the Australian climate, the strong, syrupy
Ceylon tea is no more to be compared to the light China
tea than are heavy ales to light lager. And the use of
Ceylon tea is now almost universal in the bush. What
about a Royal Commission to inquire into the evils of
drinking strong Indian tea, with a view to putting 2s. a
lb. duty thereon ?
* * * *
“Long Jim,” 7ft. 2in. in height, who has been
advertising a patent medicine in Adelaide for some time
East, left recently for S’Africa, which he will tour on
ehalf of the same firm. Dwarfs, giants, hairy politicians
and other freaks are beginning to be in demand for ad-
vertising purposes.
* * # *
The collecting craze in boys runs in all shapes,
but nearly always in some shape. One S.A. country-
bred youngster has just been unearthed whose mania
runs to gathering different varieties of grain and growing
them : this season, besides oats, barley, and rye, he grew
47 sorts of wheat, gathered from all parts. Both the
boy’s father and grandfather—the family name is Frame
—are well known wheat-growers ; either the grandfather
or a neighbor (it has never been decided which) grew the
parcel that gained the world’s prize at the first London
Exhibition in 1851, and helped to get for S. Aus. the
name of “ the Wheat State." The parcel, by the way,
is now preserved in the Adelaide Museum.
* * * *
“P.T.F.” :Be cotton. In 1853 N.S.W. Govt,
offered premiums for the best sam pies of Australian-
grown cotton, the first prize of £3O being won by Mr.
Ambrose Eldridge, of Brisbane. Also, in 1852, that
enthusiastic believer in the future of Australian cotton,
Dr. John Duntnore Lang, submitted several splendid
samples of locally grown cotton to the Manchester Cham-
ber of Commerce.
A PHANTOM OF THE FUTURE—KING
COHEN, THE PARLIAMENT-MAKER.
A proposal for the formation of a co-operative
bakery among the hotel and restaurant keepers of Sydney
is now being seriously considered. The venture will
require a capital of £2OOO, and this has already been
guaranteed many times over.
* * * *
“Josdon” asks for advice: Can any one
suggest a permanent cure for a lazy boss? The one
who keeps me out of Parliament takes the cake for
downright criminal laziness. He comes in at 9.30 a.m.,
throws himself into a chair, picks up the paper, and
starts absorbing why Smith was found in Jones’ bed-
room, and then mutters abstractedly, “My oath, it’s
hot.” I weirdly expect something to happen. It
happens. “I say, Thompson,” he yells. “Ye’sir.”
“ Bring’s a drink of water, will you; I’m too hot to
move.” When I bring the water he mutters, “ Put it down
there, so’s I can reach it without gettin’ up.’’ After five
minutes peace he breaks out again with “Thompson,
hold the glass while I get a drink ; I’m too tired to get
up.” Told a pal about him once. “ Why don’t you
chuck it over him ? ” he suggested. Followed his tip.
“ My oath, that’s all right,” the boss casually remarked.
I suppose he was too lazy to give me the sack.
* * * *
“Rypo ” has the gold fever—and recovers : We
moved to a gold-mining town recently. I had always
yearned to live in a mining town. I believed that any-
body who wanted a shilling or two thereabouts must be
a lop-sided, dull specimen of humanity. Was there not
always fossicking to fall back upon if work got slack and
funds ran low ? So I determined to get an idea of the
game the first chance. It soon came. I got a mate with
mining experience. As compensation for his superior
knowledge I supplied the tools, and we shared
the gold equally. Loaded with dishes, shovels, a pick,
a couple of old buckets to carry “ stuff ” to the nearest
water, a “ jigger ” each to cut up the lumps and stir the
dust in the dishes, until it was reduced to a degree suit-
able to work with the hands alone, and a 61b.,jam-tin to
hold the “ pannings,” we set out. I was shown the likely
“ stuff’’- the pipeclay round the old holes and the wash-
dirt. I learned the exact angle at which to hold the
dish and the proper depth of water to rest it in for suc-
cessful washing. Stir, stir, stir ; slop, slop, slop ; shake,
shake, shake ; swish, swish, swish. Water very cold.
Hands soon likewise. Feet wet and cold. Legs
cramped with squatting over the dish. Backache.
Fingers start to wear out. Mud and clay from head
to foot. Saw an occasional speck of dull yellow stuff on
the bottom of the dish—after giving the remaining hand-
ful of sand the proper swirl to uncover it. This was the
gold, my instructor intimated. Never saw such small
results from so much toil. The remnant was put into the
61b. jam-tin aforesaid. At length the sun sank below the
hills. I thought it never would. I was prepared to swear
those hills were levelling down. Never felt so tired, and
cold, and dirty, and wet, and miserable before. I judged,
from the exertion and discomforts entailed, that that
jam-tin should be half-full of pure gold, though I didn’t
know where it could have come from. “We’ll pan her
off,” said my partner. He did it. He would not
trust a new-chum like me with such wealth.' “ Few nice
specks,” he admitted, “about half a ‘weight’ for the
day.” Half a weight—one shilling each. We had a
smoke. Then the stars began ro peep out, and the moon
rose upon the scene. I went home, dug myself out of
the mud, put salve on my hands, hung myself out to dry,
warmed myself, and went to bed too tired to even dream
of gold-mining. Gold-mining! Bah ! Wood-chopping
is better and cleaner, and drier, and warmer, and more
remunerative.
* * * *
“ Eiddal ”: I have been experimenting with
the “Silver watch for nothing” puzzle business. The
one I struck looked much as usual. Some simple motto
was published, with the vowels omitted, and a silver
watch was offered as a prize to each successful solver of
the mystery. Competitors were expressly warned to
send no money with their solution. I purposely sent an
incorrect solution of a very simple motto-puzzle, and
received by return post a reply to the effect that my
clever solution was correct, and, provided I sent 265. for
a silver chain, I would receive gratis one of the adver-
tiser’s famous watches—all its wonderful advantages
were set off, even tj its hands being of best steel. Being
in the trade, and having to handle these free watches
frequently, seeing that not one in 50 is in going order
when they arrive, I didn’t bite. Swiss watches are now
catalogued by British exporters at ss. each, jewelled,
keyless &c. ; and a good price for a silver chain is 7s. 6d.
imported, or ss. to 6s. made locally. I figured out the
whole proceeding as a method whereby 12s. worth of
watch and chain were sold at over twice their value, and
the seller got a reputation for philanthropy thrown in,
and the public worried over a silly puzzle as a side-show.
