“J.F.D.”: The European tradesman who has
struck a bad mark is generally pleased when his rivals,
who have picked up the custom, fall in also. Not so the
Chow. My landlady askid her comparatively new John,
a few' days back, to chalk up a few shillingsworth of
vegetables. He inquired if she was in (he house long,
and on being told that she had only just occupied the pre
mises, he chalked up. I asked him why he gave credit
to new arrivals only, whereupon he showed me where Ah
Spud, or some such savage, had inked on the gate a
Chinese equivalent to “ Dashed bad egg,’’ and a date in
Chinese. He had made his inquiries to assure himself
that my landlady wasn’t the aforesaid egg. When X
attempted to rub it out, he said, “ No, no ! stoppum
there long time ; no other wegitable Chinaman come.”
The Chow’s Trade Protection Society is simple and
efficient.
• • * *
Competition is keen among Sydney’s cheap
restaurant-keepers ; when two of them are close together
the fight is full of meat and hate. One sausage-flinger
employs a nicely-spoken, but hard-up, new-chum to sit
how at one table and now at another and softly “ crack-
up” the tucker. He tells the customers, in a low voice,
that the soup is equal to Voissin’s or that of the Caffe des
Ambassadeurs in Paris. Mellifluous name 3 breathed in
the wharf-lumper’s ear soothe his iron digestion when he
is bolting a piece of shark-fin or struggling with a mass
of aged ewe. In such moments he likes to be addressed
kindly, and to hear nice things about foreign cook shops.
The above restaurant also hires athletic individuals to
kill the rival hash-man’s business over the way. One of
the agents was a huge American nigger. He walked in
when it was “ full house ” and ordered rabbit-pie. In the
middle of his feast he appeared to extract from the
potatoes something which resembled a dingo’s hind leg ;
it was covered with tawny hair, and it dripped as he held
it between his finger and thumb. “Say, boss, come
a-here ! ” There was a yell in the nig.’s voice, the yell of
Pope addressing Sayles at the Tivoli. “Is this a
white mahn’s food ? ” He stood up and waved the hind
leg at the quailing restaurant-keeper. “By Heaven,
mahn, d’ye kill yeh meat in a menagerie? Why d'ye
give a mahn paw feat? Ain’t there nothin’ but paw in
de house?” The hash-keeper made some inaudible
remarks, but the nigger continued his address in the
voice of a cyclone. “ Foh Heaven’s sake, mahn, put up a
card an’ call it de Dingo Dining Rooms. Blank me, yoh fry
de meat in bear fat!’’ Herewith the ebony blatherskite
began a stirring eulogy in the interest of his boss’
restaurant over the way. He referred to it, in the voice
of Sayles, as a place where the boiled pig’s cheek wore a
heavenly and sylph-like expression. “ Be Heaven, yeh get
clean mahn’s food ober de way, boys. Yeh won’t find de
animal’s feet in de pie, sonnies. " Ef yeh want a clean
chaw come ober de way. S’long, sonnies, dis place gives
me bile.” And the colored hireling departed, after
executing a cake-walk, to the hash-house where the
animal never misbehaves when you elevate the pie-
crust.
* * * *
“J.”: Met a man selling clocks on time-
payment yesterday, and I inspected the document which
bound the unfortunate hirer to the instalment vulture.
The final clause was a masterpiece. It ran thus : “ The
hirer, in the event of his returning the articles to the
owner before the final payments are made, shall pay the
owner a sum equal to weeks’ hire of the article, as
a set-off for wear and tear given to the article during the
period of hire.” I asked the canvasser how many weeks’
hire he considered a sufficient set-off in the event of
anyone relinquishing a clock valued at 255. He said
that he left that part of the agreement blank, the boss
filling it in when he took the signed form back to the
office ! Thus Henrietta Brown, after paying 10s. on the
clock, finds herself unable to meet the other payments,
and returns it to the owner to find herself confronted
with a clause whereby she loses the clock, loses the 10s.
already paid, and has to pay ss. as well, to console
the shark for getting his goods returned. The time--
payment vulture is a greater evil than the "two-up”
school. At present he brings on his cases before
magistrates who seem to have a special interest in his
welfare, and he waxes fat and wears much jewellery.
The Inky Way.
A Press Olub is being formed in Sydney to provide a
meeting place for pressmen (membership strict] v confined
to them). The club is to be run on limited liability lines,
and the prospectus reads well. The sec. pro tem. is Mr.
R. Asher, Sydney Morning Herald office.
» * * *
“A good double murder—say of wife and child-
occurring at the right hour, is worth,” said the evening
paper publishing-man, “about 15,000 extra copies. If
the murderer will then take to the bush and defy the
police efforts to catch him for a couple of days, it pans
out to another 10,000 a day. The trial is not worth
much, and hangings are not what they used to be—worth
about 5000 at the best, in ordinary cases.”
*** - »
Sydney D.T. has bought the King and Castlereagh
streets corner, whereon the Metropolitan Hotel stands,
and, on expiry of present lease (a good few years ahead
yet), will build there. The S.M.H. seems quite content
with its present antiquated and not at all central building.
A few months ago it had the opportunity of buying an
adjacent block of land (the old Bank of New Zealand
premises), but let it slip.
* * * *
“ Old Boy,” of the Argos, who took Donald Macdonald’s
place as cricket reporter during the latter’s eccentric
candidature for Corio, is R. W. Wilmot, who has written
for the Australasian since his school days. His cricket
notes are much more correct, technically, than Mac-
donald’s, but lack something of the other’s style.
* * * *
“ Vulcan ” : The Maoriland Government is now sharing
with Canada, the expenses of supplying private persons
(newspaper proprietors) with 500 cable words per week.
The Australian papers aren’t enjoying the same plum,
because the Australian Government took the Tory press
advice, in this matter at least, as to subsidising private
enterprises.
* * • *
J. H. Finlayson, late editor Adelaide Register, will
probably return to S.A. early this year. He has for some
time been its ’Ome correspondent.
* * * *
On Jan. 2, The Ovens and Murray Advertiser, pub-
lished at Beechworth, Victoria, saw its 60th year of
publication as a tri-weekly. In the half-century number,
Mr. Richard Warren, the proprietor, addressed his sub-
scribers and patrons in a retrospective article. He is
probably the oldest pressman in harness in the Common-
wealth to-day. He has spent several fortunes in mining,
retaining his active interest in his journal all the time
* * * *
Brisbane Daily Mail doesn’t set out on its fight with
the Courier with too much capital behind it. It thought
first of tackling the job on £16,000, but changed its mind
(so far, it doesn’t seem to have much mind to change)
and made it £20,000.
* * * *
The Atchison Globe, Kansas, U.S.A., is a plain-spoken
paper which does not take too severe a view of current
events. It says : “ Jerome Hoover was on the streets to-
day with a hole in his pants. However, in justice to his
wife, whom he got by advertising, it is only fair to state
that she can’t mend them until he gets a new pair.”
THE LATE MR. W. D. ASTEN
(of the American Tobacco Go. of Australasia).
“G.H.”: Of all the newspaper-Mikados I have con-
descended to be employed by, John Norton stands out as
the gentlest, mildest-mannered, most lovable, and most
unassuming. I remember with pleasure how the staff
used to meet regularly in John’s sanctum every morning
for prayers and fatherly admonition and advice ; and
how the curly-haired counter-clerk and the earnest sub-
editor and the religious person in charge of the Sportsman
used to sing Moodv and Sankey hymns in a deep voice
while John beseeched powerfully into the waste-paper
basket. There is no more affable and benevolent news-
paper boss in all Australia ; and anyone who insinuates
to the contrary is a fisherman and a cablegram.
* * * *
Mr. H. W. Robinson, District Judge, and the holder of
multifarious other offices at Nelson (M.L.), has just been
retired, being over the age limit. Robinson mined for
gold in Victoria in the early days, and eventually took
on journalism, succeeding Julius Vogel, as editor of the
Maryborough and Dunolly Advertiser in ’6O. Subse-
quently Vogel induced him to cross over to Maoriland
and sub-edit the Otago Daily Times and act as editor of
the Witness, the weekly off-shoot. When he was offered
the billet of warden and R.M. in ’63, however, Robinson
forsook the press and has been in an atmosphere of
officialdom ever since.
The boss was indulgent, but wise after a fashion.
“Mr. Storekeeper,” said the editor of the bi-weekly
Gulch Meteor, “ I’ve been here three years, now, with-
out a break, - and something I take to be brain-fag is
coming on me. Could you let me away for a month ? ”
“ I’m sorry to hear it,” replied the storekeeper ; “ your
work is very good, and I don’t see how I can get on
without you. You know I’m not much good.” “Perhaps
you could put on a man for a month?” “Exchequer
wouldn’t run it. But, see here ! A month means eight
issues. You write me out eight leaders, throwing your
mind into the future as well as you can, and relinquish
half-pay, and I’ll try to fill the gap. I must have the
leaders before you start.” And the leaders, with the
editor’s mind thrown into the future, are appearing.
“ D.” : The old blocks in the country newspaper offices
possess a vast amount of adaptability. When I was a kid,
the local paper had three, but one in particular I remem-
ber. It was a well-known block of a man riding a buck-
jumper. The local saddler had it fqr years, then he fell
out with the editor and withdrew his a'dvt. The chemist
invented a cough mixture about that time, and the editor
fixed up the block with the words, “ If you can’t throw it
off, take Billy Smith's Cough Mixture.” The rider was
supposed to represent a cough. It pulled through three
different circus companies, and brought them excellent
houses. Then it set’led down to do steady work for the
local storekeeper, who was advertising riding-boots,
cabbage-tree hats, etc., which the buckjumper luckily
wore. One day, the paper came out without the block,
and the editor inserted an obituary paragraph informing
the town that a spiteful and villainous travelling comp,
had put it under the guillotine and cut it in halves. The
paper didn’t seem the same, and the storekeeper lost a
lot of business.
..ri dail . y r !°®“ t ! y Published an extraordinary
seues of letters about Eichenflopper steel plates.” Tha
correspondent, who started the joke, wrote in reference
to a vessel he called the Urumtees: “On the vessel’s
arrival at Solothurn I had the honor of boarding her and
making experiments with the plates on this vessel, and
applied the celebrated Eisbein test, calcium oxide. I
molar indentation of animalcule had taken
P ~e- tus anot her correspondent (or the same)
replied : Some three years ago, through the courtesy of
the manager, I was shown over the works at Andermatt,
where these plates are manufactured, and where most of
the world s biggest ships are fitted. I was greatly struck
with the huge stock of machinery used in the punching
of the rivet crafters and lumbers, and the great
precautions taken to prevent the warping of the
steel grangers that overlap the bilgers which the plates
are cast from. The exactness of the grab chain when
entering the flying wood crank was astounding. The
fritishness caused by the slinging hungers can be dis-
tinctly felt b} r placing one’s hand on or near any small
quantity of waste or fire-damp when the pressure gauge
indicates eighty per cent. lb. to the inch, which pressure
the Discovery had to undergo. As all engineers know,
in fixing the plates, the pressure (automatic) should be
placed clear of the plate, to allow for the warping, and
the steam would consequently clink the top flyin»
clunkers, instead of there being any chance of clunking
the clinkers. The letters spread into a column or so
before the joke was perceived, and an angry editor shut
down on his scientific leg-pullers.
A BALLADE OF SPRING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Editor lurked in his lair
Gloomy, and grumpy, and glum ;
There entered a party, whose hair
Was lengthy and roughened and rum.
He said.to the paper-man : “Come,
Just list to this sweet little thing ”
—He cleared his throat softly : “ Ha ! Hum i
Oh ! Hark to my Ballade of Spring 1"
“ Sweet Spring is a maiden so fair,
Her charms it is hopeless to sum ;
She breathes in the bloom-scented air,
She laughs in the bumble-bee’s hum.
Then he who can mope and be mum
When everything bids him to sing,
Must sure to all feeling be numb—
Oh ! Hark to my Ballade of Spring I
“ Sweet Spring ” He just dodged an arm-chair,
A book and a bottle of gum 1
The Editor glared at him there
And cried : “ Depart, ye base scum ! ”
(The poet fled, quite overcome.)
“Spring indeed I Spring yourself, or I’ll ring ”
— He pressed a small knob with his thumb—
“ Just hark to my Ballade of Spring ! ”
Envoy:
Ye print-hoping poets, a crumb
Of advice in these verses I bring :
On the subject of Springtime be dumb ;
Remember my Ballade of Spring !
S. ELLIOTT NAPIER.
ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will betaken of
communications insufficiently stamped. Name and
address must appear on MS. No liability is under*
taken in any case re contributions voluntarily sub-
milted, whether sent by post or handed in ; and in
no case will MS. be returned unless stamps (oj any
State) accompany.

Mr. Wilson D. Asten, late managing
director of the American Tobacco Co. of Austral-
asia Ltd., died in Melbourne last month. Few
men were better known in the Commonwealth
than he, his business interests taking him every-
where. And where known Mr. Asten was like<d,
his honest worth and kindly character gaining
him friends.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Suggested song for the Jolmaynes’ Seces-
sion Party in N.S.W. : “Johnny, Get Your
Gun.”
Canada’s present attitude to Britain is
defined by London Daily Chronicle as one of
“ passive hostility.”
Henry Parkes carried an Elective Legis-
lative Counoil Bill through N.S.W. Assembly in
1873, but the Bill was rejected by the Council on
the ground that it had not originated there.
About the best thing Deakin could do to
show that he is not altogether tired-out is to join
heartily in Seddon’s well-justified protest against
the Chinese invasion of the Rand.
James Martin, before he became Chief
Justice, proposed in N.S.W. Assembly. that
Ministers of the Crown should have salaries of
£2500 a year while in office, and pensions after-
wards.
Mr. Bruce Smith’s name is “ mentioned ”
in connection with the post of Chairman of Com-
mittees in the House of Reps., which is a very
small thing for the haughty Myth to be mentioned
for.
There’s one thing the N.S.W. Secessionist
fragment does not spare, and that is adjeotives.
The more its cause is treated with contemptuous
amusement the more weird and wonderful become
its adjectives.
The Sydney Morning Herald bewails
the fact that a successor to William M‘Millan, as
Mr. Reid’s lieutenant in the Federal Parliament,
is not easily procurable. Which is a savage
criticism of the intellectual value of the Freetrade
members.
A PHANTASY.
So benevolent were some of these saints that they
would prevent the vermin falling from them lest the
insect should perish. —Old church history.
The Commonwealth probably does not value the
secessionists over much; but, for reasons , will
retain them.
Germany, in its Freetrade days, was
a country that was emptying of its population.
In 1881 —the first year of Protection —the emi-
grants numbered 4.86 per cent, of the population.
The proportion steadily declined until, in 1901, it
was only 0.39 per cent.
Speaking at a political dinner at Hamilton
(Vic.), F. Tudor, M.H.R. (referring to preferential
trade):
For him it was Australia first. England or the Empire
could come second; but Australia first, emphatically,
and any others afterwards.
Complained by some of the people in-
terested in Federal capital sites that, when tour-
ing legislators were provided with coaches and
driven around “to see the district,” they often
spent their time on the road absorbed in cards
instead of in studying the landscape.
Actually, as a result of the re-arranged
“provinces,” there is talk of one or two contested
Leg. Council elections in Vic. For the Bendigo
“ province,” which takes in the whole Northern
“ province,” there is the almost unheard-of thing
of three candidates in the field. But all are old
and Fat.
A proviso in the Immigration Restriction
Act which might be put to good use is that under
which any person, not a British subject, convicted
of a crime of violence, is, at the expiration of
bis sentence, subject to the educational test,
failure to pass which renders him liable to de-
Eortation. Rigidly enforced, this proviso would
elp Australia to get rid of the worst portion of
its alien population—the convicted criminals.
The S.A. National Ass brays blatantly
concerning the need of legislators having “a
stake ” in the country, but it is still a fact that,
with one exception, all its Federal candidates
were very hard up, and contested the election
more for the sake of the salaries than for any
other reason, whereas all the elected Labor
members are quite capable of earning a decent
living altogether independent of their pay as poli-
ticians.
“ H.S.R.” hatterises a little :
I have asked many N.S.W. people, who assure me that
we have got hold of an expensive toy in Federation, to
name any articles they pay more for now than before,
and the only refuge they have is in hats. Now, if, owing
to Federation, a hat will cost me Is. 6d. more, as I require
one in a year or two, lam going to secesh. It is too
much to expect a patriot to drop Is. 6d. a year on hats.
What is Johnaynes’ address?
A feature of the Federal elections in
Queensland was the growth of the white labor
vote at Thursday Island—the very blackest spot
of the piebald North. 1901 : Villiers Brown (pro-
nigger) 68, Bamford (white labor) 6. 1902 :
Hamilton (pro-everything colored) 47, Gough
(white) 53. 1903 :W. T. White (pro-kanaka) 64,
Bamford (white) 179.
Fully a dozen lately-defeated politicians
are applying to be accredited as Australian repre-
sentative on the Imperial Defence Committee in
London. The chairman of the Committee cables
that he would prefer a Minister; but not even the
Prime Minister, were it possible for him to go,
should be allowed to commit Australia to any-
thing at the back of Parliament.
The mismanagement, of the Victorian State
irrigation works, at the instance of the people
who now form the Kyabram party, is being used
as an argument why, in N.S.W., the work of
irrigation should be given over to “ private enter-
prise.” But that, too, has conspicuously failed—
as witness Mildura. Private enterprise and dis-
honest State management have both broken
down ; the call now is for honest State manage-
ment.
Earl Grey, when in the Colonial Office,
concerned himself greatly over Australian affairs.
He recommended in one despatch to the N.S.W.
Governor that Parliament should consist of one
Chamber only. If, however, one Chamber were
not approved of, he suggested that the second
Chamber should consist of not more than eight
members, and that these should be elected by the
first Chamber.
Chief Justice Griffith’s Queensland con-
solidated criminal code gives two years gaol and
a fine of £5OO for defamation of a member of
Parliament by “a stranger.” It is a grotesque
looking item. The defamation is to be by “a
stranger” ; one member defaming another does not
come under it. If a member loses his seat and
continues to talk as he did while holding it, he is
liable to jug and fine. And both together ! Sam
Griffith’s shadow is very like Alfred Stephen’s.
The Kyabrammers of Vic. were delighted
whilst the lamentable Bent snorted and sacked
men and otherwise worked his foolish will as head
of the railways. But now, when the hungry
Kyabrammer is in a hurry to get his stuff to
market, and there is only one man at most country
stations to do three men’s work, the straws are
flying out of Kyabram’s whiskers with the vehem-
ence of his cursing of Bent and all his works. He
is quite busy raking up instances of departmental
sweating, and passing scathing resolutions about
them at shire council meetings.
Some of the superfluous energy which so
frequently inspires N.S.W. legislators to call one
another liars and perjurers might profitably
expend itself putting through an Act to prevent
Bull and Wilhelm and Sam from choking the
local markets with underweight tinned and other
package goods. A dozen reputed pounds were
weighed lately in a Sydney suburb and in each
case barely reached 14oz. The Statute called the
Weights and Measures Act applies only to
Sydney and five miles from Circular Quay. And
even within that area it passes most of its time
asleep.
Says Crozier, a fine English thinker, not
too well-known even in England :
All undue bulk is x weakness, like that excess of fat
which is a sign of degeneration rather than of develop-
ment, or that overgrowth of territory beyond the arm of
central power, which historians have noted as a real
source of debility, and a sure precursor of decline.
Crozier is a man who knows, and, furthermore,
he is a man who thinks ; and like all sane English-
men who know and think, such as Morley and
Bryce, he sees in Britain’s Imperialism, and
its habit of annexing a square mile every eight
minutes, a curse.
Cable :
Mr Balfour believed that the Imperial Defence Com-
mittee would play a useful part in promoting the closer
union of the Empire. Every part should share its labors,
including India. The other day the Canadian War
Minister (Mr. F. W. Borden) had attended a meeting of
the committee, and assisted at its deliberations. Possibly
that principle would soon be universally applied.
Yes, and Mr. Borden went away from that meet-
ing and decided that the Canadian militia should
in future have a home-grown boss, not an im-
ported Imperial officer. Australia would not fear
to be represented in Imperial Councils by men of
the Borden or Laurier stamp ; but the Bartons,
the Copelands and the John Forrests, bewildered
by the glare of Imperial gauds—they make the
difference.
THE CALICO MAH.
Trying to cotton on to another dog now.
A very large number of stockowners who
gave the Victorian Railway Department pro-
missory notes for the freight of their starving
stock have not yet paid up. The Railway Com-
missioners have given notice that all promissory
notes will be presented, for payment on Feb. 29.
G. H. Reid told an Adelaide interviewer
that the fate of the Federal Parliament is in the
hands of the Labor party, which, he said, is a
“most deplorable thing.” In a sense G.H.R.
struck it. The Federal Labor party may either
keep Premier Deakin in office, which Reid natur-
ally regards as deplorable; or it may get office
itself, which would be very deplorable for
Reid ; or it may put Reid in office, which would
be deplorable for everybody except Reid.
“G.H.” : I saw that capitalist George
Pile wrote about, who was going to invest
£16,000,052 in iron-works or brick-bats or ping-
pong sets or something. He was standing in
front of the S.M.H. office, reading the Senate
returns for all Australia, and with the £16,000,052
tied up with some bananas in a bag. When the
clerk came out and pasted up the complete re-
turns, the capitalist groaned, and made a grief-
stricken remark about the curse of Socialism.
Then he asked me for the gift of a tram-fare to
Surry Hills.
Chamberlain’s latest speech declared “ a
scientific tariff is needed to protect our home
markets and to enable us to deal on equal terms
with protected nations,” and Balfour, the same
night, declared —
If a Protectionist meant a man who thought that the
old laissez fain school of political economy was out of
date, who thought the Government could not wholly
stand aside while every other Government intervened in
industrial affairs, and who regarded with contempt the
maxim, “We must look after the consumer, but the pro-
ducer must be left to look after himself,” then he was
proud to be a Protectionist.
The Cobden joss is having a hard time in Eng-
land now.
Ex-Gov. Brassey fell off the chain at a
recent meeting of the Colonial Institute, when he
said that, when he was Governor of New South
Wales (sic— London Standard report), he had a
conference with George Turner as to whether he
would give a preference to British goods, and that
Turner had said he would like to see what
England would give in return. Brassey said he
did this in consequence of instructions from
Chamberlain, and the Colonial Secretary then
dropped the idea. Which serves to show that
Higinbotham’s old contention, of secret instruc-
tions in Australian affairs coming from Downing-
street, was true.
Some violent language has been adorning
the Melbourne Age in a discussion between
Crouch, Federal member for Corio, and S’Malkum
M'Eacharn, Consul for Japan. Crouch started
it by telling the cold truth, at Queenscliff, that the
Japanese were a greater menace to Australia than
the Russians, and that he would rather see a
victory for a white than a colored race. M ‘Eacliarn,
as a brand new consul, rushed inbreathing hard and
said Crouch was misinformed, ignorant and unpatri-
otic, and that he was setting his opinion against
that of England, which had allied with the yellow
man. Then Crouch replied that the Japanese
Consul was not a free Australian, but had to serve
his superiors and principals at.Tokio. Afterwards
the controversy drifted on to shoals of mud-
language.
G. E. Roberts, newly-elected democratic
member for Richmond (Vic.), misconceives what
should be the true aims of his party. He is
credited with telling Richmond P.L.C. :
He looked upon the village-settlement scheme as one
of the greatest dangers to the Labor movement. The
settlers in Maoriland were with the Labor party until
they found themselves prospering, and then they formed
a Farmers’ Union and opposed it. It would doubtless be
the same with the Kooweerup settlers when they reached
the same stage of affluence.
Is not the main aim of the Labor party the
betterment of the workers’ condition? Mr. Roberts
really argues in favor of keeping the workers poor,
lest they desert the democratic ranks.
Shiels’ passion for display and theatricals
in public life came up prominently when he was
conducting the affairs of Victoria in the absence
of Iceberg Irvine on a cycle tour in Tasmania. It
was his custom to wait until each department had
a large number of papers for his signature. Then,
at a very early hour in the morning, he would
order the various heads of the departments con-
cerned to be at his office together. They would
all be shown into his room, and, while one was
getting his papers signed, the others would have
to sit around, admire Shiels at work, and laugh at
his jokes. Shiels is to be opposed for the new
district of Glenelg by T. M. Burke, secretary of
the Civil Service Co-operative Society, and one
time of the Railway Department.
There, are various indications that a dry-
rot has taken hold of the N.S.W. education sys-
tem. Out of every 1000 children aged between 5
and 15, Maoriland has 665 attending school;
Queensland has 605; Victoria, 546; S. Australia,
489; N.S.W. has only 469. The number of
ohildren attending school in N.S.W. actually
decreased by nearly 200 between 1900 and 1902,
while the number of teachers increased by 338.
N.S.W. was the only State where the sohool
attendance fell off.
Just before Vic. Parliament was prorogued
a new measure was put through giving shire
councils further powers to insist on cleanliness in
dairies. But the enforcing of the regulations is
permissive. When the measure was under dis-
cussion Kyabram cried that in some districts the
farmers were so poor that to compel them to keep
clean spelled ruin; and Parliament foolishly
agreed to a clause whioh leaves it to each muni-
cipal council to decide whether it will keep clean
or remain dirty : i.e., whether it will adopt the
new Act or not. As the majority of the muni-
cipalities did not take advantage of the old Acts,
it can be taken for granted that they will now
unanimously decide to continue dirty.
“ Exile ” writes from Inverell:
A big estate, containing a large proportion of good
land, and within reasonable distance of the town, has
been offered to the Government under the new Closer
Settlement Act. Surveyors have been appointed to
make the necessary valuations, and their reports will be
submitted to the Local Land Board as the Act provides.
Meanwhile, another estate, nearly as extensive and
suitable, has been purchased by a Sydney syndicate, and
has been cut up with a view to offering it in small areas
for sale. As it is common talk that at least two mem-
bers of the Ministry were also members of this syndicate,
I want to know what the Government’s little game is.
Either property will supply the present demand for
dairy farms, and it becomes rather an interesting ques-
tion whether the Government is really going to take
advantage of the Act, or whether some of its members
are merely going to look after their own interests.
N.S.W. Opposition-leader Joseph plays
the Oily Gammon part to perfection ; welcoming
his new Illawarra follower in the daily papers:
Mr. Allen will be a credit to the electorate, and a
worthy member of the Assembly. He has developed
into an effective platform speaker, proving himself to be
a student of politics, well versed in the facts surrounding
all public questions. He adopted a high tone in his
addresses, which won for him the respect even of his
opponents.
And every member, or prospective member, of
Joseph’s party is just as good, or better, according
to Joseph. The Liberal and Deform party is, in
fact, a herd of saints under the charge of the-
holiest cow-boy that ever was.
The tin god of the Calico Jimmy, the
obsolete prophet of Frgetrade, Cobden, in writing
of his fetish :
You might as well doubt that the sun will rise to-
morrow as doubt that in ten years every civilised nation
on earth will have followed our Freetrade example.
Cobden has been dead now for 38 years. The-
world is vastly more Protectionist now than it
was when he died. Britain has lost its manu-
facturing supremacy and begins to talk of taking
to Protection to recover it. History has proved
Cobden a false prophet of the worst description,
and yet to this day interested Calico Jimmyism
quotes him as an oracle. Looking at the above
statement, what a leak there is in the oracle !

AUSTRALIA: “ Gee-whizz! if I didn’t think you
wuz dead this long time.”
Some linked facts :
1. A Foreign-trade Government, to obtain the support
of the Sydney dailies, agrees to carry their bulk parcels
of papers free on the Government railways and tramways.
2. Twenty-seven years later. Labor member Griffith
gets the Assembly to appoint a Select Committee to
investigate the matter.
3. As chairman of that committee he (Griffith) pre-
sented to Parliament a report something as follows :
“ That the practice of carrying free on the State railways
the merchandise of the Sydney newspaper proprietors,
was illegal, being based on a mere Cabinet minute which;
had never received Executive sanction. That the rail-
way by-laws enacted a legal rate for bulk parcels of-
newspapers (i.e., quarter of parcels rates), that this rats
should be immediately enforced, and that it would yield.;
a revenue of £lO,OOO a year to the Kailway Commis-
sioners.”
4. The Government, braving the hostility of the Syd-
ney dailies, enforced the payment of this £lO,OOO a year.
5. Griffith throws up his seat in the N.S.W. Parliament
to stand for the Senate.
6. The Sydney press tells the people to vote Griffith
down, and they do it.

The elevation of J. H. Gordon to the S.A.
Bench breaks up a legal firm remarkable for its
personal successes. Within ten years Gordon has
been made K.C. and now Judge ; his brother
James was police magistrate while still a young
man, and Paris Nesbit, K.C. The old firm is now
completely at an end, Bright (Gordon’s brother-
in-law) and J. ft'. Anderson (his son-in-law)
having agreed to practise separately, and the
firm’s managing clerk—with an offer of a partner-
ship from each of them—having just made a fatal
mistake with some carbolic acid in the dark.

the fiery dragon.
F. K. Griffiths, the man who 30 years ago
had to do much of the cleaning-up, after the
collapse of Northern Territory mining and
land boom left S. Australia with a worse mess on
hand than it ever had before or since, died a few
days back. The fever and ague which he con-
tracted up there took years to kill him, but did it
at last. Walter Griffiths, the bright youngster
who for a while was M.P. for the Territory but
died suddenly, was his son.
A Sydney music-publishing firm recently
received from the lady secretary of an up-country
church choir a request for “ a book of voluntaries
suitable for playing while the collection is being
lifted.”
The sea air does not agree with the ancient
headstones removed from the old I)evonshire-
st reet cemeteries. At La Perouse the stones are
flaking, and in a few years the inscriptions will
have disappeared.

Some 22 years ago Lord Kelvin stated his belief
that the supremacy of the steam engine as a motor would
goon be challenged by the internal combustion engine
operated either by gas or oil. It has. Foremost among
the challengers stands the Campbell Gas Engine Co.,
Ltd., of Halifax, Yorkshire, England, whose products
are to be found in every part of the globe where men
use engines. Their oil engines are of special interest to
pastoralists, farmers and fruitgrowers in the Common-
wealth, and are made in various types. The ordinary
horizontal or vertical pattern, suitable for driving any
kind of fixed machinery. They are combined with
pumps for irrigation or water supply, and for winding
and hauling. The “CAMPBELL” Portable Oil Engine
is supplied either light or heavy ; the heavy type, for use
where water is scarce, is fitted with a cooling tower,
wherein constant circulation is kept up and water loss
reduced to a minimum. The light type is for use where
water can be drawn from a stream or tank and returned
thereto after passing through the cylinder jacket. Both
these types can be used to operate absolutely any kind
of machinery, from sheep shearing to electric lighting.
They will work with ordinary kerosene oil from 120” to
250" F. test. They are very reliable and easily man-
aged. They have only two valves, and are simplicity
itself. They maintain even speed, with all variations of
load, and the oil consumption never exceeds f-pint per
horse-power per hour. There are many other advantages,
which will be cheerfully explained by Mr. R. A. Hervey,
corner Clarence and King streets, Sydney, who is the
agent in N.S.W.*

engineers that by the use of the
Spiral Steam Tube Cleaner Co.’s

in cleaning in a few minutes as
against hours by the old method
without wear or tear on the tubes.
No up-to-date firm or corporation
using steam should be without this
appliance ; as an economiser of fuel
it pays for itself in a few months, and
the marvellous rapidity and effective-

Why run risks of boiler
explosions? Loss of time
in long passages, etc., etc.?

“ Encyclopaedia Britannica ” points out
that there is a city named Centralia, with about
8000 people, in Illinois, U.S. This name has
been suggested for the Australian capital, and
also for a new Central Australian province. There
is a district called Austrasia in Germany, having
borne that name long before Australia was dis-
covered.
“Eiddal,” from a Yic. agricultural dis-
trict :
Just passed down the street, on the nag that used to
take all the prizes in the 12st. hunter sections (must be
■close on 30 years old now), the one-time prize-farrner of
the district. Fourteen years ago his name was a house-
hold word throughout the State ; now he is only Old Bill.
At every show in the district this man was the terror of
■exhibitors in his favorite sections—the best bag of wheat
and all the butter classes. He has medals and certificates
by the score. His neighbors were interested in seeing
the prize for the best wheat and butter come to their
■district. After attending to their business first, they
would step over of an evening during the slack months
and give the cocky and his family a hand at “going
over ” 20 or 30 bags of grain, to pick out the plumpest
grains with which to make up the prize sample, or bag,
or to give the women a turn at the churn (when their
own butter was on the road to market, not before).
They knew that the old man’s success advertised
the whole district, and made its produce fetch
better prices. They made money, while the
other man accumulated medals and certificates.
Then when the rabbits came they could fight the pest—
they had influence and money I Old exhibitor was nearly
always away from home, with members of the family,
during the show season. All he made in prize-money
went in expenses, and more. Then the labor-saving
machinery, that does away to some extent with personal
skill, came along. Old exhibitor didn’t do so well then.
About this time the mortgagee began to knock at the
door. So the exhibiting game had to slide; there was
nothing for the family to do but ask the old chap to take a
back-seat, while they took up his responsibilities and the
mortgage. Now mother and father live in the memories
of the good old “ prize ” days—for they have only the
medals and the memories to call their own—and the
horse ; that’s dad’s, but he, too, is a “ light of other
days 1"

COLINTON is composed of a variety of sloping well-grassed ridges, with a
splendid percentage of Luoerne Flats, and has long been known as one of the Warmest
Winter Runs, as well as one of the beat fastening Runs in Southern Queensland.
COXiINTON la magnificently watered by 18 miles double frontage
to tbe Brisbane River, as well as by upwards of 48 miles of Per-
manent Creeks.
COLINTON has been kept free from pests such as lantana, thistle, and
burrs, and there is no nut grass and, above all, no ticks.

The Monaro Man.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Thr Monaro is a hospitable land ; true, poison is
aid for tea-agents and book canvassers, but they
are accounted outside the human race. A Monaro
squatteroo casually told me of how lie rode down
to a homestead one twilight, to hear the owner
explain to a stranger that the township was only
two miles further on and the road quite good ;
and of how he had for a moment loathing in his
heart for this inhospitable fellow who would send
the stranger away from his gates as the shadows
deepened into darkness, when the hearths
should glow for house-father and unbid-
den guest alike. But, looking closer, he
saw that the man sent forward was a book-
agent, and knew that the law of hospitality
had not been broken. But it is the law in
Monaro—food for the stranger and his horse—
though some few are outside the law. A man
with a buggy drawing up to a Monaro homestead
al ways takes his horses out. He knows that he
will be asked to take refreshment, and he wants
his nags, too, to have a bit of grass.
Wonderful distances will these Monaro horses
travel with no food but the grass the country
provides—rich sustaining grass which never comes
to the luxurious rankness of the out-west herbage
in a rainy season, but practically always offers an
honest nibble to stock. The Monaro nag, one
notices, is always ready for a nibble. Give him a
loose rein for a moment while you look around at
the scenery, and he slides over to a grass patch
for a mouthful or two. He is accustomed to
travel long spells without a rest, and experience
has taught him to feed whenever he can.
They are tall folk on the Monaro mostly, and
tell tall stories. Swopping kelpie yarns, this
cropped up. “Buckley Tom had a blanky fine
kelpie, could do anything with sheep. But
what made Tom the gratefullest fellow going in
regard to that bloomin’ dog was when he
went down to Sydney once. He brought the
kelpie with him and put it up in the back yard.
The boss of the pub. says as how it couldn’t sleep
in his bedroom. Well, Tom found they needn’t
have been so blanky particular about their bed-
room, for ’twas just swarmin’ with insects ; and he
couldn’t sleep a wink for them until he thinks of
the kelpie an’ whistles for it. That dog came in
and set to work, and in half a bloomin’ hour he
had every man-jack of those insects yarded into
the bedroom basin and kep’ ’em there while Tom
slept comfortable. It was a darned knowin’ dog,
I tell you.”
Ihe Monaro man doesn’t come to Sydney very
often. . The distance is great and the journey
expensive. I told of the man who wanted to
know “ who kept the pub. down in Sydney now,”
a nd was told “That’s not so curious. Why,
there’s —— ; he hasn’t been to Sydney since
45, and I’ll swear there wasn’t many pubs, there
then. And ” (pointing to a man apparently 40,
really 70, frisking about) “when he was in Syd-
ney he used to walk across the fields to school
where Crown-street and Riley-street are now.”
You meet Monaro folk who can recall when
Boydtown was a. sort of rival to Port Jackson as
a seaport. The inhabitants have a habit of living
long _ and lustily. One old Monaro man, Mr.
Jardine, of Curry Flat, was born the same year as
Queen Victoria, and he can walk up a big hill
how, or go out on horseback after cattle with the
mere youngsters of 60 or so. Austin Chapman,
Who represents the district in the Australian
Parliament, relates that when Bombala wished to
have a cemetery it was found that there must be
at least one burial as a preliminary to dedication.
After waiting weary years for someone to die—
Until, indeed, the situation got on the nerves of
the oldest inhabitant—a corpse was finally im-
ported from some less healthy locality. The men
and the stories of Monaro are tall.
It’s a patch of old Australia bright with the
nne, hearty hospitality of the early days, is the
Monaro. Even the banker there becomes a man
and a brother. Still, there is about the average
amount of human nature in the Monaro man
when it comes to a horse deal. He is not a saint,
but he’s an Australian to the marrow. Trout
fishing in the Moombah River one day, I caught
just a couple, and the Monaro men thought there
wasn t enough fun in that, and wanted me to land
the fish they had hooked. (This is not a “snake.”
It s hard to believe, but it’s true.) The man who
will let a guest have the glory of his fish catch
surpasses the traditional Arab in hospitality.
The Monaro man marries the Monaro girl
pretty frequently. Driving up from Eden to
Bombala one day I struck a honeymoon party—
the man, the girl, a buggy, a horse, and two small
trunks. Heard the man had about 255. a week
income. He evidently also had courage; and so
had the girl. But the actual landowner, small or
large, is almost always prosperous, and he marries
early and populates the district; there is no birth-
rate problem on the Monaro. Still, of late, the
fact that there is no land open for agricultural
settlement is making paterfamilias wonder what
he is going to do with his boys.
Make him a Federal territory citizen, give
him the chance of cultivating his fine dis-
intensely and getting to a market —the
chance which the N.S.W. State Government
has never given—and the Monaro man will provide
a firm backbone for Australia. His bracing
climate and his great breezy downs free him from
the incubus of That Tired Feeling. He would
give the best of soldiers, sailors and farmers to the
Commonwealth.
F. R.
Latest medical ukase is “aseptic silence f>
—a surgeon should keep his mouth shut whilst
operating, lest bacilli should escape with his
words.
The French Government has declared for
the total suppression of all teaching by religious
orders, and has a majority of the Chamber of

“ Little Mary ” : Have just finished read-
ing three lurid accounts of Nelson’s woman of
pleasure, Lady Hamilton. Have come to theoyni-
cally sad conclusion that, from Eve unto the end,
the wanton shakes the world.
In Cretan excavations Dr. Evans has
discovered several statuettes 4000 years old, which
delineate precisely the arrangement, to the
minutest detail, of the muscles and surface veins
of the Cretan arm of those days. These are the
earliest of all records of human anatomy and they
are perfect in detail. Four thousand years of
evolution have not effected the minutest change
in the structure of the human arm.
“ Petramos ” : I live about 300 yards from
the penny tram section (Adelaide). There is a stout,
sensible lady living near me, who has three children
and rheumatics. She always takes two penn’orth
of tram herself, but, when the three youngsters are
with her, she bundles them out at the penny
section, and they run alongside until mamma
alights at her front gate. It doesn’t take a pro-
fessional ped. to keep up with an Adelaide horse
car.
A Sydney man complains :
How’s this for “ fits of abstraction" by postal officers?
Have a relative in Fiji who subscribed to Tub Bulletin
for two years, but, as he only got a copy about once
every three months, concluded to “ chuck ” it. I took a
hand, and here is 12 months’ record : Bulletins posted,
52 ; delivered, 11. Magazines posted, unregistered, 17 ;
delivered, 5. Registered articles turn up about a month
late, while books and small articles such as ties, studs,
etc., disappear as completely as the political honesty of
Yes-No. _ No doubt where fault lies, as Bulletins posted
to M.L. invariably reach addresses. Now send B. care-
fully hidden within a S.M. Herald. Fijian postal
depravity evidently has its limits—it does not descend to
stealing a Herald.
NATURAL MODESTY.
The natural modesty of many people who have
used Chamberlain’s Colio and Diarrhoea Remedy
for Diarrhoea, Dysentery, etc., with splendid
results, makes them hesitate about giving their
opinion for publicity. Still these same people
have done much to make Chamberlain’s Colic and
Diarrhoea Remedy a household word, for they
have by word of mouth informed their friends
what a good medicine it is to have in the house.
Once used, always kept, follows this medicine
around the world.*
A theatre-burning is, of course, only a
one-horse show compared with the great fires on
the globe. Perhaps the Chicago fire of 1871 was
the largest ever known. The loss was set down
at £39,000,000. Next may be placed the Nero
fire of Rome, a.d. 64. It burnt for eight days,
destroying 10 out of the 14 city wards. Modern
historians doubt whether Nero set it ablaze, and
he appears to have endeared himself to the popu-
lace by activity in helping to quench it. The
third fire on the list may be that of London, in
1666. The fire of the Paris Commune, 1871, was
not one of the most extensive, but it involved the
appalling loss of £32,000,000. The Moscow fire,
of Napoleon’s invasion, 1812, is set down as having
cost £30,000,000. Other expensive fires were at
Boston in 1872 with £15,000,000, Hamburg in 1842
with £7,000,000, and Constantinople in 1870 with
£5,000,000. The Chicago fire is said to have been
caused by Mrs. O’Leary’s cow kicking over a
kerosene lamp.
“ Hiphen ” paints another picture :
If “ Athos ” (B. 7/l/’O4) wishes to see the Melbourne
“ bloke ” on a Sunday morning, let him cautiously enter
any metropolitan or suburban park, creep quietly along
the fence, and he will behold the “ lad” at his busiest,
brightest and best, exalted in his Sunday “ clobber,” en-
thralled in “ two-up.” (“ Athos” may also get a prod in
the jaw, being mistaken for a “ D.”, but that will be a
small price to pay for wisdom.) If, however, the gentle-
man does not fancy that, let him select any quiet, dingy
pub, in any quiet, dingy side street ; and let him knock
at the door three times and blow gently through his
teeth. A touselled head will appear over the back fence,
gaze at him suspiciously for a moment, then, if he looks
sufficiently like a “good bloke,” he will be allowed to
enter into a darkened, fly-blown parlor off the bar, where
he will find the persons he accuses of “ listlessly leaning
against the corner,” drinking beer and playing euchre.
Also, “Athos” says the river Yarra shares the Sunday
stagnant calm. Untrue ! Never is the Yarra busier. It
is invaded by myriads of scantily-clad youths from five
different rowing clubs, who lounge about the bank
smoking and talking to the girls, and who call themselves
oarsmen. lam one of them, so I know.
“ L ” writes on some unpleasant diseases :
Re par 13/8/'O3 as to leprosy being contagious. Dr.
Ashburton Thompson, president N.S.W. Board of Health,
tells a yarn about a monastery, somewhere on the slopes
of the Alps, devoted to the care of lepers. For one of the
monks to contract the disease would be for him a good
title to a front seat up above, and for the monastery a
splendid advt. ; so many of the monks do their level best
to catch leprosy, wearing the clothes and sleeping in the
beds of the lepers. But although the monastery is
somewhere in its second century of this work, there is
no record of any monk being infected. The tendency of
the public is to greatly exaggerate the chances of
infection. If their ideas were correct, doctors and
nurses would spend a very short life, and that in bed.
When the plague was on, one of the doctors employed to
visit suspected persons went to examine a girl, the
daughter of a small dairyman. The case was manifestly
plague, and he was just leaving when the father
staggered in with a full cargo of liquor on board.
Informed of the necessity of the girl’s removal to
quarantine, he told the doctor it was a lot of crimson
rot. He had the plague himself, but took good care
that it did not interfere with his business. The doctor,
on examination, found that he really had the plague.
But he was convalescent, and had evidently passed the
crisis of the disease whilst delivering milk and drinking.
T.P.’s Weekly summarises Labouchere’s
oharacter:—
Rich, he despises the wealthy ; clever, without judg-
ment ; unsentimental, yet wrong-headedly emotional ;
thoroughly in earnest, yet always regarded as a trifler.
He used to give out that he really had no time to get
married, and yet he espoused Miss Henrietta Hodson, an
actress of some note in her day ; and he has managed a
theatre, from which he gained a good many amusing ex-
periences, without any monetary profit. He has played
many parts, but has never achieved success in any parti-
cular one.

“In a big audience sampling ‘ The Country
Girl’ on Saturday night none showed brighter
lustre than Miss Edith Sutton, out from Africa
tor a jaunt before settling to matrimony. She
was in sunset pink and cloud-white chiffon, making
as pretty and Frenchy a confection as possible.
One of the stall occupants, same night, was an
ornamental young grace, whose trappings were
wonderfully fine. Her swan neck reared out of a
black-and-gold frock, and supported a coy head
with a towering erection of golden thatch. Lan-
guidly she waved a fan, perfumed and laden with
roses. Blushingly she talked with her escort —
one of the best-known racing identities in Victoria.
“ A word of warning to the girl of the gossamer .
Once securely enveloped in this charm-enhancing
face drape, let her remain in it. The removal of
the drape at mid-time reveals a woeful sight.
The confined breath, acting as a vapor bath,
steams and lards the face, so that her neighbors
discover the soft peach-bloom to have been a sad
delusion. Some leave the ends of the gossamer
flying, and this gives the fresh air a show, but the
effect is nothing to that produced by the gossamer
that closes up and lends its enchanting haze to
the fair countenance.
“ They’re talking in Paris of the approach of
the hoop skirt. Victoria seems safe from the
crinoline as long as the Bent system of railway
running lasts. Since there’s scarcely room for a
skimpy skirt fold, or superfluous sneeze, in any of
the morning or evening trains from. Suburbia,
there’s no chance of the hoop-la fashion coming
here.
“It’s just as well Ada Crossley’s Australian
pilgrimage is near an end; the presentations,
en route, which she seems to consider herself
bound to wear, are beginning to weigh down her
svelte figure. At the first Town Hall concert an
absence of all gee-gaws impressed itself on the
public mind. Then came the musicians’ long-
tailed lyre-bird, and that at once crowned the
singer’s chablis-colored hair. Other gifts were
added, and yet others, and on the damp day of
departure all were sported on Ada’s person, to
the joy of the givers.

—who has come here for an interesting domestic
event —attends all the shows in magnificent attire
with her husband in attendance and a nurse
within coo-ee.
“Sojourners at a big hotel are tickled at a pair
of picturesque mummers whose manners are bor-
rowed from the book they learn their lines from.
The morning is never too bilious to affect their
grand air of superfine fellowship. The practice is
for the male person to dawdle about the lounge
until my lady appears. She eschews the lift, as
the hydraulic shocks are opposed to the aesthetics,
and takes the stairs. As she descends, he comes
forward, sweeps his hat off, and, advancing, takes
her hand, bowing over it to an inch of the
knuckles. Then he resumes his hat, and the pair
talk in polished phrases, posturing the while
before cynical hall porters who know too much
even to smile. The superior mummer is a
curiosity. Two of the kind, pledged as twin
souls, make a whole museum.
“A globe-trotter just returned from abroad
tells of the unchanging nature of the Celestial.
He visited in the country of the Pigtail an old Uni-
versity fellow-student who went through a suc-
cessful Arts course in this State, and had all the
manners, habits, and tastes of his ‘shop’ chums
in those student days. But since returning to his
home among the yellow men he has resumed the
native customs, the native dress, the native
thought, and is as true a Chinaman as any. The
same globe-trotter marked how different is the
effect of travel on the little Jap. He is naturally
assimilative, drops into English manners readily,
and returns to keep them in daily use.
“The illustrious spouse of King Cole (of the
Book Arcade) has much to say about the weird
conditions of life in British New Guinea, where
she ‘touched’ on the journey to Japan. At a
little coastal village, she found her arm seized by
a warrior chief. The captain of the ship (who was
acting as cicerone) ordered the local potentate to
‘ Let go ! ’ The warrior did as requested at the
moment, but soon repeated his grab of the lady’s
substantial limb, smiling the while. ‘ What do
you mean ?’ said the captain. The warrior bowed
his crested head and replied, ‘ Good eating ! ’
Then the gallant cap. talked firearms and cold
steel to the gentleman with few clothes and many
feathers, and the coveted arm was squeezed no
more. This is Mrs. Cole’s story ; and she’s still
anxious to have the black man as a brother.
“Miss Maud Milton as Maria in ‘Twelfth
Night ’ flaunted a hearty laugh. It is the laugh
that only the charmer with teeth sound, strong
and pearly to the very wisdom stump, can give.
How few women may laugh ! In these times of
exquisitely fine dental performances—the crowned
molar, the gold bridge, and such triumphs—the
feminine mouth is re-arranged, the adopted aids
bring about a different bite, a different expres-
sion, and a different smile. Woman laughs in
brackets, never in a full, flowing, rounded period
—like this Maria in Shakspeare’s comedy.
Yours affectionately,
AKENEHI.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
The Times man’s estimate of the relative merits
ol Australian and English bowling looks very foolish in
the light of the Trumper-Hill-Duff performance on the
first day at Adelaide. Up to the fall of Trumper’s wicket
just before I o’clock, the English bowling had been “ col-
lared” all the time—not merely pottered with, as the
Englishmen pottered with the Australian bowling on
New Year’s Day—but “ collared.” The batsmen never
showed any fear of it—2Bo runs was the T.-H.-D. aggregate
last Friday. The same three batsmen, in their second
innings of the Ist test, scored 320 for the loss of two
wickets, Trurnper carrying out his bat and Hill being run
out. Granting that Trurnper and Hill are the two best
in the world, and Duff is as good as any of the others, it
needs the lynx eye of a far-off Times man to discover
“ undoubted superiority ” in bowling which gets fairly
pasted on a hard wicket, and which was proved not quite
as deadly all-round as the Australian bowling on a sod-
den pitch.
* * * •
The third test match has at last shown the
Australian team in something like its true form. In the
first match the Australian representatives were obviously
suffering from That Tired Feeling or the Don’t Worry
Spirit. In the second match conditions were too damp
to allow of a true test. After England had batted
on a good wicket, Australia had to splash along in the mud
to certain defeat. But in the match in Adelaide the luck,
if anything, was on the side of Australia. The game
opened with weather and wicket perfect. Noble won the
toss for the first time, and sent in Duff and Trurnper.
Duff shaped best, scoring 79 with ease and grace.
Trumper’s usual brilliancy was clouded over, and he
“ played the game ” as Warner’s team has taught it.
Duff more than doubled Australia’s champion batsman’s
figures while he stayed. The partnership was in striking
contrast with the Britishers’ play in the second test. The
day’s total of 3.55 for 6 wickets was poorer than the first
partnership promised, but was well up with fastest
scoring records. .
The second day the remaining four Australians
vanished, leaving only 33 behind them, and the innings
was over for 388—exactly the score made by the
Englishmen on same ground two years before. The
Englishmen found the wicket too fast for them, and the
Australian bowling and fielding baffled them. At close
of day they had lost 8 for 199. Their exhibition was
cramped and slow. Kelly and M'Leod made sensational
catches, Kelly getting Tyldesley off a leg glance when
the Lancastrian had started to run. Tyldesley was
momentarily paralysed with sheer astonishment. M’Leod
secured Warner at mid-on close in. The only English-
man to master bowling was Hirst, who, like Duff and
Trurnper previous day, fell to the last ball before adjourn-
ment. Hopkins bowled splendidly, getting 3 for 52 with
deliveries that swerved in a fashion that was very
puzzling.
Third day (Monday) the remaining four
Britishers were disposed of inside an hour, having added
46 to previous day’s total, which left the side 143 to the
bad. Australians opened more cautiously than in their
first essay, and this time Duff did the waiting while
Trurnper piled the runs. With the score at 48 Duff (14)
was caught, and 81 saw Trurnper out l.b.w. ; he had been
at work ljh. for 59, and notched 7 fours. Hill occupied
half-an-hour making 9. Just over the century further
trouble came, Hill being clean bowled by a fast one for
16. Gregory followed. Noble had made 38 when
Lilley missed him badly off Bosanquet. Two
hundred showed in 2h. 55m. At this period Gregory
was shaping perhaps as well as ever in his
career- cutting, leg glancing, and hooking in fine
style and amid continued plaudits. Noble and Gregory
were together at 32, but by the time the side’s figures
reached 227 Gregory had scored 61 to his skipper’s 11,
and the pair had the bowling completely collared.
Gregory’s hundred appeared in lh. 55m., and, a minute
later, Noble made the half century, 2h. 12m. from the
opening of his innings. Five minutes before stumps
draw Gregory’s brilliant effort closed. He miss-hit
Braund, and was easily held by Rhodes. Gregory’s share
of the 263 for four wickets was 112, which included
seventeen fours—all without the semblance of a chance.
Day’s play left Australia with a lead of 406.
* » * »
On Tuesday the Australian side again showed
that it had a tail. With an overnight score of 263 for
four wickets the innings total was only 351. Noble and
Armstrong were the only men to bat at all well. The
Englishmen were thus left with just on 500 runs to get to
win, and as The Bulletin went to press they were facing
that apparently hopeless task.
* * * *
A. E. Johns told a ’Tisek interviewer in
Adelaide that he had “ the best of reasons to
know ” that, in future, test matches in Eng-
land will be played to a finish.
Melbourne C.C. people should do
as is done in Sydney when play is doubt-
ful-exhibit notices that it is so, and that
people paying at the gate enter on the
distinct understanding that no money
will be refunded. This would remove
any real grounds for complaint.
It was Malcolm M'Eaoharn who hatched the
idea of offering a 250-guinea urn “ for the ashes in inter-
national cricket.” He didn’t do the thing at first openly.
The Cricket Association sensibly declined the offer.
* * * *
The proved importance of the bowling of
Arnold and Bosanquet to the Englishmen when the
wicket was good in the Ist and 3rd tests shows how lucky
the visitors were to get Australia on a wet wicket in the
2nd test, when Arnold and Bosanquet were unable to
play.
* * * *
Maclaren made an awful “bloomer” in pre-
dicting that Braund would prove the most serviceable
of the English bowlers in the test matches.
♦ * * *
Batting champion Vic. Trumper lost a little
sister last Saturday. She was blind from her birth, and
had never sat up or spoken.
« * * *
Trumper’s innings in third test brought up his
total runs in tests this season to 467, which pans out
at an average of 93'4 runs per innings—a record which is
far the best of -the Australians. In six visits to the
creases he has scored two centuries ; and by consistently
piling up three'-figure scores has earned a distinction
(with Englishman Maclaren) of being the only player to
have accumulated four centuries in test matches.
* * * *
“ M.” : On a wet or damaged wicket English
bowler Rhodes is a phenomenon, as witness his 15 wickets
for 124 runs in the wet, slushy second test. But on a
good, true, hard wicket Rhodes is not seen to such
advantage, and Captain Warner, wise in his generation,
never keeps him trundling on such a wicket very long.
The captain appears to be nursing Rhodes so that he will
go home with a good average, or to prevent a break-
down, which, as Rhodes is a professional cricketer, would
mean a serious loss. In eleven-a-side matches Rhodes’
average is 14.10 for 40 wickets.
» * * *
There’s plainly heaps of coin for the promoters
in the Sydney bike spec.- Attendance Thursday, Satur-
day and Monday nights last easily exceeded 40,000. On
Thursday in the opening heat of the Furracabad Mile
Handicap Mutton rose to the form of a Taylor or a Law-
son and made the sprint of his life, beating Goodson
and Yankee MacFarland just when the race looked sure
for or.e or t'other. The third heat saw Taylor, streaking
remarkably, overhaul a hot bunch inside a quarter of a
lap, and win a length from Farley in 1.57 2-5, an out-of-
the-way fine feat. Farley (60yds.) gathered the final
brilliantly from Don Walker (iOyds.), ’Murkan Dowling
(10yds.) and Taylor (fourth). Concluding run of the
International Test first distance (half mile) brought the
four fastest riders (Taylor, Lawsqn, MacFarland
and Walker) of the day together. All of the
quartet had it all out from “go,” and a high tension
race saw Taylor blocked so badly entering the straight
that he had to drop back and come on the out-
side. Bumping occurred, and Lawson appeared to crowd
the black down on to the grass fringe of the track, where
he ran fully 30 yards, and had not a chance in conse-
quence. MacFarland won, a length to the good, from
Lawson, with Taylor third, and Walker fourth. Both
white Americans were vigorously hooted. Subsequently,
the event was declared no race. Later Lawson won the
Five Miles Scratch magnificently from 17 others; he
caught Taylor near the finish, and beat him by a length,
with Mutton close up, third, in world’s record competi-
tion time of 10.37 2-5. The last lap occupied 31 2-ssec.
The same throng that hooted Lawson a minute or two
before now cheered him excitedly. N.S.W. man Mutton
made a wonderfully speedy dash over the last lap.
MacFarland “punctured,” and had to retire.
Saturday night’s bike sports proceedings bristled
with interest, and were often sensational. Big feature
was the four mile distance of International Test, wherein
Lawson and Taylor met. Tussle proved an exceedingly
fast one, each mile being traversed in just over 2min.
The last lap was a staggerer. MacFarland, with Lawson
on his wheel, shot out. Lawson, in the circumstances,
had the winning position, for MacFarland is a remarkable
pacer. When Taylor found an opening he made a bril-
liant effort, and got his feet going at a rare clip. Mean
while MacFarland had fairly run the other* off
their legs. At the members’ gate Lawson dashed
for the tape amid an uproar. The negro was fairly
flying four lengths away, but with only 100yds.
to go his task seemed absolutely impossible. Foot by
foot he crept nearer to the leader. MacFarland and
Downing were passed as if standing, and, though Law-
son finished at top gait and brilliantly, the colored man
landed the honors on the post—a wonderful effort that
brought thousands to their feet. Lawson and MacFar-
land refused to line up for the re-run half-mile distance
of the International Test, and Taylor and Walker had it
between them. The Australian never did better. After
the pacer withdrew the two raced locked together.
Rounding the bend they toiled dead level for the tape,
and fairly hurled themselves over the line. Hundreds
thought Taylor just pipped his man, while a big section
placed Walker first, but three judges agreed it was a
dead heat. In one of the big mile battles Taylor beat
B. Rolfe and Don Walker in 1.63 3-5, thus creating a new
world’s record.
The sport continued Monday night. Melbourne
Handicap (lm.) saw Glencross win his heat in 1.57 3-5,
while D. Harvison got home in 1.58. Ivor Lawson made
a bold bid for the fourth, and put in a wonderful lap and
a quarter, but had to retire half a lap from the end, and
small wonder, for winner, E. Payne (with 90yds. start)
threw the trip behind in 1.51. Taylor won his heat
splendidly in 1.56 4-5, and could have done seconds better
had it been wanted. Floyd MacFarland also scored, the
watch making him do 1.54 2-5—4-ssee. outside Taylor's
world’s best, brought into existence Saturday night.
For the final 12 stripped—Taylor and MacFarland being
scratch, with Mutton (50yds.) nearest of the others.
’Murkan couple were with the front lot entering the last
lap. MacFarland went round the field with Taylor on
his wheel, and 100yds. from home the issue was between
them, but pacing had taken too much out of the black,
who couldn’t fight it with his man at the end and was
beaten by over a length in 1.55.
* * * *
Cyclist Taylor may be criticised as a sanctimonious
cycle-buster who feels the finger of Heaven whirling
his pedals round a bit faster when the enemy gets
in front, but he’s always a favorite with the Sydney
crowd. Men and women shout for the colored
brother, and at the slightest appearance of block-
ing him, howl like wolves. At present, one of the
most popular fixtures that could be made would be
a pursuit race between Taylor and Lawson for a substan-
tial prize. It certainly should be a first-class contest, and
would settle a large and very vexed public question. At
present, it is impossible to drink one’s cocktail in dreamy,
meditative peace, owing to the wrangle between men in
silk waistcoats and pot-hats as to which is the better
of the two Yankees, and in the public interest the matter
should be placed beyond all doubt.
* * * •
Harry Parker, who won the Maoriland tennis
championship, at Napier, a few weeks back, is a small,
alert, nervous, little chap, who plays with specs, before
the eyes. His picking up and line playing are simply
marvellous when he is in proper form. Parker will com-
pete at the South Australian championship meeting in
February, and also, if there is time, in the Tasmanian.
AN AMUSING GAME.
The Petriana affair forgotten, the D.T. falls
hack on baiting John See.
Lobethal, a mostly-Grerman village in the hills
behind Adelaide, has a shoot for a “ kingship ” every
summer, and latterly it has attracted the attention of
the State’s best rifle-shots. J. T. Lake, one of the chief
of them, has just won the kingship for the third time,
but has made himself locally unpopular by refusing to
walk at the head of a procession wearing a laurel crown
and other fripperies. The committee is uncertain
whether to obtain a guarantee from every future com-
petitor that if he becomes a king he will behave as
foolishly as a king should, or to restrict the shooting to
Teutons, who could be depended upon to frivol as desired.
» * • *
Jack Perryman, Bometime Australian champion
lightweight wrestler, whom Dan Stewart (M’Leod) threw
out years ago, wrestled Gunga Brahm at Perth (W.A.)
recently. The white man made a good showing in a
stuffy house, and bumped Gunga on to two shoulders in
2min. 15sec. But the next two falls went to the chutnee
man in 2m. 20s. and 10m. 14s. respectively. Pair will meet
again with Perryman in better condition.
* * * $
Australia’s wonderful swimmer, Dick Cavill,
chipped 3-5 seconds off his own world’s record for the
100yds., and left it 58 secs, dead, last Saturday at Sydney,
in a specially arranged match with Island swimmer Wick-
ham. They left the platform simultaneously, but Cavill
rose two yards ahead. Wickham passed him, and at the
45 wa3 leading by two yards, but at 90 yards Cavill was
again in front, and he finished a yard to the good, with
Wickham a second behind him. Same day Cavill took
5 4-5 secs, off his own time for 500 yards (6.31), winning
from end to end. Another speedy display same day was
Cecil Healy’s win of the “ Hundred ” final in 59 4-5 secs
* * * *
English billiardist, H. Stevenson, who recently
toured this continent, didn’t take long to strike form at
’Ome. Playing Harverson last month and conceding
2000 in 9000, he made a break of 703 and won with 2290
in hand ; he afterwards challenged Dawson for the world’*
championship.
* * * *
Sydney bowlers’ competition for the club
pennant reached an interesting stage Saturday, when
third round was decided, and left City, Victoria Park,
and Chatswood the only clubs of 16 original competitors
without a defeat. Gladstone Park and Parramatta
dropped out, and Redfern, Waverley, St. George, Peter*
sham, and Manly have each only one life now. Closest
and most interesting, game of latest round was between
Waverley and St. George, former finishing up 18 short of
the opposing side’s total
Meteorite, who shaped brilliantly at recent
W.A.T.C. New Year’s Day meet, was once between the
shafts of a buggy. Later his owner parted with him at a
low figure, and now he is among the highest-class
sprinters of W.A.
* * * *
Brisbane Tatt.’s Club’s annual balance-sheet is
a doleful document. Debit is £325. Seven meetings at
Deagon resulted in £513 loss, while four at Albion Park
brought £209 profit. Year opened with a credit of £2712,
but a loss of £1522 occurred during succeeding 12 months.
Committee recommends that Deagon racecourse be
handed back to vendors in terms of purchase agreement.
* * * *
Mr. P. Bolger, Westralia’s big and generally
prosperous horse owner, who topped the prize list at
recent W.A.T.C. meeting, is an ex-jockey. There are
many instances of riders making money as trainers, but
mighty few of them have done nearly as well as Mr.
Bolger at racing their own nags.
* * * *
Carbine’s stock in England during the last
season failed greatly by comparison with results for the
previous season, the figures of the total winnings of the
progeny being past season, £4959; previous year,
£22,126. The Duke of Portland gave 13,000 guineas for
Carbine, and the animal hasn’t yet proved a good invest-
ment at the stud.

Sylvia Park stud (M.L.), the early home of
Carbine and other celebrities, has been dispersed for the
second time, the wealthy Nathans giving the game best.
The whole realised 11,387g5. Seaton Delaval, the pick of
the proved sires, was grabbed for the Wellington Park
stud at 1700g5., but San Francisco was allowed to go to
Mr. H. O. White, of Havilah (N.S.W.), for 1500 gs.
Maorilanders may yet feel sorry they didn’t keep him.
He is by St. Simon—lsabel, and full brother to the whilom
English crack St. Frusquin. His stock have yet to race.
* *
W. H. Smith, the crack lightweight, is booked
to leave for France during the year under engagement to
Three-Star Hennessy at a big salary.
• * * *
Glean Sweep, Melb. Cup winner in 1900, and
still reasonably young, was sold at Newmarket (Eng.) for
£3O. Bought by trainer Seoliie for 90gs. as a yearling, at
three years he scooped Coongy Hep., Moonee Valley Cup,
Melb. Cup, V.R.C. Spring Stakes, St. George Stakes,
Loch Plate, and A.J.O. St. Leger. Less than a year after
these triumphs the horse went to England for 2000g5.,
and was a dead failure. Still, as he has Musket, Fisher,
and Tim Whiffler blood, he’s worth more than £3O. Eng-
lish buyers considered him too small for a country
stallion, but if his purchaser brings him to Australia as a
sire he will make a handsome profit on that £3O.
* * • *
Negro Starlight and second-rater Jack Willis
fouled so deliberately in their fight, at Sydney Gaiety
A.C., last week, that the police stopped the contest in
the eighth round. The black, one of the straightest and
best behaved of fighters, was hooted, though he fought
fairly till the Caucasian introduced objectionable tactics.
Negro fighters in Australia, with the solitary exception
of Peter Jackson, have never had fair treatment, and
latterly no show at all. No man could have fought more
fairly than did Felix in the Scanlan and Ruenalf fights,
and yet in both cases the crowd hooted his legitimate
work and applauded the other men’s unfair tricks.
• * * *
Most promising-looking pug. seen in Sydney
for some time is Maoriland champion lightweight, Tim
Tracey, here on a holiday, and to arrange a match for
Easter. He resembles Billy M'Coll. So far, Tracey’s
best record is that he finished the stone heavier Yankee
Brown-Parker, a rough-up fighter, more quickly and
decisively than middleweight Snowy Sturgeon did.
* • * •
Match at Sydney National S.C. last week,
between Peter Toohey and Victorian little ’un Feeney,
for bantam supremacy of Australia, proved a bitter dis-
appointment. Whether because he didn’t do his best or
that he was so very much inferior to the other in point
of skill, Feeney made a decidedly poor showing, and,
after actually being counted out in the first round, was
allowed to go on to be disqualified in the next, subsequent
to getting a punch on the chin that, as far as appearances
went, rendered him deaf to the count and compelled
assistance to remove him from the ring.
* * * *
Age and the strain of fighting continuously
are rapidly sending Jack M'Gowan among the back
numbers. In his match last week with Tasmanian Billy
Maher, bar the 18th and 19th rounds, the last named had
a decided lead. Yet the referee declared M’Gowan the
winner—a surprising decision.
• • • *
There’s a curious mixture of pugilism and
religion about Newcastle’s lightweight, Paddy King, now
on his way to Westralia in search of further ring victims.
He used to be a member of the Parochial Council con-
nected with the church he attended, and carried away
with him a huge presentation Bible. He doesn’t swear,
drink nor smoke the least little bit, but woe betide the
man who takes him on in the ring if he doesn’t come
along with a good stock of skill or a hide of iron.

ance of the work, which contains a considerable mass of
information useful to country residents.
The Year Book op N.S. W. for 1904 is to hand.
It is a separate binding of the N.S.W. section of the
Australian Year Book, a trustworthy and comprehen-
sive reference work on Australia. From it, it is possible
to discover even such a trifling fact that there are
192,862 pigs in the State (these numbers apply only to
pigs which may be turned into bacon).*
