“ Petramos ” on Salvarmy captains :
It takes a man with brains (of a sort) and lung power
to be a good Salvarmy captain. He needs the gab of a
cheap-jack, the confidence of a spieler, and the persuasive
nature of a commercial traveller. He must also possess
the face of a low comedian, or be capable of making any
number of contortions of the physiognomy. At W
they had a really first-class man, who gathered in many
shekels, to the glory of God and the army, but one night
he received a free pass to Heaven and the lieutenant was
promoted. He was a mild youth whose jokes were
mellow, and was a sad failure. Booth or something
shifted him on at length, and a lady took command. She
perpetrated no jokes—only smiled a large, cracked sort
of smile that didn’t do much damage, but didn’t benefit
the Army. Now we’ve got another good man with
Darwinian features, who composes original hymns that
apply locally. But the youngest (and prettiest) female
Salvarmyite says when she becomes captain she will clasp
the hand of the sinner gently and lead him in the right
path with her persuasive smile, and so many sinners want
to be clasped that there is an agitation for the removal of
the gorilla-like captain and the promotion of the sweet
young thing in his stead.
In our advertising columns will be found
prospectus of the Western Australian loan of
£500,000 at 4 per cent, with a currency of 20
years. The loan is a subscription one, and the
price has been fixed at par. Priority of applica-
tion will be considered, and subscriptions close on
February 3rd. Fullest information as to the
financial position and expenditure of loan moneys
is given in the prospectus. Applicants may apply
through any member of the Stock Exchange.
“ G.H.” : Had I the wings of a bird I
would roost high nowadays and wear a shot-proof
shirt into the bargain. Met a female recently
with half a scrub-full of stuffed wild birds on her
hat, a snakeskin belt, and a fathom of feather boa
round her neck. I felt a desire to seize the ends
of the latter, knot them, and rush violently in two
opposite directions, but weak-mindedly refrained.
When the gaudy female demanded a contribution
of me for the heathen of Fiji, I put my voice
down, however, and told her to send the poor
heathen her pagan hat and other outfit. Then she
sniffed and passed on.
A culinary connoisseur has been seized
with a money-making inspiration, and is mooting
a co-operative cookery co. at Christchurch (M.L.),
to enable householders to get over the difficulty
caused by that sweet and beautiful creature,
Sarah Ann Jane Sophia. This public benefactor
proposes to start with an initial capital of £3OOO.
and supply gilt-edged aristocrats or workers’
wives, who are always late with teas owing to
Women’s Anecdote League meetings, with any
sort of meal from an epicurean poem down to just
plain hash. Subscribers have to arrange their
menus for the week, and the banquets will
arrive per motor car on hot water trays. When
“attendance” is required, a waiter or a bright
and pleasing waitress will whisk along, serve the
feast, and disappear with the dishes. Payment
in advance will discourage the debt-contracting
habit. The scheme also includes a down-town
restaurant for the accommodation of families who
are sick of their quiet, beautiful, and peaceful
home life.
The first Melbourne church was a shanty
where St. James’s Old Cathedral now stands ; all
Protestants used it. Afterwards, the Presby-
terians moved to Collins-street West, near Wil-
liam-street, about next to Batman’s house. The
Wesleyans moved first to Swanston-street. The
Baptists and Congregationals severally pitched in
Collins-street East. The R.C.’s were always on
the St. Francis’ site.
London Daily Chronicle very accurately
sums up the Federal Capital issues which guided
members of the Australian Parliament:—
1. Is this site near my capital city ? If it is, I must
vote for it. If not, I vote against it.
2 Will the trade of the Federal city’s area, if fixed here
or there, benefit my State ?
3. Is this site in my friend A.’s constituency?
4. Will this site be convenient to me to reach shdhld I
be returned to Parliament again?
The mansions of “ our old nobility ” are
being steadily converted into homes for charitable
purposes. The Good Samaritans have got Tox-
teth Park, Glebe, the residence for 70 years of
the Allen family. More recently the Church of
England secured “Strathmore,” on the opposite
side of Glebe Road, and the same denomination
has captured “Avona,” next door. Strathmore,
in the long ago, was the house of W. B. Tooth.
It was then purchased by George Wigram Allen,
whilom Speaker of N.S.W. Assembly. After
him came Dr. Andrew Garran, whose wife kept a
high-toned ladies’ school there. Avona, next
door, was, in the long ago, the residence of soft-
goods man Alexander M‘Arthur, who afterwards
became an English M.P. Subsequently it was
occupied by Mr. A. H. C. Macafee, of A.
M "Arthur & Co., who died there.

it is to depend upon certain words in the English language. There
are some words which have been used so promiscuously that they
have lost all significance.

anywhere between 3 and 21 years of age; but, nowadays, we call
them all “girls” up to 100. When the baby’s sex is asked, we say “IT
is a girl,” and, when you engage a 65 year old cook, she is still “a
Go into a Restaurant and ask for lamb, and you will probably
get a two-year-old sheep—ask for chicken, and you are sure of
getting hen. ....

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
• The children of Buddha the Gentle, who taught the sweet
teaching of Love,
Who spurned as the spawn of Gehenna the wielders of
glaive and of glove,
The children of Buddha are yearning, a valorous, brown
little breed,
To grip with the Russ in the battle—the Russ who hath
Christ in his creed !
And each one is proud of his Gospel, and each one is
proud of his gun,
The range of his cursfed torpedoes, the carnage his fathers
have done ;
And each will prepare for the battle by blessing his
warriors with prayer
And begging the God Who is Mercy to smite through the
measureless air.
For prate as we may of our prophets, and cant as we may
of our creed,
The Savage will finally follow and cover our Cant with his
Deed ;
For Nature is deeper than Preacher, and Man is but wild
at the core,
And the Innermost Brute that we cherish is joyed at the
tidings of war.
We talk of our Faith and our Morals—the varnish that
lies on our skin—
And over the sound of our talking there rises a thunderous
din—
A din of the bursting of battle—a splashing of blood and
of foam,
And we know by the throb in our bosoms, they shelter
a savage at home.
A savage at home in our bosoms—a savage who pulses
akin
To savages born on the Dneiper, to savages yellow of
skin,
A savage who dances on Gospel, a savage who curses his
Buddh,
And drinks to the creed of his fathers, whose gods were
the drinkers of blood 1
For ever, when Passion is mighty, the Faith is a tether
of air—
A tether that snaps in a moment when bay’nets are
ready and bare,
And the prayer of the priest who goes blessing the tigers
who ravage and bleed,
It is but as the voice of a devil who chanteth a dirge for
the Creed !
For the Russ he will march into battle, a soldier of
Christ the Benign,
And his foemen will face him and rend him and call upon
Buddh for a sign ;
And the savage within us will join them, and joy as the
vict’ries are won,
As the savage hath joyed in the battle since the days of
our race were begun.

THE NURSERY SOAP
Q. A.
Q. I har# been feeling very poorly lately, and have just been told by my doctor that lam suffer-
ing from uric poisoning. I shall be glad if you will tell me just what uric poisoning means. Is it a
serious matter ?
A. Uric poisoning is caused by the retention in the blood of various substances which should
leave the body in solution. The retention of these substances is due to a diseased or inactive condi-
tion of the kidneys. _ When the kidneys are working perfectly, they filter and extract from the blood
of the average individual about three pints of urine every day. In this quantity of urine should be
dissolved various waste material produced by the wear and tear of the tissues of the body. This is
dead matter, and its presence in the blood is poisonous. The three pints of normal urine should con-
tain about ten grains in weight of uric acid, an ounce of urea, together with other animal and mineral
matter varying from a third of an ounce to nearly an ounce.
Q. Then lam to understand that the substances you mention when not eliminated from the body
in the natural manner constitute what is known as uric poisoning. What are the usual symptoms by
which the presence of these poisons is manifested ?
A. Now you are asking a rather large question. Many complaints which are commonly called
diseases are not actually diseases in themselves. For instance, Rheumatism, Gout, Neuralgia,
Lumbago, Sciatica, Gravel, Stone, and Bladder Troubles, are all caused by uric poisons.
Indigestion, Anaemia, Persistent Headache and General Debility are often solely due to
the same cause. In fact, if the kidneys are doing their work freely and thoroughly, none of the
complaints mentioned could trouble us as the causative poisons would be absent.
Q. I had no idea that so much depended upon the efficient action of the kidneys. I suppose
that when anyone is suffering from Rheumatism, Gout, Neuraljfia, Lumbago, Sciatica,
Gravel, Stone, Bladder Troubles, Anaemia, Debility, Persistent Headache or
Indigestion, the scientific method of effecting a cure would be to directly treat the patient for the
kidneys ?
A. Exactly In fact, that is the only way in which a radical and permanent cure can be effected.
The kidneys must be restored to health and activity, so that they may be enabled to remove the daily
production of poisons in the body, or the patient must continue to suffer.
Q. I have always been under the impression that the liver had a great deal to do with the
maintenance of our general health, but it seems that the kidneys are the chief cause of most of the
disorders from which we suffer?
A. The work done by the liver is of the utmost importance and it is closelv associated with the
work done by the kidneys. Indeed, when anything is the matter with the liver the kidneys are almost
always directly affected, and the contrary is likewise true. In the liver various substances are actually
made from the blood. Two or three pounds of bile are thus made every day. The liver takes sugar
from the blood, converts it into another form, and stores it up so as to be able to again supply it to
the blood, gradually, as the latter requires enrichment. The liver changes uric acid, which is
insoluble, into urea, which is completely soluble, and the liver also deals with the blood corpuscles
which have lived their life and are useful no longer.
<£. As the functions of the kidneys and liver are so intimately related, I gather that if there is
reason to suspect that either organ is not doing its work efficiently, a curative agent should be
employed which would act equally upon the kidneys and liver ?
A. Yes, that is the case, and it was the realization of this important fact which lead to the dis-
covery of that invaluable medicine Warner’s Safe Cure. About thirty years ago, certain medical
men, knowing that if they could find a medicine which would beneficially affect alike the kidneys and
liver, they could control most of the common disorders, devoted themselves to the search for such a
remedy. After many disappointments, their efforts were rewarded, and a medicine now known as
Warners bafe Cure was proved to possess the required properties in the fullest degree. Warner's
Safe Cure has a marvellously stimulating and healing effect upon both the kidneys and liver, and
by restoring those vital organs to health and activity, it necessarily cures all disorders due to the
retention in the blood of urinary and biliary poisons, such as
Rheumatism
Gout
Neuralgia
Lumbago
Sciatica
Blood Disorders

From Two Points of View.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I shall never get tired of it, never / ” said the
V»ride of a week, as she rested her hands on the
white verandah railing, and looked eagerly about.
It was in the Spring that he brought her home,
and the wattles were out in the hollow behind the
house their gnarled horny limbs honeyoombed
with bloom, and crazy with wild bees. It was
their heavy scent she noticed first when they
drove in at the top white gate, and the breeze
swung in their faces. The gums were blossoming
along the creek, showering waxen creamy blooms
and pungent leaves into the brown, sleepy tide
below, that circled and eddied so slowly between
the warm mud banks, on resistlessly to the wide
clear river beyond.
“ It is delicious,” she said, following the sweep
of the green lush swamp, that curved in a long
half-moon between the house and the red high
road. The marsh-land was a-glow with golden
buttercups, and a strange wild purple iris her
city eyes had never before seen. Great white
cranes stalked warily amongst the shallows,
gobbling for frogs amidst the blue-green reeds.
Ever and anon flocks of waterfowl swept past
with a furious scurry of wings. “ How could it
be lonely?” she cried. “Listen to the birds.”
In the pepper trees a flock of parrots swung and
shrieked, flashing gold and crimson flames among
the drab green leaves, and shaking the small red
berries tumbling beneath. On the garden path a
family of “apostles” danced to and fro, scolding
vigorously, and ruffling their sober feathers;
while on the fence a magpie—a magnificent dandy
—sang a welcome to the bush.
The sun had set below the distant hills—they
reminded her of vague, purple billows, far at sea—
dying out in drunken crimson behind the white
ra ggcd gums on the plain ; and shadows like
great clouds were stealing from the bush. The
voice of the creek protesting at the weir came
murmuring through the heavy-scented dusk. It
was growing so still. The flights of shrieking
cockatoos sweeping from horizon to horizon had
long since died to silence. The stars throbbed
into the sky, bigger, vaster, closer somehow than
they had ever seemed in the city. A thin saffron
haze told where the light had died. From the
swamp came the booming of the frogs, the thin
“skreel” of some desolate night bird. The clink
of a hobbled horse broke sharply from beyond
the fence. The girl laid her face on her husband’s
arm. “I am content,” she said.
■« * * *
“ How can I bear it ? ” said the wife of nearly a
year, lifting her head from her arm and staring
blankly outside to where the hessian curtain
swung to and fro, giving uninviting glimpses of
the dry, dusty paddocks that throbbed with
heat. _ The high road was deserted, save for the
whirling eddies of dust that, slanting through
the grey wire fences, drifted lazily towards the
house. Where the swamp of buttercups and
reeds had been was a scar of hard caked earth.
The wattles had forgotten to blossom. The
pepper trees drooped languidly, sand-laden, and
all her bright garden blooms were dead months
ago. With them the flowers of her life had
withered away, and her heart was bitter with lost
illusions. This hideous land of torture! Day
after day unbroken but for the whirlwinds of
dust, and the vagrant, floating crow. Her head
dropped. To-day the harbor would be undulating
blue above a sky of faintest steel, with the gulls
cleaving it lazily, sinking, rising, from the cool,
shifting water, and poising delicately midway
above the green, sun-warm swell.
The blackness of night had blotted out the
hills, and the heat that seemed to ooze from the
ground was worse than the vicious sun. Round
the kerosene lamp and about the table lay grey
heaps of singed and dying moths and insects.
The rustle of a week-old paper, and the swearing
of her husband as he mixed himself whisky and
water, reached the girl vaguely on the verandah,
where she crouched hopelessly in a canvas chair.
The sound of trickling water had not been heard
for months; the weir was now a wall of blister-
ing, gaping stones, across a dusty hollow, into
which the drought-seared gum-leaves sorrow-
fully fell. There were no birds to call from the
swamp ; even the curlews—harbingers of drought
—were never heard. A dog howled intermittently
at the back, and the sand, gritted shrilly on the
iron roof. Was there nothing but silence behind
that impenetrable darkness, and those miles of
trees? Her husband stumbled to bed, and the
dog howled on. Somewhere the ocean was
thundering on its beaches. Somewhere the
harbor was whimpering at its wharves. The mail
would be passing soon. If she caught it—there
was money in her room—to-morrow, the next day,
the next, she would be back again in the jargon
of the streets. She rose, the chair creaked pro-
testingly, she listened in apprehension. “ Are
you never coming in?” came irritably from the
room beyond. As she passed in to the stifling
gloom, and the reek of spirits, the mail rattled
and lurched past, and the swaying lights and
the voice of the driver died out among the trees.

Re the committal at Sydney Water Police
Court of John Miller, on a charge of breaking and
entering, on the evidence of the (alleged) identi-
fication of finger prints. In Judge Burton’s time
a, man was hanged in Sydney on the evidence of

court.
Irrigation in Australia.
Tbn or 12 years ago, at a time of the greatest
social unrest that Australia has ever known,
communism was put on its trial in a number of
intensely interesting settlements on the lower
Murray.. The experiment failed as completely as
the similar _ New Australia experiment did in
South America. But, even after communism had
been regretfully ruled out as a beautiful dream,
most of the settlements remained ; and another
experiment, almost as valuable, began—an ex-
periment which it is of particular value to inquire
into at a time when, as the result of a phenomenal
season, the “great natural industries” are being
boomed, and the people are being told that,
if they only had water, every year might
see as bountiful a harvest, it being, of course,
suggested that irrigation might supply that water.
Australia has very few waterways from which to
irrigate; but suoh as there are, these village
settlements have the very best that the continent
can offer—the southern half of the continent, at
any rate. They get not only the Murray waters,
but those of the Darling, Murrumbidgee, and
Lachlan also, when there are any. The settlers,
moreover, began their work filled with enthusiasm;
they probably knew no more about growing fruit
and cereals than most people, but they had
Government advice and they had the before-
mentioned enthusiasm, which was a good asset.
And yet, with all their advantages, only six of the
original 13 settlements have now any existence
whatever, and those six are, for the most part, in a
pitiable state. From first to last the S. A. Govern-
ment (to Nov. 30, 1903) has spent £116,848 on the
experiment, irrespective of a small amount which
was recovered from the abandoned settlements
during their existence ; all it has got back so far
is .£14,424. It has written off as lost £65,235 ; it
recovered a trifle from the sale of plant
on the abandoned settlements; and as for the
balance, £35,489, it has made an arrangement
with the settlers to pay it in 42 annual instal-
ments, and instalments 42 years ahead can’t be
relied upon with any great amount of confi-
dence. Already, in fact, although this arrange-
ment was made less than a couple of years ago,
the Government has been requested to be merciful,
and refrain from calling for the instalments ; and
as one of the settlers’ spokesmen backs up the
appeal with the statement that he only made £l2
last year, and had to keep a wife and three chil-
dren on that, the Government will probably have
no option in the matter. Here and there cer-
tainly are settlers who are satisfied ; but the fact
that, all told, only 89 heads of families remain,
whereas formerly there were as many as 557, is
more eloquent than any settler’s words.
Another eloquent fact is that, although lemon
trees were planted freely during the early
years, and bore splendidly, no lemons whatever
have been sent to market for two years because
there has not been enough demand to cover
expenses. In other words, in order to make these
settlements profitable, even under the most advan-
tageous natural conditions, there must be a larger
manufacturing population of consumers at hand ;
and that, of course, there will never be under a
Foreign-trade policy. For wheat there is, no
doubt, always a market, but only at a price which
makes irrigation impracticable along Australian
rivers, where, in almost every case, the irrigating
water must be pumped. Pumping is a costly pro-
cess—so costly that to grow wheat on land thus
irrigated costs more, in almost every case, than can
be got for wheat when the cost of exporting it is
allowed for.
“G.H.” writes•
Those who have any doubts at all about the
land resumption should visit Wando Vale, near Oasterton
(V.). Four years ago it was a one-horse station-property,
owned by one family, and populated by one manager,
two station-hands, one domestic servant, a couple of child-
ren, and half-a-dozen gaudy, but useless peacocks. To-
day it is oocupied by 66 different persons; some with
families, others young, virile men who may be depended
on to have families later. There are no peacocks or
flamingoes or other ornamental poultry on Wando Vale
at present; their place has been taken by ducks, hens,
turkeys, and other useful if unsesthetic animals. Prac-
tically every holding has a decent dwelling erected on
it. To-day, December 24th, a score or more of reapers-
and-binders are rattling along the hills and flats where a
few cattle and sheep browsed a couple of years back ;
and the wheat and oat-sheaves are falling from the
binder-tables like a golden hail. About the centre of the
settlement, and near the blacksmith’s shop and post-
office, there is a large hall, used as a school, and for
general meeting and concert purposes. (Anyone men-
tioning a concert or other social entertainment at W.V.
in the year 1900 would have been scoffed at as a lunatic.)
An example of another kind is furnished by Muntham, an
estate with better land than Wando Vale. Instead of being
occupied by fresh small-landholders, however, Muntham
was grabbed by a number of local farmers with land
already in their possession, and who are mostly using
their new property for grazing purposes instead of culti-
vation. Wando Vale had its cutting-up and disposal
managed exclusively by the Government, whereas the
sub-division of Muntham was very badly mismanaged by
private individuals. That there is more prosperity and
gain to the State in one quarter of Wando Vale than in
the whole of the larger and agriculturally better estate
is a pretty terse commentary on the benefits of Govern-
ment cutting up.
♦ —
Advantage is evidently being taken of the pro-
mise of a good season to work a “ boom’’in
farming land in N.S.W. Speculators are buying
up estates suitable for closer settlement and
subdividing them for sale, while farmers, forgetful
of the recent prolonged drought, are eager to pur-
chase at tall figures. Farms will be sold on terms
on the basis of present conditions, and when a
series of bad seasons returns, the buyers will, in
many cases, not be able to keep up their pay-
ments, their deposits will be forfeited, and they
will either have to clear out or remain as rack-
rented tenants. And this will be no new thing in
Australia. During the land boom, in Victoria a
dozen years ago, a quantity of private land was
disposed of at exorbitant figures to foolishly
sanguine buyers who found they could not com-
plete their bargains, and the land went back, with
their instalment moneys and improvements, to the
seller. In N.S.W. the circumstances at present
are exceptionally favorable to the holders of
country land, for, owing to the policy of using
land-sale money as revenue and making many
non-reproductive works out of loans, successive
Governments have been able to construct all
kinds of public works for country districts with-
out any tax being levied in return. The present
penny in the pound of land tax assessed on the
usual very moderate valuation, is far too light to
destroy speculative holding. Those people who
buy at present values and under present taxes
will find, very shortly, that they have made bad
bargains.]

TV. H aa■ on a Melbourne election episode :
““ ol '
A swell-headed member of the breed entered the 6d.
house where I take in fuel every dinner hour.
He had a cow-face, and, appropriately enough, a
miniature cow-bell hanging from his sign-post tie-pin, a
spotted leopard vest, and a nose whose contemptuous up-
W The girl Tsked Mm whom he intended to vote for, and
he answered in a voice that made the electric fan wobble,
and caused a nervous old man near to give his vest a
drink of soup—“ The Argus faw.”
The little rabbit-faced man opposite him coughed
leo a u P set a y° un K cataract of tea over the
Xttered an apology and grabbed at his hat, which
dropped off the peg on to the Conservative’s cow-heel.
Latter swore a most democratic damn, and the crowd
laughed nastily.
“Marat”: “Rita Sunyasee” (8.,31/12/’O3)
discourses on “the rasping sounds of bush-life
awakening.” A suburban street is a whole zoologi-
cal garden compared to the bush in sound volume.
A tram guard living next door to me arrives home
about 1.20 a.m. and, after kicking up enough row
for a Chinese funeral, goes into the yard and
splashes 200 gallons of water under his bedroom
window to cool the air. He has breakfast then
in the kitchen, and roars out to his wife, who is
in the bedroom, various items concerning his last
“run.” Just as I am dozing off again. Casey,
who lives on the other side, gets up. He has to
be at work at 4.30, but gets up at 3 so as to be in
time. He wears hob-nailed boots, and the row he
makes rushing round to get Mrs. C. a cup of tea
reminds one of an avalanche on a drunk. When lie
leaves he slams the door with a bang that startles
every infant in the terrace ; they perform in turns
till 5 o’clock. Then the milkboy arrives next door,
and that conductor hasn’t put the can out : so
Mr. Cow kicks the door until the neighbor on the
other side gets up and tells him with 319 oaths to
pour the milk under the door —which he won’t do.
My nerves are in open revolt now, and so are the
nerves of M‘Gifford’s kids in the back lane. I
get up and swear forcibly till breakfast time. 1
wish I had the “quivery, quakery, husky call of
the wagtail.” I never heard it, but I’ll bet it’s
tame compared to Casey, and the “piercing
tremolo ” of the peewit is not in it with
M‘Gifford’s kids.


Are You Like A Stopped Clock?
A stopped clock is “run down.” Are you?
Summer s heat and close, trying days tell on the
business man, and still more on the woman in the
home. Loss of appetite, loss of energy, a feeling
of constant tiredness, frequent headaches and
inability to go your old pace—these are the_ signs
of being “rundown.” The cure, if you wish to
apply it without wasting time and running up a
heavy doctor’s bill, is Bile Beans. This is no
mere theory, as witness the following case:—
“ Twelve months ago,” says Cecelia M. J. Monaro,
of 10 Lackey-street, St. Peters, Sydney, “I
suffered considerably from headache, tired feeling,
loss of appetite and sleeplessness. I tried doctors
without success. Subsequently Bile Beans were
brought to my notice and I decided to give them
a trial. The contents of the first box gave me
great relief, and thus encouraged, I continued a
course for a month, with the result that I now eat
heartily and enjoy a good night’s sleep. I praise
Bile Beans highly for what they have done for me,
and always recommend them to fellow-sufferers.”
Any chemist will suppy them at Is. or 2s. 9d.
per box. Don’t take any substitute.*
If the man on the land wishes to remain
there in peace, he should employ W. N. Willis,
Land Agent, 21 Bligh-street, as his agent. No
results, no payments.*
Knagg’s Almanac for 1904 is now issued by
Messrs. W. H. Sproull and Co., of Newcastle. The
Almanac is of interest to those who go down among
great whales in ships and floating coffins, and relates en-
tirely to the shipping business. The present edition is
much enlarged and improved, compared to those of former
years.*
Diverse replies to Seddon’s invitation to
protest against an Asiatic invasion of S’Africa :
From Cape Colony, the Afrikander State: “ Cape
Colony has not been silent upon the question of Asiatic
immigration. Many public meetings have been held, at
which strong adverse resolutions have been carried. The
Government sent a vigorous protest against it to the
Imperial Government five months since. That protest
has been renewed since the passing of Sir George Farrar’s
resolution a few days ago.”
From Natal, thi Imperialistic State: “The Govern-
ment of Natal holds the opin : on that additional unskilled
labor is urgently required for the development of the
Transvaal, and is unable to join in the protest suggested
in your telegram. A motion deprecating the introduc-
tion of Chinese labor into South Africa was brought
before the Legislative Assembly of Natal during last
session of Parliament, and was negatived by 30 votes
to 2.”
It is a bad sign for British Imperialism that it
should in S’Africa be so completely identified
with Chinese labor. Australia would have
declared for independence if she could at no other
price have got rid of the Chinaman, and it is
dangerous for the British Imperialist faction in
S’Africa to unite together in one opposition all
the genuine whites.

The Railways and Tramways, after paying out of their Revenue £35,000 for construction, of
Stations and Sidings, and .£54,490 for replacing obsolete and worn-out Rolling Stock, returned a
surplus for the year of £322,458, or £33,802 more than was necessary to pay interest on the Capital
Expenditure of £8,905,868, and this year the result will be quite as favorable.
The Eremantle Harbor Works returned for the last 12 months, clear of all expenses, an amount
equal to 3| per cent, on the total expenditure on this work. This is exclusive of the returns from all
other Harbors in the State.
The Goldfields Water Supply is fast approaching completion, and the net revenue from this and
other Water Schemes this year will pay nearly 2\ per cent, on the capital involved, and at an early
date there is every justification for the expectation that the schemes will pay full interest.
On the 30th June, 1903, the total amount expended on Public Buildings, Roads and Bridges
amounted to £2,850,000, but of this large sum only £209,208 has been expended from Loan Funds, the
balance having been paid from Revenue.
The Expenditure under the Heads of “Develepment of Goldfields” and “Development of
Agriculture” contains about £500,000, which is directly reproductive. The balance can only be
classed as indirectly reproductive, although each year increases the direct reproductiveness of the
expenditure under these heads. The bulk of this expenditure was incurred some years ago, and was
rendered necessary in the earlier days of the Goldfields and Agricultural Development, but an ever-
increasing proportion of the votes specified is now paid from Revenue.
The State is constructing from Revenue a number of public works which are distinctly repro-
ductive. From this year’s Revenue provision is made for Railways, £87,000, being for part cost of
the Malcolm to Laverton Railway, the Gooseberry Hill Railway, the Owen’s Anchorage Railway,
and the Jandakot railway A further £25,000 is provided for Public Batteries, which pay working
expenses and interest; £72,787 for Additions and Improvements to Open Railways and Maintenance
and Repairs of Rolling Stock; £28,000 for Rabbit-proof Fencing, or a total of £212,787 is paid from
Revenue for works which elsewhere are paid from Loan Funds. An equal sum was provided on last
year’s Estimates for similar works.
Every industry in the State is solidly prosperous. Nearly £9,000,000 worth of gold was produced
for the year ended 31st December last. The agricultural and pastoral industries, owing to the never-
failing average rainfall, are experiencing phenomenal development. The Timber, Tin, Copper and
Pearl industries were never sounder. The population of the State during the year just ended
increased by 14,000. Employment is regular and capital profitably invested. The Mining Dividends
alone paid during the year ended 31st December totalled £1,988,755. Taking the nominal capital of
the mines now working at £30,000,000, this is over 6J per cent, per annum on that sum.
Three facts stand out prominently in the Finances of the State :
1. The rate of Interest on our Loans is .£3 9s. 6d. per cent., the lowest of any State in
Australia.
The proportion of Interest on Loans to total expenditure is £l3 19s. per cent., or including
the State’s Sinking Fund (amounting to £177,000 per annum) £lB 11s. 4d. per cent., as
against the next lowest Australian State of £26 per cent., or the average of Australasia
of £29 per cent.
Every Loan bears a Sinking Fund, varying from 1 per cent, to 3 per cent. This Fund is
not under control of the State Treasurer, but is sent to London to Trustees, who invest it
in purchasing the State’s own Stocks. Owing to the low price of Australian Stocks
generally, the State is by these means purchasing its own Stocks with its own Sinking
Fund at a Discount ranging from £4 to £l4. The annual contribution to this fund is now
£177,000, and, at the end of the present Financial Year, the fund will total £1,012,000,
or nearly one-half of the total amount provided by all the Sinking Funds of the whole of
the Commonwealth.
The Stock now offered will rank pari passu with the Stock issued and sold under “ The Loan
Act, 1891,” and all subsequent Loan Acts.
The principal and the interest will be payable at this office, or, if desired, in any of the Common-
wealth capitals, free of exchange ; or will be sent by post to any address at the stock-holder’s risk.
Applications, addressed to the Colonial Treasurer, must not be. for a less sum than .£lOO, and
I above that amount must be for multiples of £5O, and be accompanied by a deposit of £5 per cent.
Payment in full will be required on the 10th February, 1904.

There is one really bitter theatrical critic in Australasia.
’Tis said that his wife is susceptible to actors, and whom-
soever she raves over he slates with envenomed pen.
Harry Rickards knows. Vulcana, the muscular lady,
is advertised as the “most beautifully developed woman.”
If her muscle was boasted too much, many males might
think she owned limbs strong, but unattractive.

“ Haresfoot” : What became of Barlow, the Blue-Tail
Fly ? I have a dim idea of his great hit in London.
Then he came to Melbourne, and was at Astley’s, I think,
and Cremorne, besides all round the Australasian com-
pass. Then he was amid the Queensland sugar pioneers.
Not much sugar in that. So you found him busking
again—l don’t mean in the vulgar sense, round bars.
Even at L. L. Smith’s Melb. Polytechnic he was a sort of
fly in amber. Long afterwards I was at a publisher’s,
when Barlow came in with a very big idea. It was to
publish his reminiscences. The bookman shook his
head. No money in it. “ What, me I —the Blue-Tail
Fly 1 Barlow, you know 1” The man didn’t know.
Wellington (M.L.) on Boxing Night sported two panto-
mimes and a drama of rainbow tints. At the Theatre
Royal Mr. P. R. Dix produced the “House that Jack
Built,” an old-new work from the pen of Bert Royal,
which caught on at once. The music is by Mr. Alec. Hill,
a local man. The “ Fuller Proprietary ” (who have added
to their real estate by purchasing the Opera House in
Tuam-street, Christchurch) staged “ Robinson Crusoe ”
in nice green tights. The Opera House sheltered the
Anderson company from the wrath of heaven which that
agonising shriek, “Her Second Time on Eartn,” might,
otherwise have brought down.
Ze Great Napoleon Boffard is the latest victim of bad
times in Mel murne. Nap.’s insolvency is quite pathetic-
ally unpretentious, considering the first-class appearance
he has presented to the world for years back. Liabilities,
£3GI ; assets, £6 ss. (consisting of diamond jewellery). A
few of his personal observations on this tragic event, as
communicated to The Bulletin: “The appearance of
M. E. Napoleon Boffard at the Insolvency Court this day
came as a great surprise to society and musical people,
and great svmpathies are expressed to him. Although
he alwavs emphatically deny it, the tenor was reputed a
man of means. But for his personal friends it was per-
fectly known that since a little over two years he had a
hard'struggle to get along. Weary of fighting, without
seeing any good prospects, in the impossibility to obtain
delays with some of the more heated creditors, and acting
under the decision of his advisers, M. Boffard fired his
schedule.” Well, he ha* nothing to reproach himself
with, except bad luck.
The number of competent actors who can’t hope to
earn more than £5OO. a year in Britain is about twentj'
times as large as the number who can earn £lOOO a year
—if exceptionally lucky. Hence the bitter cry of mum-
merdom concerning the salary paid to Consul, the chim-
panzee who rides a bicycle and otherwise apes the ways
of man. Consul is said to get £2OO a week from the
London Hippodrome, receiving the cheque in person and
handing it over to his trainer. There would seem to be a
great opening for a bad actor to become a monkey at a
great salary. There’s Mr. Wilson for instance.
“F.” : Antoinette Sterling, not more than 55 when she
died (last week), is remembered in Australia as an oppress-
ively stout contralto—a somewhat swollen-looking lady,
strong of voice, but scant of breath. Her voice was con-
tralto to a fault, and its distinctive quality was best
displayed after she grew fat, in songs of the sombre
character, such as “Three Fishers” and “The Three
Ravens.” In her prime days she could do full justice to
“ The Lost Chord,” which was written for her by Arthur
Sullivan, and which will keep her name green in musical
history. When Sterling came to Australia in ’93, she was
following Madame Patey, a better preserved and sweeter
voiced contralto, who travelled on her artistic merits,
pure and simple. The huge woman, with the red face
and the temperance principles, travelled as a sort of mis-
sionary balladist under the patronage of the Sex’s purity
societies and shrieking sisterhoods. She invited ridicule,
but she was terribly in eirnest, was Antoinette. When
not singing on the platform she talked, talked, talked
with members of some Christian union or other until her
warble degenerated out of knowledge. And how those
Christians did bleed the soul ! She sang to crowded
Town Halls pretty well all the time, yet at the end of her
profitable tour she had to cable to England for money to
take her home again. The genial Lohr, who acted as
manager for pious Antoinette, would never allow that
there was the slightest taint of business tactics in her
Christian Endeavoring. To lay up treasures in Heaven
was her dart. She wanted to pluck brands from the
burning. In saying good-bye to Lohr on the boat, the
“old girl ” implored him to turn from whisky, tobacco,
and all godless indulgences, and Lohr, like a good
diplomatic sinner, said he would give the matter his
earnest consideration. That he might consider the
matter from a new standpoint, he eventually took the
hotel at Anderson’s Inlet, and when news of Antoinette’s
death came to hand, he probably tied a bit of crape
round the neck of his blackest bottle, and drank there-
from a nobbier to her memory.
“J.A.C.”: I remember the recently deceased Antoi-
nette Sterling about the time she made her London
debut. She was then tall and thin. As a singer, many
good judges thought little of her, for, though she had
a beautiful voice, her vocalisation was mechanical and
cold. Also, she was ever then that extremely aggravating
person, an “aggressively good” woman. It was said
that her religious scruples prevented her appearing in
opera, but a more likely reason was that she was a bad
music-reader and timid —constitutional defects, which
caused her to abandon oratorio, about which she could
have had no religious scruples. When she sang “ The
Lost Chord,” her cheval de bat.aille, in Sydney Town
Ilall, she nearly drove Weigand, who played the organ
obligat', out of his mind. Her fads concerning
W.O.T.U.’s, and that sort of thing, amounted to a vice.
While iri Melbourne she presided at a meeting in the
then Alexandra Theatre with reference to the rescue
of fallen women. The subjects discussed were so strong
that soon the women in the audience began to file out,
followed soon afterwards by the men, who got tired of so
much moral excellence. As one moral detail followed
another, Madame Sterling worked herself into a fine
frenzy, and, burying her head in her hands, tragically
cried out “Oh, my God ! an exclamation no doubt
echoed by many of the departing assembly. But doubt-
less she was sincere enough, and, taken all round, was,
if not a great artist, at leas”- a very good exponent of the
poppycock ballad.
“ 0.K.” : A small thing in theatre panics happened at
Melbourne Royal some years ago. The last act of a
melodrama was being played, and Kate Bishop “held”the
stage, when two men in the gallery came to blows. The
disturbance, with its consequent general rising of the
gallervites, sounded like a rush for the doors; aery of
“ Fire ! ” was raised by a fool in the pit, and immediately
the whole house had the impulse to flee. My recollection
of this exciting moment is that most of us who were
happily situated at the back of the house, in a position
to "et safely off the premises if necessary, showed
“ presence of'mind ”bv yelling “ Sitdown !” to theothers
who had more cause for terror Also, Miss Kate Bishop,
who was clearly “ready to sink through the floor,”
stood her ground at the front of the stage, and
put out her hands beseechingly to the audience.
The panic subsided very quickly, the rush of feet ceased,
people in the front stalls were observed to be taking it
coolly ; somebody raised a cheer, everybody cheered, and
the few people who had actually scooted into the corri-
dors came slowly hack to their places. Some looked
ashamed of their'panic. In more than one case (I am
speaking of the dress-circle) men had left their women-
kind to shift for themselves, and it must be an awful
humiliation to return to one’s forsaken “ missis” under
such circumstances. Some showed a certain moral
courage in confessing that their legs had mastered their
reason. I have in my mind’s eye the picture of a cheery,
sprv voung man who rejoined his cooler-headed friend,
saving—“l had to go, old man ; for the life of me, I
couldn’t have stopped myself.” The candid panicker
was one who might have been backed to put up ft very
good fight under Queensberry rules, but the instinct of
self-preservation dominated his will when he thought Ills
life was in danger.
Total number of Kanakas in Queensland
at present, 8308. In 1903 there arrived in Queens-
land 1037 Polynesians, while 1097 left for the
islands. There are two more vessels—the Clans-
man with 36 recruits, the Lady Norman with 59
to come, and then the importation of sugar-serfs
finally ceases. Already the white workers are
invading plantation work in great numbers.

Pott Fret.
A Canadian paper prints this:—
Two hundred moulders were induced to come from
England to Canada to accept better positions than they
had in England. After arrival and incarceration in the
foundry (they were actually prisoners), they learned that
they were taking the places of strikers, and immediately
voted to stop work. However, when an attempt was
made to leave the foundry, constables with drawn clubs
barred the way, and honest workingmen, decoyed from
their homes in a distant land, and housed in cattle stalls
during their voyage across the ocean, were forced to
struggle for their freedom.
■A-nd yet some foolish people denounce as brutal
and barbarous a law, such as Australia and the
United States have, to prohibit contract-labor-
lmmigration, and assume that nobody benefits by
it but the local workman. As this case proves, the
law is really a valuable protection to the foreign
workman as well. He may come, but he may not
be trapped into coming.
There was another “shunted” politician
but the event did not come off. It was in the
years gone by, and Henry Parkes was the shunter.
A newspaper proprietor represented a N.S.W.
country seat. The Government Printership was
about to become vacant, and the newsman retired
from Parliament, having been practically promised
the appointment. Then Parkes pointed to the
rule which said that a man must have been out of
politics for six months before receiving a Govern-
ment billet. That politician-very old now —is
still to be seen on Sydney streets.
“ H.P.” writes from Wellington (M.L.) :
There is only one copy of any paper at that tiny little
icebound calabash known as the Matte Brun hut, away
up among the snow-clouds of the Southern Alps of
Maoriland—a Bulletin of April 12,1902. “The Hermi-
tage ” is some 2500 ft. up the rugged faqe of Mt. Oook.
Fourteen miles away to the bleak south is the stupendous
ice-flood known as the Tasman Glacier, and another ten
miles across this frozen ice-plain, 5700 ft. above the level
of the sea, is the hut known as Matte Brun. In making
the slide across the glacier every half-ounce of weight
tells. Up till last year literature had no part in anyone’s
haversack. Then a devotee of the disreputable paper,
miserable without it, packed up a copy surreptitiously
with his pemmican, and even thus handicapped reached
the tiny haven without mishap. Since that date the old
back-number has been read and re-read scores of times,
and the guides have made that Bulletin tapu.

NO SOM
MUSICIANS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A sucking Satyr, on a throne
Of flowery-netted silver stone
Espied, one day,
A dainty Dryad, thro’ the trees,
With shining arms and naked knees
In wanton play.
A woodland fountain bubbled near
For joy to hold, reflected clear,
The morrow’s moon,
Too early in the beaming sky
To rule the starry empyry
At Night’s high noon.
Forthwith the Satyr seized his flute
From where it lay, asleep and mute,
Amid the glow
Of dewy leaves; and to his lip
He, smiling, pressed its honeyed tip
And ’gan to blow.
Down every trembling aisle and lane
Like mating birds, the magic strain
Fled hurriedly;
Or whispered in the shy young ear
Of daisies, so they blushed for fear
And modesty.
And never Earth felt sweeter shame
Than when the Satyr’s lyric flame
Fell on her breast,
Most like to that imperial shower
Jove rained on Danae in her tower
With jealous zest.
Now, thro’ the fountain’s timid song
It swept its tidal wave along,
In ravishment,
To woo the Dryad and to raise
Within her heart a sudden blaze
Omnipotent.
She paused. Then listened, all her shape
Bent forward in some half escape
Of daring dance;
Thus was she won, and thus subdued,
A captive caught; no more pursued,
Yet held in trance.
She came (so languid from the stream
It seemed she wandered in a dream)
Between the vines,
Her elfin ringlets, set with drops
Of golden pollen from the tops
Of sacred pines.
Through every trembling aisle and lane
She followed close the magic strain,
And lo! anon,
She spied the flowery-netted stone
Whereof the Satyr made his throne,
To play upon.
She saw the moist-eyed Satyr, too,
Perched leeringly against the blue,
Unclouded sky,
And thought Endymion to pale
In beauty, and the nightingale
Sang jealously.
And so he drew her, thread by thread,
Until he even felt her head
Hide in his breast,
Where now there seemed that all the store
Of myrrh and cassia, musk, and more,
Were sweetly pressed.
His greedy lips forsook the flute,
To hold the kingdom absolute
Of her dear mouth,
When suddenly she sped away,
Like blossoms early blown in May,
Athwart the South.
In vain he spread his empty palms ;
He could not gather back her charms
To his embrace,
While thro’ the trees the cry up-flew :
“I loved your music ; never you —
Old Evil-Face ! ”
And so such Satyrs of to-day
As fancy, when they lightly play
An instrument,
That maidens sigh for them a lot,
Should bear in mind this god, and what
He underwent.
N.S.W.
HUGH R. M'CRAE.
The Maoriland person’s tendency to ad-
vertise that “I, John Smith, am not John Smith,
but another person of the same name,” breaks out
very strongly at times. Recently, a lady adver-
tised in a country paper that two persons, whom
she named, had stated, but not in the presence of
witnesses, that she had had an unlawful operation
Eerforined on her, whereas nothing of the kind
ad ever happened. This seemed to be giving a
reasonable amount of publicity to a rumor which
would be much better treated with well-directed
road-metal, or by pouring a large jug of ink over
the traducer from a balcony.

Strong illustrations of the folly of his
kreetrade policy are now being pushed under
uil s notice daily. For instance, a London mer-
cnant sends along to the press a business letter he
has received from America :
Gentlemen—lf you are shippers to the United States
you are hereby advised that American wooden packing
cases can now be promptly supplied you by Messrs.
* **. ~ street, London. American packing
cases are admitted here free of U.S. Customs duties, but
foreign packing cases are dutiable at same rate as the
goods they contain, therefore by using American cases a
saving of 40 per cent, to 60 per cent, is effected.
“ Anglo-Amerioan,” in the North Ameri-
can Review :
What is it at bottom that makes the English atmo-
sphere so difficult for an American to breathe? It is, I
ben eve, that he feels himself in a country where the
dignity of life is lower than in his own ; a country where
a man born in ordinary circumstances expects, and is
expected, to die in ordinary circumstances; where the
scope of his efforts is traced beforehand by the accident
of position ; where he is handicapped in all cases and
crushed in most by the superincumbent weight of caste,
convention, “ good form,” and the deadening artificiali-
®*. an society. That unconquerable buoyancy
which infects the American air like a sting and a chal-
jenge, and braces every American with the inspiration
that he has a chance in life—that here are open oppor-
tunities, unreserved possibilities, no battering at locked
doors, no floundering in blank alleys ; that here, in short,
iu ..u man himself "’bo makes his career—is something
that the English have so utterly lost as to be incapable of
realising it.

The present Hobart Treasury in Murray-
street was, in the old days of “the System,” the
Police Court, and was opposite to the gaol and
the gibbet. The old cells have never been
touched, and oan be seen to-day, mouldering away
beneath the Government money-ohangers.
Re Chicago theatre fire. Against the
advice of Dr. Gresswell, ohairman of the Board
of Health, the Irvine Government, a year ago,
dismissed three of the board’s inspectors. These
men were engaged to see that the necessary pre-
cautions in case of fire were taken at theatres and
public halls.
“ D.” : Some parsons know the ear-biting
business down to the ground. Happened to
stroll into a bethel the other day, and when
leaving the churchwarden handed me a little
paper envelope about two inches square marked
“Church Debt. 2/6. Special Collection. To be
placed in the plate at any of the services on the
first Sunday in the month.” I found it empty
and went back and asked where the 2s. 6d. was.
Then he told me that 1 was to place the coin in
it ; the parson supplied the bags and the 2/6 was
printed on them lest someone might put a
sovereign in by mistake. Tf you wanted to give a
sovereign you oeuld get eight bags. He was so
good in explaining mattors that I took eight bags

“ Saltee ” from S.A. :
The salt lagoons which abound on the southern end of
Yorke’s Peninsula are put to some curious uses occasion*
ally. A fair-sized one at the back of a certain town-
ship is used as a rubbish tip by the community generally.
Tins, bottles and other trifles all find a resting place here,
as well as carcases of eats, dogs, etc., with a stray horse
or two. Some years ago a township resident had a large
shark brought up from the nearest seaport for the sake of
getting its teeth, which are considered decorative when
used for breast-pins, etc. When the shark arrived he
found someone else had removed the coveted molars and
the question of disposing of the useless carcase had to be
faced. Ultimately the common dumping-ground afore-
mentioned was resorted to, and the sixteen-footer was
carted down and deposited to work off its superfluous
odor in the lagoon. The salt which comes from there-
abouts is said to be of very superior quality. By-the-
way, the local cemetery is also situated on the bank of
the lagoon. For general usefulness that lagoon may be
said to hold a record.
Related by Fitzgerald Molloy, in his recent
book on “The' Sailor King,” that William IV.
was anxious to be styled Henry IX., but was
dissuaded by the church dignitaries, who recalled
(and apparently believed in) an old prophecy
that, “as Henry VIII. pulled down monks and
cells, so Henry IX. would put down bishops and
bells.” With Edward VII. now on the throne,
and George V. and Edward VIII. to follow in
the natural order of things, the church seems
likely to have a good long respite.
T. P.’s Weekly tells a good story of
Professor Banks, president of the English
Wesleyan conference. Preaching one Sunday,
lie saw a young man apparently much moved.
Thinking to give the youth time to compose
himself, he asked his hearers to join in singing
the hymn, “Sometimes a light surprises the
Christian while he sings.” There was a general
grin, and he then found that the young fellow
had set'his coat-tails on fire by sitting on his
lighted pipe.

“Thurla”: Like “Ku-Klux” (B.
24/12/’O3) let me also air my biblical knowledge
to correct The Bulletin’s statement—“ It is
pleasing to note that there is not a single lady
mentioned in the Bible.” The term “lady” occurs
twice in chap. 47, Isaiah :
Sit thou silent, and get thee into darkness, O daughter
of the Chaldeans ; for thou shalt no more be called the
lady of kingdoms.
And thou saidst I shall be a lady for ever; so that thou
didst not lay these things to thy heart, neither didst
remember the latter end of it.
Hiphen One damp evening last week,
a shabby individual, very wet inside (and out),
carrying the inevitable supper cray-fish of the
very drunk, stepped into a tram, seated himself
next a pompous military gent, covered with gold
lace and hauteur, and, after wishing him the
compliments of the season, entered into a lively
discussion as to the prospects of war. He of the
brass buttons and tin spurs stood it for a while •
then remarked freezingly, “I—ah—don’t know
you, sir! You—ah—have the advantage of me.”
The drunk gasped for a moment, then looked him
up and down in beery criticism and turning to me
remarked, “Don’t see where th’ advantagesh
comesh in, d’you ? ” The military gent sprang to
his feet and, drawing his sword, plunged it to the
hilt in the drunk’s body, which squelched grace-
fully onto the seat —at least that’s what he would
” av ® done had it been in Germany. As it was in
the St. Kilda Road he only stopped the tram and
stepped off breathing cuss words.

Brushook's Great Scheme.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Wur yer ever on Cooyar Crick ? ” asked Scully,
taking the pipe from his mouth, and chuckling
softly to himself. “It was a pretty lonesome
place when I knew it,” he continued. “That’s
sev ral year ago now. I was dodderin’ along
down the crick with Matilda, lookin’ for a job, as
per usual. An’ hour or so ’fore sundown I spotted
a ramshackle sort of a humpy off the road a bit.
It didn’t have a very invitin’ look to a trav’ler,
but the next place was a good bit on. I put the
swag down an’ stood, consid’rin’. Presently a
queer-lookin’ old feller comes shuffling over from
th humpy. He was an unord’nary squat junk of
a chap, all knobs an’ corners, with a great straggly
beard stickin’ out all round. His face was sallow
an wrinkled, an’ its expression was peculyer ;
he looked as if he’d lost something, an’ had a sus-
picion that I was that something transmogrified.
He didn t speak when I said ‘ Good day ’ to him,
but his eyes kep’ peerin’ at me so rummy-like as
made me feel uncomfortable. He walked round
in ® thought he was goin’ ter hit me at fust—an’
when he come to the front of me ag’in, he stood,
an cast his lamps up an’ down me in a pre-
occupied sort o’ way as though he wur lookin’for
a splittin’ tree, an’ had mistook me for something
of the sort.
“ ‘ How old are yer ? ’ he ses all at once.
'!' —about 40,’ ses I, with a bit of a start.
“ ‘Are yer married?’ he asks next, quite as a
matter o’ fact.
“ ‘No,’ I ses, starin’.
“ * Knocked about the bush much ?’
“ ‘Years!’
“ ‘ D'yer go seein’ gurls, nights or Sundays?’
“ ‘ Dunno any.’
“ ‘ Know anything about kangarooin’ ?’
“ ‘ Don,) a bit o’ shootin’,’ 1. ses, gettin’ more
an’ more mysterfied ev’ry seiond.
Anything of a butcher ? ’ ses he.
“ ‘ Quite a dab,’ ses I.
“He took another look round me. ’Looks just
the thing,’ he muttered. ‘How’s yer joints?
Chuck yer limbs about—let’s see how they work.
Some coves’ joints wants ’ilin’.’
By gum,’ I thinks to meself, ‘ this bloke’s as
cracked as a dry plain. Better humor him.’ I
shook me legs an’ whirled me arms around. He
didn’t seem satisfied.
‘‘ ‘ Gor an y corns or bunions ? Ever have back-
ache ? ’
“ ‘No.’
“ 4 How’s yer teeth ? ’
Oh—purty fair.’
“He took a pin from his shirt-front an’ threw it
on the road. ‘Pick that up,’ he ses, short an’
sharp, as yer might to a dog. I picked it up.
Not too bad,’ ses he; ‘seems just the thing.’
I hen he drew the heel of his boot across the track.
Le s see yer jump,’ he ses, an’ stood aside with
Ins hands on his hips.
“‘But wot yer drivin’ at?’ I asks him.
I nought lie might be one o’ them fool jokers
takin a rise out o’ me . He didn’ seem anyways
unord nary ter look at, now I come ter take stock
0 <l U . m ’ ’ ce P t ’ l }’ his manner was peculyer.
It’s orlright,’ he ses. ‘Yer’ll see it all
d rectly.. It’s a great scheme. But lemme see if
yer can jump.’
‘ I toed the scratch an’ took a big leap ter get
a '??^ rom hini. I was gettin’ uneasy ag’in.
1 Good !’ he ses. ‘That was real good. How’s
yer lungs ? Have yer got good wind ? ’
“ ‘ Oh—fust-class !'
“‘Gome up here a bit,’he ses, ‘an’ le’s see if
yer can run.’
‘‘But wot’s the game?’ I protests ag’in. I
was gettin’ about full of it.
“‘Just up ter this ironbark,’ he answers.
Come on. He waited for me, so I thought I’d
better go.
we got to the ironbark he sung out.
Off ! j an’ started back full lick. I lost about 10
yards start; but he couldn t sprint for sour
apples, an’ I caught him half-way. He pulled up
on a sudden an’ yelled after me, ‘ Can ver
so dder ? ’ J
“ ‘So’dder what?’ sez I, puffin’ like a broken-
winded cart orse.
4< lT‘ n cans .’ he ses ; * I want a good so’dderer ’
I looked at him, wonderin’ what all this tom-
foolery had ter do with solderin’. But I thought
I might as well see the tiling through, an’ told
h’mi a served an apprenticeship to the trade.
iTn , ust th® thing,’ he ses, ‘just the thing*
What s yer name ? ’
“ ‘ J an l? s Scully,” ses I; ‘ mos’ly called Jim.’
Scully ? he ses, musin’. ‘ Think I’ve heard
that name before. Wur you ever in gaol, Mr.
Scully—if it’s a fair question?’
No,’ I ses. ‘lf you met a Scully in gaol, he
was no relation o’ mine.’
He faced towards me, but looked down at me
boots while he lifted his finger an’ give me his
pedigree. ‘Le’me tell you, Mr. Scully, that my
name s Samyel Brushook, an’ it’s sufficient tester-
mony to my noble ancestry ter say that no
Brushook has ever been in gaol. That isn’t the
place ter make the acquaintance of a Brushook
allow me. It was orig’nally a hyphenated name’
1 might tell you. Me grandmother’s name was
Brush, an me gran’father’s Hooke. They put
em tergether an’ made Brushook ; an’ Samyel
Brushook is the larst of a long line ’
“‘Just so,’ I breaks in. ‘l’m glad ter meet
yer.
‘‘‘Yes.’ he ses. ‘Yer oughter think yerself
highly honored. Yer honest, I’spose’’
‘“Well—l hope so.’
‘‘‘S°. d ° L ’Tanyrate, yer look just the thing,
an I think yer’ll do. Gome on over an’ ’ave some
tea.
“I was with him there, an’ we went over to the
humpy, an he put th’ billy on. I reckoned on
gettin a good feed into me, an’ slippin’ away as
soon as it got dark. In the meantime I tapped
him for an explanation.
“‘ I w ,ant a Rood partner,’ he ses ; 1 a man after
me own heart—ter join me in one o’ the best specs
yer could root out m a month o’ Sundays I’ve
been doin’ purty middlin’ with scalps and skins
since I took up this s’lection ; but slaughterin’ for
scalps an’ skins means a woeful waste o’ good
meat—a loss o’ millions o’ pounds, mate. It’s a
big thing; an it’s a surprisin’ wonder no one’s
ever hit on it before. Shows a deplorable lack o’
brains. The natch’rel resources o’ this countrv
' S ( J™P y marvlous, an’ all neglected-sadly
neglected Now, I’ve been tryin’ for months ter
get a good man ter go into the bizness with me
but could never get one ter suit. Most of ’em
would be orlright for a day or two; then something
was bound ter go wrong with th’ works. It’d
be backache or toothache, or stringhalt or screw-
matics Some ’ud have crank feet or broken wind
an couldn t run ; an' a man as can’t run in this
industry am t worth his salt. So of late I’ve been
puttm the apphcants through an examination
an you re the fust one o’ 50 that’s passed muster’
Yer am t pertiekler bout pay, I serpose ? ’
Well,’ I ses, ‘ I should like ter know wot I
was gettin’, an’ I’d also like ter be sure I was
goin’ ter get it.'
“ ‘ I’m sorry ter hear that,’he sez, an’, by gum,
he looked it. ‘ It’s the fust p’int you’ve failed in,
an’ I was beginnin’ ter hope yer wur purfect.’
“ ‘A man can’t work for nix,’ I protested.
“‘The money would come in lumps—big
lumps,’ ses he, ‘but it might be a long time in
showin’.’
“ ‘ That’s a different thing, ’ I ses, ‘ providin’ it’s
morally certain it will come. But give us the
prospectus of the concern.’
“ ‘lt’s this way,’ he ses. ‘As I told yer, I’ve
been scalpin’ an’ skinnin’ a good while "ere, an’ it
goes against me grain ter see all the good mar-
supial meat left rottin’ in the bush. I propose ter
have it all brought in, boiled down an’ canned—
preserved, yer understand—an’ shipped ter W.A.
They tell me the miners there ’ave been livin’ on
tinned dog for years. I hold as dog ain’t a
proper diet for a miner, no matter wot breed o’
dog he might be; and I reckon the Sandgropers
would jump, at good, sound marsupial meat. We’d
label the tins “Sheep’s Tongue,” “Chicken an’
Ham,” “ Veal,” “Beef Paste,”an’ such like. It’d
bust up the dog industry in one act.’
“ * But wot about the plant? ’ I asks him.
“ ‘l’ve collected some pots an’ boilers an*
karersene tins,’ he ses. ‘That’ll do ter begin
with. As bizness expands, of course, we’ll improve
an’ enlarge the works, and I look forward with
confidence to the day when we’ll have a great big
factory in full swing, with a hund’ed hands
in constant work. Of course, we’ll have ter be
satisfied with small consignments for a while.
I’ve collected all the jam-tins an’ sardine-tins, an’
other sorts on Cooyar, an’ when we’ve got ’em
filled an’so’ddered up, they’ll bring enough ter give
us a supply of regulation tins an’ labels. We’ll
’ave ter see about a trade-mark, too, as there’s
bound ter be heaps o’ imitators. There always is.
But we can settle little details like that after-
wards. The main thing is ter get the preservin’
works goin’.*
Have yer made any arrangements with
regard to the supply o’ stock ? ’ I asks him.
“ ‘ That’s simplicerty itself,’ he ses. ‘ The run is
swarmin’ with marsupials, an’ there’s any gors-
quantity o’ blacks ter run ’em in. They’ll do the
yardin’, killin’ an’ skinnin’. Th’ expense ’ll be
nothin’. A little opium an’ terbacca will satisfy
’em, an’ the waste meat ’ll do ter feed ’em on.
Our work ’ll be mainly in the shed—boilin’ down
an’cannin’. We’ll have the gins ter help us, too
—stokin’ an’ skimmin’, f’rinstance. Then there’s
the hides an’ scalps an’ bones. They’ll attend ter
them departments, too.’
“ ‘ You have teams, I s’pose, for takin’ the
stuff ter port ?”
“‘ I ’ave a horse, but he’s crippled. ’Tanyrate
the niggers can hump the stuff down to the
wharf.’
“ ‘ How far is the wharf from here? ’
“ ‘ ’Bout 125 mile. ’Fore we ship the fust lot
we’ll arrange for an agent to act for us in the
West. I know a party over there—if he ain’t dead
—who would be just the thing.’
“‘An’ do yer think it would pay from the
jump?’ I asks him.
“ For a moment speech utterly failed Brushook,
an’ his eyes blinked at me in a way that was pain-
ful to see.
“ ‘ Pay f ’ he gasped at last—* why, man, there’s
thousands in it— millions / If success wasn’t
assured, I’d float the scheme into a company an’
work it that way ; but who wants ter have a lot o’
deadheads pocketin’ divs. they’ve no right ter’?
It’s a simple, inoney-makin’, legitimate spec, that
you an’ me’s goin’ ter work on our own. We’ll be
independent, and kowtow to no gold-glorified
nobody ’
“Brushookstopped sudden, with his lips parted,
an’ a, blank look came into his face as a tall, toffy-
lookin’ man walked into the hut. He didn’t seem
very pleased at seein’ me there.
“ ‘ Take the saddle off my horse, Brushook,’ he
ses. He went through the hut, lookin’ every-
where ; then went out an’ met Brushook. ‘ Who’s
that feller in there?’he asked. ‘Oh, a trav’ler,’
ses Brushook. ‘Well, get rid of him. I’ve told
you often enough not to encourage travellers
here.’
“He came in again, an’ I hurried out ter
Brushook. ‘ Who’s that fellow in there ? ’ ses I.
‘ Oh, that’s the boss,’ sez Brushook, quietly. He
didn’t seem inclined to discuss the matter any
further, but pushed past. All the fire of enthu-
siasm had gone out of his eyes, and they looked
about as live as the eyes of a dead eel.
“I took a hitch in me swag, which I’d left at
the door, an’ while I was at it the boss come out
ag’in. ‘ Excuse me,’ I ses, ‘but I can’t quite get
the bearin’s o’ this establishment. Brushook told
me this was his selection, an’ led me to b’lieve he
wanted a man.’
“The boss laughed. ‘lt was his, one time;
but drought an’ wallabies put the dead finish on
h!m. ( He’s bound’ry-ridin’ for the station now.’
I see ; that accounts for the marsupials
hoppin’ on his brain. Well, good-night,’ I sez
an’ I went down an’ camped on the crick.”
EDWARD S. SORNNSON.
S.M. Herald in a recent Saturday’s issue
pointed with pride to a golden wedding announce-
ment, that of Mr. William Henry Rose, who was
married on December 27, 1853, at Wiseman’s
Ferry, Hawkesbury River, to Miss Jane Green-
tree. He is a grandson of the late Mr. Thomas
Rose, of Dorsetshire, England, who, according to
the Herald, “came out as one of the staff of
Governor Phillip in 1788.” As a matter of fact,
Mr. Thomas Rose arrived in Sydney, with his
wife and four children and a relative —Elizabeth
Fish—on board the Bel lon a, as free assisted
immigrants, on January 16, 1793, just 100 years
ago. As Phillip had left Australia on Dec. 11,
1792, it is hard to discover how Mr. Thomas Rose
oame to be on his staff. Australian history is not
one of the strong points of S.M. Herald, or of
any Australian daily for that matter.

A Cycle Song.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Let us go out, for the Spring’s in the morning,
You and I only,
Far from the city, its noise and strife scorning,
Where roads are lonely,
Into the land of the river and sea shore,
Ever enchanted,
Where memories sweet of the days that are no
more,
Deep are implanted.
Grey is the dawning,
Cold is the west wind,
In the lone highways
Brown is the bracken,
Gold the gorse gleaming,
In shadowy by-ways.
Red-brown and long are the roads that we follow
By the tall hedges,
Gleaming and grey lies the lake in the hollow
Framed by the sedges,
A shadowy land where white mists are curling,
When, o’er the ranges,
The sun rises swift, his bright banners unfurling,
And the world changes.
Red is the sunrise,
Cool is the west-wind
In the long highway.
Sunshine and shadow
Link now together
Your way with my way.
Where is the charm in this land of the far West?
Fair land of gladness !
Holding in memory so much that we love best,
Nothing of sadness.
Is it because we first knew it in brightest,
Sunniest weather,
And rode through its long roads with hearts that
were lightest,
We two together ?
In the bright sunshine,
By hill and hollow
Merrily winding,
In all the moorland,
Meadows and mountains,
Some pleasure finding.
Here through the trees we see the sea gleaming,
Washing the beaches,
Rippling and sparkling, ’neath sunlight streaming,
Until it reaches
Far to the ocean, blue and unresting,
Slumbering never, •
With glistening sea-foam its rolling waves
cresting,
And tossing ever.
On the long hillside
Dark are the pine-trees,
Deep are their shadows,
Steadily lengthening,
As falls the daylight
Low on the meadows.
When in the west the sun slowly is sinking,
Onward we’re wheeling,
Travelling slowly, each of us thinking,
Each of us feeling
That the wide world holds a harvest of pleasure,
Ours for the reaping,
To garner in memory’s storehouse, and treasure
Through long years’ keeping.
Redly the west glows
In the pale twilight,
And lights are burning
When to the city,
From the long, lone roads,
We are returning.
M.li.
LILLA McKAY.
Tree'd!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Hb was stone-broke. Even his billies and blan-
kets were gone. He asked me to put in a word
for him with the Government Resident regarding
a free passage back to Oooktown.
Like many others, he had landed at Port
Moresby with little more than his swag, and, pro-
foundly ignorant of Papua’s interior, had at-
tempted to cross the island to the goldfields on
the north coast by the Yanapa river track. And,
like others, he returned—only sooner.
‘ ‘ So you are up a tree ? ” I asked.
He stared at me with blanching face and shud-
dered violently.
“Touch of malaria, eh?”
“No-o,”he answered, slowly. “It isn’t that.
You just reminded me of my experience up
there.” He pointed in the direction of the dark
ranges.
I scented “copy.” By the promise to secure
him a passage with next boat, I succeeded in
loosening his tongue.
• * • »
“I was warned not to try my luck single-
handed. But I have carried bluey from Cue to
Coen, and only laughed at their tales of hardship.
I took passage to the mouth of the Vanapa, and
when they landed me I started away by myself,
for there was no mistaking the track through the
scrub The second day out I got to a place called
Kone. It’s the site of a forsaken native village,
and lies right on the bank of the Vanapa. The
river ran past—a broad, deep torrent. A patch of
about five or six acres had been cleared of timber
by the former inhabitants, and all around it the
scrub grew as a dense wall, looking for all the world
like an expectant crowd standing on tiptoe wait-
ing for a procession to pass by.
“But what made me open my eyes were three
enormous trees that had been left standing in the
clear space, their naked, branchless trunks rearing
up to 100 ft. or more, and then opening out into
enormous crowns. Right up into the forks were
built three little houses, roofs and all complete.
I suppose the niggers used them as watch-towers
or fortresses. There was no way of gaining access
to these, but by a kind of rope-ladder, made of
lawyer-vines, and dangling loosely down to the
ground.
"It was late in the afternoon, and beginning to
drizzle. I truly believe it never stops raining in
those hills. I determined to camp for the night.
I didn’t care for any of the empty huts that stood
about on their shaky piles, and so made a big fire
near the foot of one of the house trees, the biggest
of the lot—a giant with a slight lean towards the
river. Soon I had got a decent blaze going, and
stacked a large pile of firewood around the trunk
for the night. Then I had some supper and
settled down for a quiet smoke.
“Nothing can make you feel quite so miserable
as to camp alone in the scrub. And the strange-
ness of my surroundings made me feel doubly
dismal. All the stories I had heard about the
Papuan cannibals and head-hunters came vividly
to my mind. Suddenly something hit me a smart
whack on the shoulder blade. I dropped my pipe
and turned round in affright. Then I laughed.
It was only the swinging end of that Jacob’s
ladder leading up to the top story above.
“I don’t know what put the thought into my
head. I must have been mad. Or perhaps I
needed some excitement to rouse me from the un-
pleasant broodings into which I had fallen. Any-
how, I found myself testing curiously the strength
of the supple vines, and taking a preliminary
swing on the bottom step. Then I climbed
another rung, and another. It was easy, appa-
rently safe, and distinctly exhilarating. Scon I
swung giddily midways between the ground and
the landing on top, and was still climbing.
“ Crunch—crack ! Something was giving ! A
spasm of hair-raising terror shot down my spine
and seemed to settle into my stomach. A
moment’s reflection told me that my only salva-
tion was upwards. I was within about a dozen
feet of the platform. I crawled up the shaking,
swinging ladder. I could now plainly see where
the ominous sounds originated. The rotten lash-
ings that fastened the vines to the lowermost limb
were giving way. I jumped for the ledge above
me, grasped it with both hands, and hung dangling
in space. Then the vines gave way, and fell with
a swish to the bottom.
“Half-crazy with fear I drew myself up to
the level of the platform, and fell down on the
springy bamboo floor.
Then I carefully sat up and looked around me.
The treehouse consisted of nothing but the slight
plat of bamboo strips and a roof made out of
betel palm leaves. It was quite empty. I
crawled cautiously to the brink, and looked over.
The depth appeared frightful. And the lean of
the tree gave an indescribably horrible sensation
of falling. I crouched back and considered.
“I was caught in a trap, and would most
probably perish. Even if some travelling miners
came along, what could they do? It was impos-
sible for a white man to get up or down the
smooth trunk on account of its girth. The very
idea of the attempt produced an attack of vertigo
that made me dig my fingers frantically into the
bamboo latticing. There might be another ladder,
or even a single vine, I thought. I investigated.
But no —nothing !
“ Clutching a roof support I raised myself and
looked out from my eyrie. A splendid panorama
lay spread out below. The river rushed angrily
away under me and wound into the distant sea ;
while, to the north, tier upon tier of mountains
towered to the horizon. But I wasn’t taking any
interest in landscape just then. I felt an almost
irresistible inclination to laugh. But I repressed
it. It seemed unpleasantly akin to madness.
“ Through the cracks in the floor I could see my
camp and the big fire at the foot of the tree. A
high wind from the coast began to rise, and my
castle creaked mournfully. The breeze caught
the flame below and carried it into the spare stack
ot wood. It flared up, notwithstanding the misty
rain, and sent forth dense volumes of black
smoke. I could smell the sweet sandal fumes
scenting the air.
“ The wind grew stronger. The crown of my
tree started tc sway to and fro, and I sank down
in a heap, clinging shudderingly to my support.
Every time the head of the tree bent down before
the gusts, a shriek formulated in my parched
throat. I could have sworn the groaning trunk
would snap. But elastically it flew back again,
and dipped into space in the opposite direction.
My stomach turned, and my hair stood on end.
A choking sensation of dropping, like an ever-
lasting series of epileptic fits, tore my inside out.
I tried to pray. But the gasps and gulps of
terror would not let me. My hands bled, so
frenziedly did I cling to the frame work. The
whole landscape began to turn and turn around.
Sometimes I felt like letting go and ending the
torture. But I could not.
“How long I swung about like that I cannot
say. But all at once I realised that the fire below
was assuming greater proportions. I could feel
the heat intensifying, and heavy masses of
smoke blew up into my face. I put my eye to the
floor and stared down.
“If by any possibility my fright could have
become acuter, it certainly did then. I tried to
wet my cracked lips with my tongue. But my
mouth was dry. I shut my eyes for a minute, and
then looked again. Yes —the trunk of the tree
was on fire !
“I think I must have fainted. At any rate, I
became delirious. I dreamt I was flying through
space for years and years, falling and falling.
And somehow my heart and my stomach could
not keep pace with my body. Far beneath was a
light that gradually grew larger, till at last I had
to turn away purblinded. The heat became
insufferable, and the smoke suffocated me. I
knew I was dead, and yonder lay Hell!
“Crack ! Like a rifle-shot the report rang out
and brought me again to my senses, or, at ieast,
to such fragments as I still had about me. An-
other report, and an awesome grinding noise of
tearing fibres. The flames had enveloped the
whole lower part of the trunk in a fiery circle.
The end had come. I sprang to my feet in a
frenzy, regardless of the danger of toppling over.
At the same time a fiercer blast of wind caught
the trembling crown, bore it down towards the
river, and tree and myself went down into empti-
ness.
“A sharp, shooting pain in my head, a pres-
sure as of an iron band on my temples aroused
me. I instinctively stretched out my hands and
caught at something hard. Then a breath of air
seemed to flow right through mv veins like a re-
vivifying fluid, and I opened my eyes.
“I was floating down the river on a log of drift-
wood. And it was night.”
* * * *
I procured a free passage for this tree-dweller.
And some time after I visited the deserted village
of Kone myself. It was exactly as my friend had
described.

Nothing annoyed Dickens more than the unauthorised
dramas that were built on his novels. His c< Nicholas
Nickleby” was dramatised even before he had
finished it in serial form. When Dickens lectured
through America he saw hie name writ large over
all the big theatres announcing unauthorised version*
of his novels, but he was spared a Yankee version
which was foaled in London many years afterward*.
One of Tay Pay’s scribes writes thusly on the horror:
“ I shall never forget the indignation caused in London,
some years ago, by the production at the Opera Comique
of an American adaptation of " The Old Curiosity Shop,’ 1
wherein the most curious, but not the most treasured,
article- qf all was a cheeky Little Nell, in silks and frills
and high-heeled shoes, who cheered herself in her afflic-
tion with a banjo, and performed high-kink dances. It
was a very shocking business altogether, the faces of the
critics were set with the expression of insufferable gloom,
to which there came no relieving light when Quilp fell
into the Thames, and Little Nell, who arrived in time to
witness his final struggles for life, picked his hat up from
the wharf and tossed it to him with the feeling remark,
‘ Hi! you’ve left some of your luggage.’”
Lindsay Campbell, a young ex-Melbournian who
understands his business, writes a gossipy letter from
New York concerning himself and other people: "Things
dramatic are in a strange way- almost every ‘ star ’ has
fallen, the cause being in most cages a bad piece. Since
commencement of last season 127 companies have had a
‘ knock ’. . . . The musical comedy failures are appalling
—little wonder when fellows write here that hardly know
C. from B. . . . Patti, beset with litigation, is having a
bad time. Grau, her manager that was, withdrew from
the contract, giving for his reason ‘ complications of a
legal nature.’ Patti is a frost everywhere. Last week
she * held up ’ a concert. Was to receive fiCOOdol., and
there was only SOOOdol. in the house. She refused to
sing until the proprietors of the house came to the rescue
with balance of cash. I heard her in N.Y. and was
bitterly disappointed. I have quitted conducting
for a time, having been selected to "compose
the music for a comic opera. The ‘ book' has been
accepted by a prominent manager, and I am to complete
the score by February. . . . Fay Templeton will, I hope,
sing my ‘ Hush-a-hye ’ in ‘ Runaways ’. She is pleased
with it, and calls it ‘ a classic in coon songs.’ ” Lindsay
Campbell’s success in America should encourage many a
struggling young Australian musician to try his luck in
the same direction. A‘man with a real practical know-
ledge of music can tnake a decent living in the States at
present, and if he has also a talent, or at least a turn for
composition, he can live like the proverbial fighting cock.
